THE RED PAGE.
FOB EVER.
Out of the body for ever,
Wearily sobbing, “ Oh, whither ?”
A Soul that hath wasted its changes
Floats on the limitless Ether !
Lost in dim, horrible blankness ;
Drifting like Wind on a Sea,
Untraversed and vacant and moaning,
Nor s allow nor shore on the lee !
Helpless, unfriended, forsaken ;
Haunted and tracked by the Past,
With fragments of pitiless voices,
And desolate faces, aghast!
One saith —“ It is well that, he goeth
Naked and fainting with cold,
Who worshipped his sweet-smelling garmentt,
Arrayed with the,cunning of old !
“ Hark ! how he crieth, my brothers,
With pain for the glittering things ,
He saw on the shoulders of Rulers,
And the might in the mouths of the King l l
“ This Soul hath been one of the idlers
Who wait with still hands, when they lack,
For Fortune, like Joseph, to throw them
The cup thrust in Benjamin's sack.
" Now, had he been faithful in striving,
And warring with Wrong to the sword,
He must have passed over these spaces
Caught up in the arms of the Lord."
A second : “ Lo, Passion was wilful ;
And, glad with voluptuous sighs,
He held it luxurious trouble
To ache for luxurious eyes !
“ She bound him, the woman resplendent;
She withered his strength with her stare ;
And Faith hath been twisted and strangled
With folds of her luminous hair !
“ Was it well, O you wandering wailer,
Abandoned in terrible space.
To halt on the highway to Heaven
Because of a glittering face ? ”
And another: “ Behold, he was careful:
He faltered to think of his Youth,
Dejected and weary and footsore,
Alone on the dim road to Truth.
“ If the way had been shorter and greener
And brighter, he might have been brave ;
But, the goal was too far, and he fainted
Like Peter with Christ on the wave ! "
Beyond the wild haunts of the mockers—
Far in the distance and gray,
Floateth that sorrowful spirit
Away, and away, and away.
Pale phantoms fly past it, like shadows :
Dim eyes that are blinded with tears ;
Old faces all white with affliction —
The ghosts of the wasted dead years !
“ Soul that hath ruined us, shiver
And moan when you know us,” they cry—
" Behold! I was part of thy substance
“ And I"—saith another —“ and 11"
Drifting from starless abysses
Into the Ether sublime,
Where is no upward nor downward.
Nor region, nor record of Time l
Out of the Body for ever
No refuge—no succour nor stay—
Floated that sorrowful Spirit
Away, and away, and away.
— Henry Kendall, in The Australasian, September 8,
1866.
Of Adam. Lindsay Gordon.
(“With Whom Be Peace!”)
JUNE, 1870, and it is bitter cold ia Mel-
bourne. No leafless boughs as yet to hiss
in the west wind ; no trams passing with a
cheery whirr; Burke and Wills in placid
rigidity on the rise of the eastern hill; and...a tall
man coming round by the Town Hall corner.
Tall, spare, battered, depressed, his very eyes
lacking either speculation or apprehension—a sad,
a moody and embittered man. A poet? Yes;
Gordon, the poet. He has been interviewing his
publishers and has obtained “ a statement.”
Meets him another, short, spare also, slightly
-stooped, a gleam of mingled self-elation and
genuine camaraderie in his eyes. He is now too
well clad for the day. He i 3 Kendall, the other
poet. In his pocket he has proofs of the review he
, has been permitted to make of Gordon’s book for
The Australasian. Beyond all doubt, they were
the two most noteworthy men in Melbourne that
day, the two who had even then made for Aus-
tralia heritages inalienable and eternal. For us
and for all our children and children’s children, a
something which neither we nor they can ever sell
or mortgage or lose, unless self is first sold or
mortgaged or lost.
“Come and have a drink,” said Kendall, and
the two crossed over to the old ramshackle Argus
Hotel, which stood just below the old ramshackle
Argus offioe of those times. They entered in, and
the glass doors swung to behind them, shutting
out this world for one, and that one out from that
world for ever. It was Gordon’s last day.
J*
We can picture the two in the comfortable
shabby snuggery of the place, drinking occa-
sionally, and talking very much in earnest, as the
day waned and the many people came and went.
There is no record of the conversation. Kendall
did not at any time make an effort to record it,
which matters much, for assuredly it was all
literature. Apart from literature and the things
directly appertaining thereto, Gordon and Ken-
dall had not one square inch of common standing-
ground.
Many men looked in through the day who were
intimately known to both, but none intruded.
The proofs may have been between them for a
while, and later such kindling response from eye
to eye as would ward off all save intruding fools.
The day waned as they sat, the bitter, dull June
day ; and when they came into it again the twi-
light had cloaked the streets, and the discordant
rumble, clatter, and jangle of the evening’s home-
ward traffic tilled all the air. The stream of
people on the pavement was congenial to Gordon
as % brawling creek in a hungry country to a
weary, benighted traveller. He had been for a
little while on a puny crest of exultation;
he was down in the corresponding hollow
of depression—sordid cares, inherited despond-
encies, rash resolves often enough hinted
at, overwhelmed him. When he arrived at
Brighton the chill and the sob and the sibilant
invitation from the spumy lips of the sea were all
influences to be escaped from while possible. He
went inside his house and shut the door. He
opened that door once more only, for on the
morrow he succumbed—to what ? Drink ? Rub-
bish. Julian Tenison Woods—great philosopher
and white man—who knew Gordon well in the old
Mount Gambier days, says “he was never a
drinking man, never had any pleasure in the
company of hard or noisy drinkers.” And men
who knew him well in later days, J. J. Shillinglaw
and Dr. Pat Moloney amongst others, have placed
it on record that he oould not drink, did not care
for drink, “got no good of drink” (in the way
good drinkers understand the phrase).
What ailed him, then? Was he broken by dis-
appointment over his failure to establish his
rights to the Esselmont estates? Alexander
Sutherland tells us so on one page, though on
another he had declared that Gordon never
sickened on that occasion. And that previous
statement is true, for Gordon sickened of nothing
but his own hands and head, from the earliest
wild outbursts to the last grim tragedy. It was
not drink, it was disappointment. It was the
clinching of the curse ne inherited, the grip of
the Dead who possessed him, rendered possible
by circumstances over which, in the familiar but
here strictly accurate commonplace, he had no
control.
*
Adam Dunford Gordon married his own cousin,
Harriet Elizabeth Gordon; and from the mar-
riage came Adam Lindsay into the world, with
all the aberrations and consequent risky pros-
pects a physiologist might have foretold. But
worse, his mother, two years before his birth,
disclosed such signs of mental aberration as to
render special treatment necessary; and these
continued and increased till, in acute melancholia,
she died.
Adam Lindsay Gordon was thus conceived,
born and cradled in actual madness ; also within
limits of consanguinity on the part of his parents
which are not entered in that way without peril. Is
it possible to lay too much stress on such facts as
these ? Do they not justify the assertion that the
devil who tormented him his whole life long, and
ultimately slew him, was with him from his birth
and begetting and in him through all his up-
bringing ? Alexander Sutherland, in his inconse-
quential prosings, will not say so. Henry Gyles
Turner, in his offensive and often illiterate
patronage, would flout such an idea. But neither
the one nor the other of them helped ever towards
the understanding of a man whose ways seemed
always to be dictated by whims. Not much more
did they know of the actual characteristics of his
verse.
It was compulsory on Gordon that he should
attempt great things. It was also laid on him
that, in all great attempts, he should be hampered
and hindered; and in the end be called back in
such peremptory fashion as to brook no denial.
His wild riding, his lonely broodings, his sudden
graspings at anything offering resource, occupa-
tion, distraction, were not begotten of any desire
to escape from a reckless or shameful past. There
was a something in, on, and about him always.
The companionship which impelled him to un-
conventional deeds was not that of “ Black Tom
Oliver,” or any of his set. Read De la Motte
Eouque’s Sintram if you want to understand. If
that does not assist you, admit your own limita-
tions, take down Sutherland and Turner, and be
content.
Jt
Gordon’s loves were idyllic in their chastity.
H« was absolutely untainted by Corsair lusts. The
idea of wrong or dishonour to any woman re-
pelled him even in his youth. If he loved the
village maid, or the maid of the inn, it was
with a love that would place her at his side and
bind to her his homage for ever. This attitude of
mind always maintained enabled him in his very
best work to find an absolutely new track in a
very-well-beaten country That work is “The
Rhyme of Joyous Guard,” the true story of the
man Launcelot, whom Gordon introduces to com-
prehension and worthy esteem better than any
who went before him. Tennyson’s Launcelot,
after direst wrong to man and woman, king and
queen, does but repent as a monk. Gordon’s
Launcelot loves lustily, recklessly, and suffers
correspondingly. He cannot, in the time of the
avenging of his sin, muffle it in any cowl or
strangle it with any rosary, or pluck its sting
from his heart by any process whatever. Proper
natural agony of remorse wrings from him a great
and exceeding bitter cry—
If ever I smote as a man should, smite,
If I struck one stroke that seemed good in Thy sight,
By Thy loving mercy prevailing,
Lord ! let her stand in the light of Thy face,
Clothed with Thy love and crowned with Thy grace,
When I gnash my teeth in the terrible place
That is filled with -weeping and wailing.
The verse is not equal to the sentiment. Gordon
realised more there than he could express ; but I
think that to the nobly intolerant fierceness of
that sentiment in him a good deal of the real
strength of that fin® poem may be attributed.
My good blade carves the casques of men,
My tough lance thrusteth sure,
My strength is as the strength of ten
Because my heart is pure.
Pure in the virile, the manly, not the monkish
sense. In the absolute unalterable conviction that
to the woman he loves man must be absolutely
and eternally loyal and faithful; and that at need
he must face and endure hell to uphold her honour,
atone for her sin, or cloak her shame. More is
born out of that sort of mood than comes forth by
prayer and fasting even. And the agony of re-
morse is commensurate with the passion of fight.
I do not know where to seek elsewhere for such
realistic and flamboyant glorying in battle as is in
the “Joyous Guard.” How sharp is the contrast
between disci pie Peter’s abortive onset (suggestive
of Winkle’s incontinent assault on the small boy
in Pickwick) and the true man’s fighting way.
Not thus on the shattering visor jar?-ed,
In his hand the iron of the Kilt cross-barred,
When the blade was swalloived up to the guard
Through the teeth of the strong blasphemer.
And what of the wild delight of the mSlee, the
rapture of onset —
Then a steel-shod rush and a steel-clad ring,
And a crash of the spear staves splintering
In the billowy battle blended ;
Riot of chargers, revel of bloivs.
And fierce flushed faces of fighting foes,
From croup to bridle that reeled and rose
In a sparkle of sword-play splendid.
It is wonderful work, but not Australian.
Gordon at his best is never, or on one occasion
only, is Australian. “Britomarte,” very fit to be
bracketed with “Joyous Guard,” and infinitely
better from the literary craftsman’s standpoint,
is old-world all. Through it again, observe the
inspiration of the “love that never knew its
earthly close, ” nor had any earthly gratification,
and in it discover, if you may, about as good gal-
loping and fighting verse as the world wots of —
which is a bold saying, but true. Ido not think
any other horse whose hoof-beats sound through
Australian rhymes is fit to make “Britomarte”
extend herself.
In “ The Sick Stockrider” is voiced the best of
the inspiration Australia gave to the wandered,
obsessed, and suffering soul. But that, to those
who have breathed the breath of life of the con-
tinent, is “inside blackfellovv” work altogether.
Its philosophy is “ inside ” philosophy in the same
sense, and that tag which J. J. Snillinglaw “ re-
covered ” and the little literary coterie of Mel-
bourne approved, caps it very fitly.
Enigmas that perplex us in the world’s unequal
strife
The future may ignore or may reveal,
Yet some as weak as ivater, Ned, to make the best of
life
Have been to face the worst as true as steel.
Just so. But we do not choose to grapple Gordon
on to our hearts with stuff of that sort. Its value
is in its dismal harmony with the all-pervading
sentiment of the man’s life. He was bog-ridden
by such a sense of the inevitable as would hardly
permit him to laugh. He could ride, he could
fight, and not recklessly or brainlessly, as is very
commonly supposed now. *‘ A brainier man
never got across a horse,” said one of the eldest of
the old Ballarat riders. “ A ’cuter after hounds
never rode up the wind to keep the track of the
drag.” He was cool as the oldest fighter with the
gloves. But everything with him was to a finish.
Broken hand, cracked ribs, even a smashed
skull mattered nothing so long as be could get to
his feet and order, by any means, the impulses of
the tortured brain.
Pity he did not face Ballarat in the earliest of
his Australian days. The mounted-trooper life
in South Australia was the most sordid he ever
endured, and when he pitched it up for horse-
breaking down about Penola, it was to camp on
the outer fringe of whatever was heartening and
inspiring in the life of the time. There was, as
we know, good fighting in those days, but it was
Gordon’s bad luck never to get into the actual
fighting line. The muddling away of the belated
Eatrimony is not pleasant reading, nor yet the
usiness side of the Ballarat career. There was
not much joy in the bit of cottage by Lake
Wendonree ; and in the fierce and lusty devildom
which still hung about the digging town he had no
part. He would have been seen, at his best,
probably, at the Riddochs’ place, YsLllum, where
his wife stayed a good deal, and he visited
frequently; or at Herbert Power’s home at
Toorak later on. But the best was always shaded
with moroseness, the worst redeemed from any
touch of baseness by innate and cherished chivalry ;
and from sordidness even more detestable, by the
scant grace he had of his birth.
J*
Happiness to Gordon must, I think, have been
mostly in dreamland. Riding was always in grim
earnest for money, needed the moment it was
won. Like Keats, when out of the body he
radiated ’twixt extreme heights. The “ long white
plumes” would dance before his eyes, the virgin
choir would “ make delicious moan.” But all the
time round about him
The hills like giants at a hunting lay
Chin upon hand to see the game at bay.
And always he knew that to the dark tower he
should come at last. Podas Okus is good dream-
ing—
Lower yet, my own Briseis,
Denser shadows veil the light —
And how supremely good is the ending !
Pallas ! thus thy soldier comes !
Not thus did he approach that dark tower,
“blind as a fool’s heart,” of his own fate. It grew
on him down by the melancholy coast where at
night time seems the breadth of the outer sea, but
lifelessness therewith.
Drones a murmwr faintly splashing,
’Tis the splash of Charon’s oar.
After that long talk with Kendall and the
subsequent long journey in the chilly fore-night,
he felt the closing in, the snapping of the jaws of
the gin. The pinion of Apollyon’s bird had
touched him, the horrors strewn on the threshold
had appalled but could not repel him. Revela-
tion came, and imperative command and irre-
sistible impulse—
Burningly it came on him all at once,
I'here stood the dark tower blind as a fool s heart,
Without a counterpart in the whole world.
And yet
Dauntless the horn unto his lips he set
And blew—Childe Adam to the dark tower came.
Shall vve repeat it once again, the
Frater ave atque vale
of best beloved paganism ? And end thus the little
attempt at the recovery of what was actual and
true from much that has since been heaped up in
misapprehension, narrow-mindedness, literary
Eharisaism and conventional oommonplace with
is own verse—
With a flash that ends thy pain
Respite and oblivion blest
Come to greet thee.. lin vain
Fall; I rise to fall again :
Thou hast fallen to thy rest-
And thy fall is best.
F.M
MEMORANDA
Jack Cameron (brother of Mary Gilmore), a Westra-
lian journalist who went as war correspondent in South
Africa and is now employed in London, has written
A History of the Guerilla War in Cape Colony, soon to
be published in Dutch and English.
N.S.W. Legislative Assembly recently voted £m to
Roderic Quinn, for a year’s work in compiling widely,
distributed literary matter designed to advertise New
South Wales.
The proprietors of The Sydney Mail advertise
a prize of ATOO for the best tale, suitable for publication
in The Mail, reoeived before 30th June, 1904. Only resi-
dents in the Commonwealth may compete, Copyright
reverts to the author six months after serial publication
is completed.
An exhibition of some of Phil May’s English
drawings, including many contributed to Punch, was
announced to be held in London early in October. The
curious thing about the reprint of May’s Punch work that
has reached Australia since his death, is that (as a local
illustrator comments) many of the drawings “ do not look
like Phil May.” In a sense, and generally speaking, the
handling is improved as compared with the Australian
work: it is softer, more free,-better for illustration.
Bui it has lost a great deal of May’s peculiar
vivacity, vigour, individual charm. The London
work is at once stronger and weaker. And, as it is
stronger in the direction of subjective art—which was
not May'8 best point, and weaker in the direction of
objective art—the statement of line and mass, colour and
form, apart from illustrative meaning—where May was
pre-eminent, one is free to prefer a drawing like “A
Curiosity in Her Own Country ” (No. 384 of the Sydney
sale) to almost anything May did in England except the
“ Guttersnipes.”
REGEIVED
Roimd Anvil Rock, by Nancy Huston Banks
(Lond., Macmillan ; 2s. 6 d.) [On« of the tribe of
anaemic American novels, moulded to the ideals of
Boston. A “ real elegant" romance of externals- with
the proper sentiment, and the preferred historical
reference, and the familiar insipidity.]
A Passage Perilous, by Rosa Nouchette Carey
(Lond., Macmillan ; 2s. 6<f.) [Here is a third-rate
Englishwoman’s novel in contrast with the preceding.
Rosa Nouchette comes of the homely stock ol Mrs.
Henry Wood. Her books are Mrs. Wood’s without the
spice of melodrama —the characters and incidents less
shrewdly gripped. They furnish good reading for the
disappearing domestic female. Compared with the
thin American culture-novel, they are much to be pre-
ferred. The English solidity is in them ; their conces-
sion to sentiment is based on honest instinct; and their
sincere commonplace is healthier and far more agree-
able than the high-flown artificiality found over the
water.]
Re-echoes from Coondambo, by Robert Bruce
(Lond.. Long ; 5s. net). [Four hundred and thirty-one
pages of bad verses by a retired South Australian
squatter].
That Great Lying Church of England, by
Morrison Davidson (Lond., F. R. Henderson; ls.).ex-
hibits the aged and learned reformer to advantage.
His facts and arguments for disestablishment are
curious and valuable.
Poems of Henry Clarence Kendall, with Memoir
by Frederick C. Kendall. Revised and enlarged edition.
(Sydney, George Robertson Proprietary ; 5s.)
Sarah Tuldon, by Orme Agnus (Lond., Ward,
Lock; 3s. 6 d.). [“The Romance of a Brave”...
woman. A study of English peasant life in Dorsetshire,
somewhat in Hardy's manner, but more close and de-
tailed, with little of the generalising spirit that
ennobles Hardy’s work. In its genre an unusual book,
well worth reading.]

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
By the Sea.
Have you heard the snowy wave-lips whisper
Their wondrous legends on the yellow sands ?
And have you felt how roaming winds blow crisper
There, where the seas and lands
Lie breast to breast, with inter-twining hands?
Have you seen the white spray-fountains springing
When the waves meet the sculptured, glistening
rocks ?
And have you seen the long, brown sea-weeds
clinging
In festoons and in looks,
And firmest where the wave most rudely shocks ?
Turn from the opulent might of seething tides
To mermaid-pools, prisoned in rocky sides :
Stars of a bright vermilion,
And stars of a living brown;
Bright sea-flowers in the weedy bowers,
Floating sceptre and crown ;
Spine-girded globes and discs star-rayed—
And where was the plan of the marvels made ?
Shells of a Tyrian purple,
And shells of iris dyes ;
Shells of the pearl and the rose’s furl,
And shells of the summer skies;
Scallop and whorl, volute and fan—
And where is the wonderful artizan ?
Turn from the magic mermaid-pools ; and dream
What wealth of beauty hides in ocean’s gleam !
M.L.
AHAU.
Athirst.
Dear, never think those days of passion ended
With waving hands and widening stretch of sea;
Still in my heart is fiercest longing blended
With far, faint hope; how is it, dear, with thee?
I am not false to thee, nor have bereft thee
Of one swift thought; my soul is still thy slave !
I have but sought to ’suage the thirst you left me
All unappeased, though wealth of love you gave.
There have been other arms as soft and clinging
Since those mad days when Heaven and Hell
were one ;
And other lips have love’s sweet song been
singing:
Art thou, O love of mine, content with none ?
Nay! I know well! Of all sweet women living,
Of all the passion-lipped of days gone by,
Thou, whose great love but greater grows in
giving,
O, perfect flower of flame, must love or die.
N.S.W.
J.
Love’s Defeat.
Love stood suppliant at the gate
Where Beauty’s face was smiling;
And even while he bent his bow,
With tender words beguiling,
His bright wings hid the cunning dart
He fain would fix in Beauty’s heart.
“ Come hither, sweet,” said jealous Love,
“ My golden bow is harmless,
And youth that lacks my kiss,” said he,
“ I trow were sad and charmless.”
But still the maid, with feigning coy,
Disdainful, mocked the ardent boy.
“ Beware, sweet maid,” he whispered oft,
“ Perchance, if thou defy me,
The wine of life shall lose its glow,
And joy for ever fly thee.”
"Nay, pretty Love,” she smiling, said,
“ The days of old romance are dead.”
While still Love pleaded, sore at heart,
In words fond, wise, and burning,
A mighty lord rode proudly by,
His mien Love’s armor spurning;
And even while he woo’d the maid
Poor Cupid saw her coyness fade.
“ My name is Gold,” the gallant said,
All bold to Beauty pleading.
She laid her hands within his own,
Love’s offer all unheeding.
He turned in triumph : “ Fool ! ” quoth he,
“ To dream that you could cope with me.”
Poor Love went sadly from the gate
When Beauty so betrayed him,
And down among the summer grass
In musing sorrow laid him.
“Life is an empty jest,” he sighed,
. “ When hearts with gold are satisfied.”
S.A.
PATRIO DARE.
The Bose Lovers.
I gave her a rose in early June,
Fed with the sun and dew;
Each petal, I said, is a note in the tune,
The rose is the whole tune through and through.
The tune is the whole red-hearted rose,
Flush and form, honey and hue,
Lull with the cadence, and throb to the close,
I love you, I love you, I love you.
She gave me a rose in early June,
Fed with the sun and dew;
Each petal, she said, is a mount in the moon,
The rose is the whole moon through and through.
The moon is the whole pale-hearted rose,
Bound and radiance, burnish and blue,
Break in the flood-tide that murmurs and flows,
I love you, I love you, I love you.
This is our love in early June,
Fed with the sun and dew,
Moonlight and roses hid in a tune ;
The roses are music through and through.
The moonlight falls in the breath of the rose.
Light and cadence, honey and hue,
Mingle, and murmur, and flow to the close,
I love you, I love you, I love you.
N.S.W.
MAGGIE SULLIVAN

Acute Dyspepsia.
“Clements Tonic Restored
all my Strength and Vigor.”
The Case of tyre. R. WATKINS.
(by our reporter.)
Thers is a period in every person’s life,
particularly when in sorrow, that the sym-
pathy of a friend in need is greatly appre-
ciated, and although it is difficult to con-
jecture how many hearts may be gladdened
by the news appearing below, it is safe to
remark that Mrs Rachael Watkins, of
Grosvenor-street, Woollahra, was actuated
by the best of motives in saying :
“ My health was in such an unsatisfac-
tory state some time ago that I could not
enjoy life. Every day I gut up with a
splitting headache which made me feel sad
and wretched from morning till night, and,
owing to the taste I had, I felt so sick that
I could not touch any breakfast. My tongue
was dry and my throat was so parched that
I was constantly drinking water, yet the
thirst from which I suffered still remained.
Sometimes midday went by and still I did
not feel hungry, but as sure as I did take a
little food I paid dearly for it afterwards.”
“In what way, Mrs. Watkins ? ” enquired
the pressman.
“I used to feel ill all over me, especially
if there happened to be a little salt in my
food, which had the effect of making my
mouth and throat so much inflamed that it
was painful for me to swallow anything;
but the stomach seemed to be the worst
part, as it was distended with wind
which came from the food which had not
digested, and the cramping pains inside
were simply horrible. Windy spasms often
caused me to get up in the middle of the
night and walk the floor in agony, for it
was more than I could do to lie there quietly
and suffer such frightful torture. What-
ever I had eaten always repeated, but it
would not come up, much as I would have
liked it to, as then I would have felt a
little easier.”
“ How long had yon Suffered in this
way ? ”
“I was very ill for twelve -months.
Everything went wrong, even my chest
being painful with a heavy sensation which
almost stifled me. Many a time I tried to
vomit, thinking that if I could only do so I
would be much relieved, but the lump in
my chest seemed to be a fixture, and fre-
quently made me feel as if I were taking
my last breath, the trouble in breathing
was so great. I became so terrified that I
might suffocate whilst in bed that I used to
prop myself up with pillows, and, notwith-
standing the fact that I always felt weak
and worn out, I could not sleep when I did
retire, being very restless till about two or
three o’clock in the morning. When I
ought to have been asleep was the only
part of the twenty-four hours that I seemed
to liven up a bit, but it was very unnatural
for me to be so fidgety, and my system was
drained of all its vital force on that account.
If I did fall asleep I began dreaming, and
when I woke up I tried to recollect what I
had been dreaming about, but my memory
was so defective that I could not recall any-
thing t® mind. Scarcely an hour passed
without some annoyance being caused by
sharp pains between my shoulders, and es-
pecially under my left shoulder-blade, and
my legs ached so much that after standing a
little while the aches developed into dis-
tinct pains. Across my loins the aches
were really terrible. I used to think they
came through sitting down so much, but I
found when I walked the pains were still
more severe. The least effort on my part
resulted in giddiness coming on, sol had to
be very careful how I went about, particu-
larly as my eyes were often so affected that
I could scarcely see out of them. If I
stooped to pick up anything from the floor
I felt half-dazed for a while, my head reel-
ing awfully, and small black specks dancing
all round. Not only did I lose all my
strength, but my nervous system was quite
unstrung.”
“I believe you consulted a doctor, Mrs.
Watkins. W hat did he say your complaint
teas ? ”
“My doctor said I was suffering from
acute indigestion, and he treated me accord-
ingly, but to my intense dismay I could
see I had no hope of recovering under his
treatment, as the medicines he prescribed
did not have any good effect. In my sor-
rowful condition I lost heart completely. I
did not know what to do, especially as I had
got so much more feeble that on many oc-
casions I had to lie down in a state of ex-
haustion the whole of the day. It looked
to me as though my remaining days were
to be spent in misery, from which there was
no escape; but I thought I ought to try
everything at my disposal, so I started doc-
toring myself with advertised medicines,
and when I had taken enough of them to
base an opinion upon, I was forced to con-
clude that my afflictions were incurable.
As those medicines were also useless I
thought, in my desperation, that everything
else was the same, but a lady friend spoke
so favourably about Clements Tonic that I
could not help buying some.”
“ Did you find it acting as your friend
said it would ? ”
“Yes, thank God! her advice was not
misleading, for Clements Tonie had precisely
the effect which I was told it would have,
although my condition was so low that it
took a little time to make headway. Once
Clements Tonic got my maladies on the
move, however, it shifted them with great
despatch. I started by picking up my ap-
petite and getting an occasional sound
night’s rest; then, when my head was free
from pain, I slept tranquilly right through
every night, and the aches left my limbs by
degrees. I considered this a great amount
of success for Clements Tonic to achieve,
taking into account the repeated failures I
had met with, so I got more of it as it was
required, and I shall never regret that I
stuck steadfastly to the same remedy, to
which there was nothing to compare for so
restoring my internal organs to a healthy
state that my food digested properly. My
vision became clear, the distention of and
pains in my stomach did not occur again,
and the stifling sensations left my chest.
To put it briefly, I was relieved of every
pain which had made my life a martyrdom,
besides having my nervousness quite
abolished, and you may depend I was de-
voutly thankful to Clements Tonic for bring-
ing back all my strength and vigor. In
every respect I was like a new woman, for
I had suffered from rheumatism close on

all cases of sickness I recommend to my

EDINBURGH and BIRMINGHAM
Wore a Torture
BELT.
SUFFERED TERRIBLE AGONY.
From wearing an Electric Belt
charged by the use of
SULPHURIG TIG ID.
Mr. W. Folley, Alfred Woollen Mills,
Williamstown, Vic., writing to the Dr.
McLaughlin Electric Belt Co., tells how he
was scarred and burned by the use of a
FPee Belt, which he had erroneously
been led to believe was as good as theirs.
He paid £\ for the Free Belt because
, he had been convinced that by paying
this cash price he was obtaining a similar
appliance to Dr. McLaughlin’s Famous
rejuvenator which has gained World
Wide fame as a Curative Agent. It goes
to show how misleading are advertise-
ments which seem to offer something for
nothing.
Gentlemen —In answer to your kind in-
quiries after my health, I beg to state that
I have been completely cured of the Scia-
tica that has troubled me for years. I
cannot thank you enough for your gentle-
manly treatment and fair dealing with your
patient 9, as I know from others that their
treatment has been the same as mine.
I may just as well let you know my ex-
perience in seeking a cure for my trouble
before I purchased one of your High Grade
Belts. I had been under doctors and re-
ceived scant relief; and also tried various
quacks, but it was all the same. I then
placed myself in the hands of a Specialist
(in Melbourne) guaranteed to cure Rheu-
matism and Sciatica, but received very
small benefit from the treatment. I then
had my attention drawn to Electricity.
Well, I thought, “ Never venture, never
win,” so I PROCURED first of all a Belt
for £l and was very much disappointed
because I was informed that it was neces-
sary to take a long and exceedingly ex-
pensive course of drug treatment. I would
not do this and then I wrote for, and ob-
tained a so-called FPee Belt, with a
most “ Wonderful Pedigree,” at a cost of
M- i wore the Belt according to the in-
structions given faithfully for a period of
five weeks, and by that time I was Burnt
and Blistered so badly that I could not
wear it longer, and still no relief was
gained, although the Belt was guaranteed
to Cure Anything in Sciatica in One
Month. You bet I was very much dis-
couraged in regard to Electric Belts.
However, I was persuaded to come and
just see you, gentlemen. I then found
that I had been wearing a Torture Belt
and very Wrongly Applied, and quite
enough to give and patient a Horror of
so-called Electric Belts. After the con-
sultation I decided to place myself in your
hands. I knew my case was very diffi-
cult, as I was compelled to follow my em-
ployment, and standing at the vice was
irksome, and the complaint was a very
stubborn one. However, gentlemen, your
Belt, coupled with your advice, has made
me a grateful patient. I thank you kindly
for supplying me with a remedy which has
Completely Cured Me, and strongly recom-
mend it to all and sundry that I find are
suffering from the distressing malady
called “ Sciatica.”
I have not been wearing the Belt for the
past fortnight, but have passed it over to
my wife, who has received great benefit
for nervous troubles.
I remain, your grateful patient,
W. FOLLEY

THURSDAY, NOVEMBER 5, 1903.
Price 6d.
Recent London cables :
“SONS OF THE EMPIRE.-PROPOSED TABLET IN
ST. PAUL’S.
“London, Tuesday.— A cartoon showing the Princess Louise’s
design for a bronze memorial is now on exhibition in St. Paul’s
Cathedral. The design shows an angel with outstretched wings lean-
ing tenderly over a figure of the crucified Redeemer. It is intended
for a tablet commemorating the deeds of the colonial sons of the
Empire in the late war.”
“ London, 20th October.—Mr. Cresswell was examined yesterday
before the Rand Native Labor Commission. He stated that the chair-
man of the London directorate of his company had written to him
privately to the effect that Messrs. Wernher, Beit and Co. and other
Rand mining magnates had been consulted on the question of employ-
ing unskilled white labor, and that they were adverse. They feared
that the engaging of a large number of white laborers on the Rand
would ‘ cause the same troubles as had prevailed in Australia,’ and
would enable a combination of labor to dictate the rates of wages,
and to dictate also politically when responsible government came to
be established in the Transvaal.”

THURSDAY, NOVEMBER 5, 1903.
Wanted-a Freetrade Party which
Doesn’t Find its Own Gospel Too
Obscene for Daily Use.
New South Wales was, for many years, the one
alleged Freetrade State in Australasia, and the
solitary beacon of Cobdenism in a dark continent,
and it is claimed that it is still in the beacon busi-
ness, more or less, though the light is obscured by
extraneous circumstances. In the bygone years it
had every kind of Freetrade politician—except
the one who practised Freetrade. It had Free-
trade politicians who shouted about the beauties
of Freetrade and the accursedness and obvious
folly of Protection ; it had many lying Freetrade
politicians who said they practised Freetrade
when they knew they didn’t; it had Freetrade
politicians who didn’t get their hair cut, who bor-
rowed half-sovereigns, who were deficient in edu-
cation, who wrote alleged poetry, who dealt in
shady land transactions—in fact, it had every
possible Freetrade politician, except the one who
had enough faith in his own exploded creed to put
it into practice. Their unanimity in the matter
of avoiding their own blessed political remedy was
the most unanimous thing the country has ever
seen - *
Twelve years ago, in 1891, the great Freetrade
epoch of Henry Parkes came to an end. His
Government included those highly-superior
scoffers at Protection, M'Millan, Bruce Smith,
Joey Oarruthers. Gould, Pa Brunkeb, Sydney
Smith, and James Henry Young—all dodderers
of great size whose elevated snort of pity at persons
who believed in Protection was so superior that
they might have been a drove of emperors or an
association of brass gods. And this was the
tariff which they supplied to N.S.W. :
M'Millan and Sydney Smith Protective Tariff
(up to 1891).
Import Excise Protection to

* * * *
After M ‘Millan and the two Smiths and
Young and Pa Brunker and the rest of the
superior and conscientious Freetraders who pro-
tected 43 N.S.W. industries had dropped out, the
State had a period of definite and honest, though
insufficient Protection, under the Dibbs Govern-
ment. The Dibbs Government wasn’t so very
much more protective than the Pumpkin Smith-
Bruoe Smith-M‘Millan Government, but it
didn’t lie about it, and the rooster of reproach
didn’t crow on its fence to remind it that it was a
traitor to its principles. Then followed the
alleged Freetrade Government of Reid, Sydney
Smith, Joe Cook, Pa Brunker, and other strange
characters, which was even more astonished than
the old M'MiLLAN push that anyone could believe
in so outlandish a thing as Protection, and which
pitied Protectionists with so wild and lofty a sniff
that it might have been a herd of archangels.
This was its tariff :
Reid and Sydney Smith and Joe Cook Tariff
(up to 1901).
Protection
Import Excise to local
duties. duties. product.
Beer (draught) .. 6d per gal. 3d per gal. 3d per gal.
„ (bottled) .. 9d „ 3d „ 6d „

Penny newspapers Id postage Nil each.
Also a large general Protection by differential railway
and wharfage rates on coal and agricultural produce.
* * * *
The Bulletin goes back to this subject (and
will probably keep going back to it for some time)
because it involves a question which badly needs
to be answered, yet as regards which it seems as
if dynamite wouldn’t blow a reply out of a single
Freetrade politician. Reid and the two Smiths,
and Joe Cook and Conroy are all prepared to
hold forth for three hours at a stretch about every
question except this one, which is the most
important of all; as regards it, their silence is
such that the grave is pandemonium by compari-
son. So that there may be no mistake, the situa-
tson is here categorically set forth once more :
(1) Any and every politician, paper or person who cir-
culates the hackneyed tale about the relative progress of
N.S.W. under Freetrade as compared with Victoria and
other places under Protection is a liar, and knows he is
one ; for N.S.W. never had Freetrade. Being a liar, he
may settle with his own conscience the question whether
he is a gentleman or not.
(2) Every Freetrade politician who has held office in
N.S.W. has practised Protection of set purpose. He was
not driven to it by revenue necessities ; instead he lost
revenue by it. By raising the excise on local beer, wine,
tobacco, cigars, and sugar, for instance, to the level of
the import duties, the essential feature of Freetrade
(complete freedom of competition and complete absence
of Protection) could have been secured along with an
increase of revenue. The party which paid away a lot of
money for the privilege of being a traitor is something
unique.
(3) These Freetrade politicians either lied when they
said they believed cordially in Freetrade as the best
policy to promote the interests of the country, or they
deliberately tried to injure the country. In the latter
case the subsidiary question arises whether they were
hired by Paul Kruger or by some other violent outside
person.
• * * *
That is the situation as regards the past. The
past is dead, but its bones still hang around in an
unpleasant sort of way. With regard to the
present and future these bones have a large
significance. The alleged Freetraders, who now
ask to be put in charge of the destinies of
Australia, are the same men who did
this unexampled fraud and treason in the
past. And they propose to do exactly
the same fraud and treason in the future.
Among all the crowd of political bounders who
insult the intelligence of Australia with the
assurance that Freetrade is an axiomatic truth,
and the only way to commercial salvation, there
is not one who promises to try Freetrade when he
gets into power. There isn’t one who doesn’t
shudder at the idea of his callous gospel in actual
practice. Not one person in the whole bunch is
prepared to advocate that every import duty
which is necessary for revenue purposes shall be
counterbalanced by an exactly equal excise duty,
that postage and railway rates on Australian
papers shall be the same as on imported ones,
that railway and wharfage rates on local produce
shall be the same as on foreign produce, that all
public contracts shall be given to the cheapest
tenderer in fact, that Protection shall be
utterly abolished. The Freetrader says that
his gospel 13 the only one that can make
the country great and prosperous, that he
flatly declines to make the country great
and prosperous, that Protection is utterly
disastrous, that he will practise a certain amount
of the disastrous policy if he gets into power, that
Protectionists are fools, and that lie proposes to
borrow a good part of the fools’ policy; and that
kind of Freetrader is an undesirable person. Yet
he is the only kind Australia seems to have.
• • • *
The question has been asked why Protectionists
should object so much to the clan of Reid, and
Bruce Smith, and Bruce Smith’s little brother
Pumpkin, and Joe Cook, and the rest, seeing that
they are prepared to give so much Protection in
practice, however much they denounce it in
theory. But the Australian Protectionist, unlike
the Australian Freetrader, has some reverence for
his political faith. He doesn’t want to see it
mauled in the dirty hands of renegades; he doesn’t
want to see monkeys drinking out of his com-
munion cup and apes gambolling in his tabernacle.
‘ He wants the ape to go and gambol in his own
tabernacle. If Protection is good enough to be
practised even in a small degree, it is also good
enough to be preached. If the Freetrade party
is willing to give the country Freetrade, then it
has as much right to office as any other party,
and is as honest as any other party, and The
Bulletin will treat it with respect accordingly.
As matters stand, and judged by its past record,
it is outside the pale of honest dealing, and the
laws which apply to other parties don’t apply to
it. So far, it hasn’t been a party in the customary
sense of the term, but a crowd of political spielers
living on the most impudent confidence trick ol
the generation.
The Australian Political Position.
There is no sure light ahead in Australian politics
yet. Mr. Reid, on behalf of the Opposition, has
exhibited his skill in ground and lofty tumbling,
feats of legerdemain, and lightning-change turns,
at 2s. per head, in various Victorian towns and
hamlets. The Labor party has issued a well-
thought-out and moderate manifesto, summarising
its work during the past Parliament—most of it
excellent, some of it mistaken. Premier Deakin
has spoken at Ballarat, and devoted his energy
mainly to being eloquent. “ Eloquence in a
public man is often, like beauty in a poor woman,
a fatal gift. Charmed with his own faculty of
uprearing lofty edifices of well-polished words,
with bright stained-glass trimmings, the elo-
quent politician is, in time, almost necessarily
obsessed with the notion that it is the
form and not the substance which is im-
portant in pronouncements on public policy;
that what is said is not of so much account as how
it is said. Such is a foolish notion to get into the
head of a public man in any country ; in Australia
it is particularly foolish, because in no country m
the world is there less real sympathy with eloquent
high-faluting. The Australian is inclined to be
somewhat cynical, a good deal matter-of-fact, and
very little inclined to give reverence to sonorous
periods and lofty flights of sentences. His typical
word for eloquence is “ gab,’’ and for any gifts of
language outside of those appropriate to the
bullocky he has, if not contempt, mere amused
toleration. Plain statements or plain issues
put directly, and—if that is the gift of
the speaker—with some wit, appeal to hint
best. But almost the whole stock-in-trade of
Premier Deakin is his eloquence and his lady-
like manner. He would as soon be seen in public
with a plain unvarnished statement as with a
straw hat over a frock coat. It was to have been
expected then, as the event proved, that his
Ballarat speech would be highly ornamental, and
not very useful in the direction of indicating a
straightforward and resolute party programme.
Alb red Deakin is not the man to plod along a
dusty road to the attainment of an arduous duty ;
he would sooner sit down by the wayside and
moralise. It is pretty moralising, and Mr.
Deakin makes a pretty figure as he speaks ; and
that’s the end of it. The Commonwealth still
lacks a policy for its next Parliament.
* * * *
Without any sure beacon ahead, there are one
or two points of guidance. George Reid’s plat-
form (which is, of course, not necessarily any
indication of his real views), as so far announced,
puts him out of serious consideration. All that
he proposes is to go back on the past, and, with a
mutilating hand, hack at the good work already
accomplished by the Federal Parliament. He
would break with the White Australia principle
by amending the Postal Act so as to allow Aus-
tralian money to go in subsidy of lascar-employing
capitalists, and Australian coastal shipping to be
subjected to the competition of black-labor boats,
and by withdrawing the encouragement which Aus-
tralia now gives to the Boy of the Bulldog Breed.
He would so change the tariff as to greatly reduce
its Protective incidence in order to serve his
calico friends. As regards the preferential trade
issue, he professes to be in favor of allowing
to British imports a 50 per cent, tariff concession,
which would certainly destroy the revenue sol-
vency of Australia. With all this destruction-
destruction of the White Australia policy, of the
measure of Protection existing, and of the finan-
cial basis of the Commonwealth —Mr. Reid pro-
poses nothing constructive. Clearly he and his
following should be, as far as possible, swept away
at the general elections. The fact that if Mr.
Reid is returned and finds it convenient to do so,
he will turn completely around and be as Protec-
tionist as Mr. Kingston, and as radical as Mr.
Watson, doesn’t affect the validity of this argu-
ment. Mr. Reid is in the position of a fox with
a brand to his tail being driven into the harvest-
fields of the Commonwealth by his master, the
foreign importer. His own secret views may not
be in sympathy with the work of destruction,
and, being a wily fellow, he may be able to
elude his drivers and take up a more comfortable
and less dangerous role. But Australia can take
no risks. Mr. Reid’s present platform is purely
destructive, and the destruction of the Reid
following is one absolutely sure and certain duty
of the electors in December.
* » * *
The Labor party has, on the whole, the best
record of the past Parliament, and from it is to
be reasonably expected the best programme for
the coming one. It has, by its insistence on a
white Australia; by its opposition to the fetters
of Imperialism; by its sturdy and successful
stand against inoculating the Commonwealth with
the borrowing virus; by taking a thoroughly
Australian attitude on questions of defence
—made itself more truly representative of
the Australian democracy than any other
of the party organisations. In some respects, it
has failed. It has not yet, for instance, learned
that the necessary complement of fair-wage con-
ditions in Australia is high Protection, since,
while the products of sweated labor can enter our
ports and compete in our markets, the home
worker must either sweat or go workless. The
Labor party is made up mostly of Protectionists,
but has a few members of foreign-trade views, and,
as a concession to them, “sinks the fiscal issue.”
That, and practically that alone, prevents it from
being accepted as a _ truly national party,
but does not prevent it, in the present lack
of such a party, having, on the whole, the best
claim to the electors’ confidence. The party which,
in addition to “fiscal peace,” promises a_ devo-
tion to Australian as against crazy-imperial ideals,
is the one best deserving of support.
• • * *
The Deakin following cannot be deprived of
the credit of having co-opBrated with the Labor
party in securing a White Australia and the other
sterling national reforms of the last Parliament.
It has, in the past, gone a long way on the path
to securing a prosperous, a free and a White
Australia. Now, however, it lacks a future rather
than a past. Mr. Deakin is eloquent on what
has been done, but very silent as to what is to be
done. He aspires to be a beautiful note of
admiration for a space of three years. And
the call is for something more progressive.
On only one question, preferential trade,
does he propose anything definite. His idea
of increasing the present duties against the
foreigner, whilst leaving them as they are against
British goods, is undoubtedly the best method, if
preference is decided on at all—a question which
cannot be resolved until Britain has made its
choice between Freetrade and Protection. Further
than that, Premier Deakin does not go in affirma-
tive policy. He does not propose to improve the
preßant tariff, though he knows that it needs im-
provement. He says nothing at all about the
financial issue—whether he intends to be a borrow-
ing Premier, or a Premier who lets public works
go to ruin, or an honest Premier who does
neither, but regards the Australian as a
possibly self-reliant and self respecting person
who can build his post office out of bis
own pocket. He is silent, too, on the issue
of the progress, or otherwise, of the Federal
idea, and makes no suggestion as to the increase
of the power of the Australian Parliament. VV hat-
ever indication lie gives regarding his attitude as
between the State-frights party and the Australian
party is discouraging ; on the issue of the Arbitra-
tion Bill he will be just as much the creature of
Irvine as was his predecessor, Barton, and will
refuse to bring State Government employees
under its provisions. To practically all the
questions put to Mr. Deakin by The Bulletin
last week, he was either silent or gave an
unsatisfactory reply. National Australians can
hardly follow him now with any consistency. So
far as he has gone, he is mostly right, but then he
proposes to go no further forward, but some dis-
tance backward. And with an absolutely demo-
cratic franchise and a Legislature entirely repre-
sentative of the people, with no Chamber of mere
obesity and no block property-vote to hinder its
work, there is no reason why Australia should not
go a great deal further.
As a moderate and reasonable programme for
the forthcoming session, The Bulletin would
propose
An Industrial Arbitration Bill embracing all workers,
so far as the Constitution allows ; a very firm attitude
towards Irvine or any other small State Premier who
ventures to make remarks on the subject. Federal old
age pensions. An intimation to all the State Govern-
ments that the Commonwealth is anxious to take over
the whole subject of industrial legislation.
Federalising of the State debts. An absolute prohibi-
tion of further borrowing, except for renewal or conver-
sion of old loans.
Federal land tax— progressive as regards large estates,
extra progressive as regards absentees. A Federal income
tax on absentees.
The strengthening of the system of Protection, as
regards at least one branch of industry—say the iron
industry—so as to wholly substitute Australian manu-
factures for imports.
The settlement of the Federal capital on a nationalised
area of at least 1000 (and, if possible, 5000) square miles,
where a Federal port is available. The appointment of a
democratic and able High Commissioner.
There are many other matters of pressing im-
portance which might be settled by a really strong
Premier within the space of the next Parlia-
ment. But this programme is put forward as
a moderate “ instalment ” one. Unfortunately it
is apparent that Premier Deakin is not the. man
to adopt it, since that would mean doffing his fine
frock-coat, and putting his silk ha,t in the dust, and
forgetting his eloquence, and feeling, if not speak-
ing, “bullocky.” It is impossible to imagine Premier
Deakin saying “damn ” publicly; and that is a
mournful thing to say of a man who wishes to lead
in affairs of state. There is a leader capable of
taking up and forcing through such a programme—
Kingston, and the best hope for Australian
democracy is that the electors will return enough
men of his mind to, with the help of the Labor
party, put him in power. The next best hope is
that the Kingston party and the Labor party will
be, if not strong enough to hold office, at least
strong enough to push Premier Deakin along.
Deakin has no great power of motion of his own,
but he would probably progress if pushed firmly.
The Sound of Freedom’s Song.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I loved it well —the sound of Freedom’s song
That rang in every pulse’s joyful beating;
I felt a sunlit Day would dawn ere long
For every downcast serf of Adam’s throng;
I felt Man yet would hail with proudest greeting
The sound of Freedom’s Song !
I love it still —dear Freedom’s sacred name !
Though one by one I see the weak surrender,
Altho’ her one-time champions do her shame
And desecrate the altars of her flame,
I cherish yet with rev’rence deep and tender
Dear Freedom’s sacred name !
I love it yet—the sound of Freedom’s song!
I hear her song for aye —the deathless siren
Of coming days that drive the Powers of Wrong,
Of strenuous years that come, serene and strong,
To help us burst our serf-enchained environ !
The sound of Freedom’s Song !
Q.
P.I.

Bill Lyne Makes Concessions.
The Bulletin, in the name of public honesty,
demands of Premier Deakin an explanation of
this item published in the daily press, and not, so
far, corrected: —
Definite action has at last been taken by Mr. Ltnb to
abolish the rule laid down by Mr. Kingston that all per-
sons guilty of breaches of the Customs Act, «hetner
innocently or fraudulently, should be haled before t
police-court. To-day he approved of regulations to em-
power the collectors of customs in the seieral States to
investigate certain charges laid under the provisions o
the Customs Act. The Minister stated that the cases
that the collectors would deal with would be those 1
which there was no suspicion of fraud. If fraud was ala-
closed during investigation, there would be P?*'® r J; "
once remit the case to the police-court when jt t
simple mistake had been made, the Minister add
the recommendation of the collector in each case 1
be forwarded to him, and he (the Minister) would dete -
mine the extent of the punishment.
If Premier Deakin doesn’t come forward with an
explanation, it must be concluded that he assent?
to this announcement by Mr. Lyne. In that case,
he must plead guilty either to—
(1) Deliberately reversing the honest poKcyof an'w-
colleague, and thereby making room for dishonesty and
Ch (2) a c e rueily unjustly maligninsr the character and
administration of an ex-colleague in order to pander to
dishonest section of the community.
The Customs policy of Kingston was not only
iust, but it was merciful. In June, 19w, 1 _
Sydney daily papers, which had been “H 1 *
otherwise, were challenged by Mr. Kingst -
come along and investigate for themael es
working of his department as regards prosecu-
tions. He did not ask for an inquiry .
facts by a judicial body; he t i, em .
accusers to come and get the facts
selves, and twist them, if they couid, mtoa jmh
fication of their accusations. The change -
accepted, and Sydney Telegraph the
result of its investigations (a full statement t
which was reprinted in The Bulletin 20, Mo!.
The investigation showed that from J J,’
to May 31, 1903, in N.S.W. there were detected
586 cases of errors in invoices and there were Jl
89 prosecutions; in the other 49/ ,
offenders were given the benefit of the dou


treated as honest but mistaken folk. Of the 89
prosecutions only six failed —the courts upheld 83
of them. The 586 offences under the Customs
Act were classed under various headings. In the
first heading (which was the only one under which
“ honest error” was obviously a reasonable plea)
there were 164 cases and no prosecutions. Under
only one heading, that of importing prohibited
goods (such as vile obscenities) were there
more than 25 per cent, of prosecutions. The
return showed clearly that Kingston’s adminis-
tration had not been harsh; that it had allowed
the maximum of mercy, consistent with justice.
Any modification, then, of his system must mean
injustice and concessions to dishonesty. Either,
therefore, Minister Lyne means to conduct the
Customs on a system less than honest, or, for pur-
poses of pandering to the importing interest,
represents that Kingston’s previous honest
administration was tyrannical and unfair and is to
be modified, though intending all the while not to
depart from it. Which horn of the dilemma does
Lyne chose to be impaled upon ? And is Premier
Deakin content to be in the same pillory ?

The Loan Mental Disease.
An interesting example of a gallant effort to sup-
port a very hopeless case is the reply of the
TvjavxT* r^'HK Bulletin’s strictures on the
£i •i * kabor Party for its subservience to the
helpless, drifting, squandering See-Owe’Sullivan
Government:—
There is one general excuse to be made for the Labor
Party’s support of an extravagant Government—it was
necessar3' in the great national crisis through which we
have at length happily passed that something should be
done to stave off a national calamity. Only three courses
were open to the Government: (1) To let the poor and
workless starve ; (2) To feed the poor and workless on
the bread of charity ; (3) To find work with borrowed
money for the poor and workless. Therecan he no doubt
but what if this were put as a hypothetical case to The
Bulletin, or to any other of the Labor Party’s denouncers,
they would be compelled wholly to admit that the third
course was the only wise one. Now that was the one
taken by the See Government, with the Labor Party’s
general approval. Surely it would have been ridiculous
to substitute for that Government, because of its extrava-
£al?c®> another Ministry which would have been com-
pelled to take an almost similar course.
Here arises again the strange mental disease which
assumes that no public work can possibly be done
except out of loans—that it is almost criminal,
or at least quite revolutionary, to build anything
otherwise than out of loans. If the unemployed
had been maintained without working—simply as
a matter of charity—it seems to be assumed by
the Worker that that might have been done
out of revenue, but when it is proposed that
they should be maintained at about the same
cost on public works, then the only alternative is
“to find work with borrowed money.” The
criminality of building anything except out
of loans asserts itself immediately. The
See-Owe’Sollivan Government, it is true, had
fully £2,000,000 more money to spend last year
than was available in 1899; its revenue was about
£1,500,000 larger than that of 1899, and it had
been relieved by the Australian Government of
about £1,000,000 of postal, telegraphic and
military expenditure ; and as its old age pensions
absorbed about £500,000 it was £2,000,000 to the
good. Then it had every opportunity to increase
the land-tax on large estates, and the income-tax
on large incomes, and to levy an absentee-tax, and
make the local government bodies carry a larger
share of the public responsibilities (as they do in
Britain) and increase their rates. It was quite
possible to find £3,000,000 a year for public works
without borrowing, but the Government was shift-
less and incapable, and the Labor party was
the same, and so the good old loan happened
along as usual. If the Labor party made
honest finance the price of its support it would
probably get its price, but nothing seems to be
further from its intentions. If it couldn’t get its
price it might make a bid for office itself, move
its own vote of censure on the great Loan Govern-
ment, and let a M‘Gowen Ministry (if it couldn’t
attract to itself enough new supporters in the
present Assembly) appeal to the country as a
Ministry that proposed to meet its public works
expenditure out of revenue and be done with the
borrowing disease. Apparently nothing is further
from its intentions than that also, yet it is quite
possible that such a Ministry might come back
with a majority, and then Labor would be free
from the dismal necessity to buy democratic con-
cessions by winking at jobs and extravagances,
and would no longer have to put up with See
because Carruthers is as bad. But the Labor
Earty seems to have decided that it must either
e the dejected tail of a See Loan Government
or the dragging caudal appendage of a Car-
ruthers Loan Government, and that is a good
frame of mind to get out of.
N.S.W. Government and tke Citizens’
Life Office.
The papers laid before N.S.W. Assembly in
connection with the lease by the See Government
from the Citizens’ Life Assurance Co. —a pro-
prietary concern with Premier See as chairman of
directors—do not make the position any the better
for the Government, though they reveal some new
and rather curious facts. Percy E. Williams,
Comptroller of the P.O. Savings Bank, in a long
letter, states that the necessity of new accommoda-
tion for the P.O. Savings Bank was apparent to
him before “he was even aware that such a co. as
the Citizens’existed,” which ignorance on his part
the Comptroller trusts the Citizens’ will kindly
pardon, and “that not until after he had sent
the Citizens’ proposal to the Minister for favor-
able consideration was he incidentally made aware
that John See was a member of the directorate of
that company.” There is, of course, nothing
censurable in Percy E. Williams’ ignorance,
but it is at least curious that a bank
manager should not know of the existence of a
large and well-advertised financial institution
which rears its tall head office only a few yards
away from his premises, and that he should be
able to enter into a large business negotation
with a company without finding out even by acci-
dent who were its principal officers. However,
that is not of much importance ; nobody suspects
Premier See of any corrupt motive in the matter.
What is charged against him and against his
Government is a neglect of the public interests,
and an astonishing blindness to the requirements
of political propriety—not to say decency.
One remarkable matter is the statement that,
whilst the Citizens’ Life Co. could put up a
suitable building for Savings Bank purposes in
seven months, it would take two years for the
Government to do it, and it is owing, it seems, to
this astounding incompetence on its own part
that the Government, instead of building on
its own land, is driven to lease, at a huge
rent, the building that the Citizens’ is going
to put up. No word of explanation as to the
cause of the Government’s incompetence in build-
ing matters is offered. Is there any statute in
existence which forbids the See Government being
even half as prompt as a private co. in building
operations ? It seems there is, for it is accepted
as quite a fixed and unquestionable fact that the
Government must take two years in doing what
someone else can do in seven months. Another
faot, which has not been hitherto published, is
that the Savings Bank is not to have the ivhole of
the building erected by the Citizens’ Life Co. Two
floors of it are to be reserved for the co. But the
Government is to pay rent on a 5 per cent, basis
for all of the land at £3OO per foot, and for as
much of the building as costs £13,000 —which may
be the whole of it. There actually was one public
servant who saw the transaction in the right
light, and had the courage to point out the flaw
in it. J. Davis, Under-Secretary for Public
Works, wrote:
Assuming that the value of the land fronting.the Post-
office is £6OO per foot, amounting to £51*003, then the
interest at present being paid at 4 per cent, is £2040. If
this be added to the proposed rent to be paid the Citizens’
Life Assurance Company, £2OOO, the amount involved
will be £4040. On the other hand, supposing a £13,000
building were erected on the block of land opposite the
Post-office, then the interest at 4 per cent, on the build-
ing and land would amount to £2560, or a difference in
favor of the Post-office site of £l4BO per annum.
Assuming, however, that the value of the land in
Martin-place is the same as that in Moore-street owned
by the Citizens’ Life Assurance Company, £3OO per foot,
although in the former case it has a less depth than in
the latter, then the position would be as follows :
Cost of building .. £13,000
Value of land 25,500
Interest at 4 per cent, per annum .. .. 1,540
As against the Citizens’ Life Assurance C 0... 2,000
That, of course, is the position, from an economi-
cal point of view, in a nutshell. The Government,
through not using its own land, but allowing it to
be idle, is losing 4 per cent, at least on its own
value ; by utilising it for a Savings Bank that loss
would be saved. But even omitting that con-
sideration, the action of the See Government in
coming to a bargain with the See assurance com-
pany is a most improper one. Even if the bargain
were a good one, which it is not, the transaction
would be censurable. The Assembly has to come
to a decision on that point this (Thursday) even-
ing, and if it does its duty will certainly insist on
the repudiation of the contract, irrespective of
consequences. It is already forecasted, however,
that there will not be any such development.
The motion reads:—
That this House disapprove of the agreement entered
into by the Treasurer with the Citizens’ Life Assurance
Company.
And it is rumored that the Labor party will move,
quite irrelevantly, that the P.O. Savings Bank and
the Barrack-street Savings Bank should be amal-
gamated; and that under cover of that amendment
a vote on the direct issue will be shirked. Such a
conclusion to the matter would involve an astonish-
ing display of political agility, but nothing is
impossible to the N.S.W. Assembly or to any
party in it. If, however, it is desired to avoid a
direct vote on the matter, the joke might be made
a little more obvious for the sake of a public which
is sometimes rather obtuse. An amendment, for
instance, expressing the House’s detestation of the
Balkan massacres would be just as pertinent to
the issue.

A Remarkable Claim.
A remarkable-looking claim was that of the
Howard Smith Co., Limited, shipowners, for
£250,000 as compensation for the resump-
tion by the Government of two wharves in
Sydney. (The Government offered £35,250.)
The case looked remarkable in many ways,
but chiefly in the . light of the company’s
balance-sheet. That dooument (dated 31st
December, 1902) declared to the shareholders
that the value of all the company’s freehold and
leasehold property in all parts of Australia, and
anywhere else where it possesses property, was
£43,901, and the balance-sheet containing these
values was sworn to, under the Victorian law,
which makes a false declaration punishable as
wilful and corrupt perjury, to be correct to the
best of the manager’s knowledge and belief. On
the face of things it seems difficult to suppose
that the manager should imagine the value of all
the co. ’s freehold and leasehold property to be only
£43,901 when its wharves in one city alone were
worth nearly six times that amount, therefore the
matter seems to call for some explanation. The
company’s whole paid-up capital, at last Decem-
ber, was <£386,245, of which £249,521 was invested
in “ steamers and plant,” so everything it owned,
apart from steamers and plant, doesn’t seem to even
approach the alleged value of these two wharves. It
would be specially interesting to know what land-
tax or municipal tax was paid on this immensely
valuable Sydney property. Eor the sake of its
own credit the company should throw some light
on the situation in its next balance-sheet, and thus
clear itself of any possible suspicion of attempting
to make a wholly unjustifiable profit out of the
State. It is a great deal more objectionable to
try to make an unjustifiable profit out of 1,400,000
people all at once than to try to do, t(|e same out
of only one person—which is the reason why ex-
planation is called for. If any person or corpora-
tion tried to get from the State, say, £200,000
more than it was entitled to, and succeeded, then
the writer’s share of the loss, for self and family,
would be about £1 ss. That is sufficient reason
for inquiry on the writer’s part, and on the part
of every other citizen.

Victorian Old Age Pensions.
The Victorian Government’s Old Age Pension
Bill is worthy of it. The maximum pension is to
be cut down to 7s. a week—the pension may,
however, be any less amount. The little dole is
now assumed to be a loan, and the pensioner
can be sold up to recover the money. The
total amount spent on pensions is not to
exceed £150,000 a year, and only one new pension
is to be granted for every two old pensions can-
celled by death or otherwise, till this limit is
reached. The claimant is now required to show,
not merely that he is unable to maintain himself,
but that he is ‘‘incapable of maintaining himself,”
which is quite a different thing. There are very
many persons of 65. and even 70, who are quite
capable of doing work to the value of Bs. or 10s. a
week if they could get the work to do; the reason
why they can’t get it to do is that the average
employer doesn’t want four or five venerables
pottering round at 10s. each to do, among
them, the work that one young man will
do for £2 or 50s. The State sets this kind
of employer an example, in many cases, by
compulsorily retiring men at 65 (provided they
have no compensation rights) whether they are
fit for work or not, and it is just that tendency
to get the venerable out of the way that makes
the trouble. Irvine’s Bill sets up the theory
that when the State throws a man out for being
old he isn’t entitled to a pension because he
might have been able to go on working if the
State had allowed him to do so—-which it didn’t.
The new measure further provides that nobody
with more than £lO worth of assets is to receive a
pension. Also all manner of relatives are held
liable for the support of their aged connections,
and financial institutions may be compelled to
supply information about the positions of those
relatives and their ability to pay. This is almost
the only redeeming feature—indirectly redeeming
feature, that is—in the case. Quite a number of
very high-toned persons have hitherto dodged the
duty of supporting aged uncles and other family
side-issues, and the possible spectacle of Premier
Irvine calling at the National Bank with a lump
of ice in his hand, and demanding to see the state
of the overdraft of Blank, M.L.C., who has failed
to aid in supporting his first wife’s brother, has
something to recommend it.

Mr. Spruce Myth’s Nigger.
The beautifully-dressed figure of Spruce Myth
looms up largely nowadays among the crowd of
Tories who are trying to lead the newly-enfran-
chised Australian woman astray. The following
is from a recent address whioh that well-tailored
person delivered at Burwood (N.S.W.), and ex-
hibits Mr. Myth at his best
Legislation had been passed which was an insult to the
Japanese people. When the matter came before Parlia-
ment he found he was the only man brave enough to
stand up for the Japanese, and, if he lost his seat through
it, he would continue to advocate his views, not only with
regard to the Japanese, but other races as well. When a
man became a citizen of an Empire he should be able to
go to any part of that Empire, and he had no sympathy
with legislation of the “six hatters” description. He
advised them not to be led away with talk of the negro in
the United States. A similar danger did not exist here,
where the colored races were coming and going continu-
ally. In the Northern Territory even, there was nothing
in the state of affairs to cause them to stand up and pass
measures of an hysterical and ridiculous description, and
make themselves the laughing stock of the world.
Apart from the fact that the overbearing
Japanese bounder started the insult business by
enacting that no Britisher or other foreigner was
good enough to hold an inch of land in his coun-
try (probably Spruce Myth rather likes being
trodden on by brown heathens), these questions
are worth asking :
(1) How does Mr. Myth know that, if we admit colored
men freely, no nigger problem can possibly arise here
such as exists in the United States ? It takes an immense
amount of foresight to know that, and Mr. Myth hadn’t
even enough foresight to know that a certain land bank
was going to burst. (The fact that he showed great
pluck and honesty in facing the financial consequences
makes no difference as regards the lack of foresight.)
_ (2) Where is it laid down that when a man becomes a
citizen of an empire he should have the free run of that
empire ? Writer has hunted all through the Scripture,
and can’t find it.
(3) When did the Hindoo and all the other niggers
become full-blown citizens of the empire, seeing that they
are, in their own country, debarred from the franchise,
declared unfit to enjoy self-governing privileges, and
the alleged British principle that taxation and represen-
tation go together is suspended in their case ? If Britain,
which knows these niggers so much better than we do,
declares them unfit to enjoy the privileges of citizenship,
who is Myth that he should contradict ?
This last question is one that The Bulletin has
laid before many prominent nigger advocates, and
the issue has been meanly shirked by all of them.
The Bulletin now makes Spruce Myth a straight-
out offer. If Britain will put its Indian nigger
on the same level as the Englishman, make
him a full-blown citizen, acknowledge him as
an equal, give him, in proportion to his
numbers, the same right to help to govern
the empire that the Englishman enjoys—in
fact, will decide that he is the same as any
other Britisher —then The Bulletin will vote for
his free admission to Australia. Meanwhile, it is
willing to join with Spruce Myth and George
Reid in remonstrating with Britain on its vil-
lainous conduct in shutting out the Hindoo from
so many of the blessed privileges of British citizen-
ship. The fact that the best-educated native of
India is debarred, in his own country, from poli-
tical rights enjoyed by an English agricultural
laborer should make Spruce Myth’s blood boil,
and should almost make him turn Boer.
