The Religion of an Tipper House.
A Stawell (Vic.) storekeeper died recently,
leaving his property (£583) to an acquaintance,
and a balance of an odd shilling to his widow.
That proceeding is quite legal under the fool
law which the Victorian House of 48 Rich
Landlords has just flatly refused to amend. A Bill,
was submitted to it providing that a widow should
have a claim on a certain proportion of her hus-
band’s estate, no matter how his will ran, and the
House of Landlords decided that the idea repre-
sented a gross outrage on Property, and cast the
measure out with indignation on its reddened.coun-
tenanee. The Stawell storekeeper, be it noted, had
no power to cut off his wife with Is. while he was-
alive; she had a good and sufficient claim against
the live man’s estate. But the foolish law
gives the dead man privileges that it never dreams
of giving to a man before he is dead. At times,
of course, a man who .cuts off his widow with Is.
may have a legitimate grievance against her, but;
even that doesn’t seem to materially affect the-
case. The public probably has to support the old
lady who has been disinherited, and so it pays for
the deceased’s grievance, and there is no reason,
why it should pay for the grievance of any can-
tankerous old person. If she was a bad wife the
public has still quite enough to do with its money
without supporting anybody’s bad widow merely
on account of her badness. The Upper House,
which refused to amend this law, is, curiously'
enough, a very religious body—a certain kind,
of religious. It believes that the wicked and.
revengeful man will be fried in the next;
world, and the bitterly unforgiving man who-
dies in the hope that his widow, whether
good or bad, will starve when he is gone, will,
certainly fry something considerable according to-
the theology of these hard old M’s.L.C. Yet the-
proposal to prevent him carrying this extra sin
with him when he goes to his long gridiron didn’t
appeal to the House of Landlords. In its view,
the right to be bitterly revengeful to the last, and',
to die in sin, is a sacred right. The Bulletin
somehow doesn’t envy these stony old gentle-
men their religion. It seems to be one that any
decent citizen would prefer to be without.
*
The Partial Caving-in of the
Landlords.
The Victorian House of 48 Rich Landlords, afer
its outburst of blind fury against the Factouies.
Bill, finally listened to a certain amount of reasom
—or was terrorised into a semblance of doing so..
The papers had hardly announced that the minimurai
wage clause had been cut out as applied to adults
male workers before the Anti-Sweating League’
and the Trades Hall Council moved. Sunday
saw a huge crowd throng into Fitzgeralds’ Circus;
building to listen to well-known citizens denouncing:
the obstructionists in good set terms. And as the-
beefy ancients trammed up Collins-street on.
Tuesday evening to the House, where the Bilk
was to be re-committed, they saw another throng,,
that would fill the building three times over,
hammering at the doors of the Town Hall in the-
vain hope of joining the packed thousands inside
who were even then urging Peacock and
M‘Kinnon to “give it them straight” and “let
themselves go.” It then probably dawned on-,
the Incapables that, compared to these thou-
sands, the Employers’ Federation was worse-
than a broken reed and the Sweater a thing.
of naught; and that possibly a few hun-
dreds—or even the overflow thousands —might
take it into their heads to walk into the sacred
Council Chamber itself and argue with them per-
sonally. Also, in the interim, Ministers in the
Council had rounded up certain Landlords who
had been absent on the former occasion, and told
them plainly that their House was kicking
against a wall, and that it was a wall that was
always liable to fall upon the kicker. Anyhow,
before the Town Hall meeting was over, the Land-
lords had crawled down, and though an important
point or two was yielded by the Assembly at the
subsequent conference, the existing boards remain
and the Act was passed.. In some respects, it is
an improvement on Turner’s first Act; in others
—where the Council interfered—a considerable
retrogression. A simple majority, instead of
seven-tenths, now fixes a Wages Board de-
termination ; witnesses may be compelled to
f'ive evidence on oath ; apprentices are un-
imited, but must be indentured for three years
and have their wages fixed by the Boards; all
other “infants” are improvers, whose numbers
are limited and wages fixed ; an Appeal Court —
consisting of a Supreme Court judge and two
assessors—may revise Wages Board decisions.
This Court must not fix a minimum wage higher
than the average wage paid by “ reputable ”
(whoever they are) employers, and must not fix
a wage or determine hours or do anything else
that may injure an industry, but must in no case
substitute (for the Board determination) a rate
below a living wage. Wages Boards may fix
piece-work rates that will scotch, if not kill,
the “task” system (whereby an extra fast
worker is put on wages and others paid at
piece-work rates based on the fast man’s
output). Children of employers are exempt
(while in their parents’ factories) from mini-
mum wage provisions. The Act applies to
industries within 10 miles of cities and towns,
and elsewhere in the State if municipal councils
petition. The worst amendment is that new
Wages Boards may only be appointed on resolution
of both Houses. The laundries and the furniture
trades still come under the Act, though Chinese
are not specially legislated for. In other respects
the Act is practically Turner’s first measure over
again. It isn’t what it might be by a good deal,
but then again, it isn’t what the 48 Rich Landlords
and the Employers’ Federation would like, by a
good deal more ; also, it is standing evidence that
the 48 Tories are as timid as they are heartless;
and that it only wants a really strong man to
dragoon them into Submission on almost any
point. The new Act expires on December 31st,
1905, and when that day arrives it is to be hoped
the man will have arrived, too; or that, by
Federal action, there will be no need for his
advent.

The Unstable Mining - Industry.
The most interesting and important amendment
so far proposed in the Mines Bill, now before the
Vic. Assembly, comes from Alf. Bailes, one of
the Bendigo members, who wants to insist upon
every No Liability co. building up a reserve fund.
Bailes’ idea is that 2| per cent, of the amount of
every proposed dividend shall be put to the fund
until it is equal to 5 per cent, of the subscribed
capital, and that every director who takes part in
declaring or pacing a dividend without complying
with this provision shall be personally liable for
the reserve-fund contribution. The principle the
Bendigo man seeks to establish is sound to the
core, but all his figures are too insignificant. The
statutory contribution ought to be 10 f>6,r cent.,
and the contributions should not cease till at
least 20 per cent, of the subscribed capital is
available. In fact, there is no particularly
good reason why there should be any limit at all.
Further, the fund ought to be invested in
some public security; if there isn’t some
such provision the money will be “invested
in the business”; that is to say, it will be put
into a crab-winch, or a multi-tubular boiler, ora
patent stone-cracker, or something of the kind,
which the c-o. would have required in any case,
so that, after all, there will be no money available
with which to explore new country and carry on
operations when the payable run is worked out.
One of the Moon group of mines, for a couple of
years past the most profitable in the State, lately
began to put by a little, and the directors
admitted quite cheerfully that that was just how
they proposed to “invest” it—in machinery.
Mining always will be a good deal of a
gamble; even Bailes’ proposal, improved as
suggested, can’t put a payable reef into a wild cat.
Still a whole lot of other undertakings would be
gambles, too, if they were run in the same fashion.
The insurance company which, not having had to
pay many claims in the year, declared a bonus
big enough to swallow up the whole of the surplus
premiums, would get on its uppers quite as
rapidly as most mining companies do when they
pursue just that sort of idiotic policy. Down
to now the State has done a good deal to make
mining a gamble instead of an industry. It has,
for example, provided a No Liability Act, under
which the shareholder, even though he has taken
up a £1 share, is entitled to drop it at any moment,
notwithstanding that only eighteenpence has been
actually paid, and thus leave all the other share-
holders in the lurch. The State has done
this for the crowd that looks on a mine in
much the same way that another crowd (which is
to a great extent the same crowd over again) looks
on a racehorse—as something to hang a bet on to.
Now, it is about up to the State to do something
for the miner—the man to whom a mine is the
basis of an industry, and not a mere counter in a
gamble. That man’s curse is the uncertainty of
his occupation. The mine that can employ-
hundreds to-day, and scatter thousands in divi-
dends, is either shut down to-morrow or is able to
pay only a handful of men out of the calls that
are dragged from reluctant shareholders. One
result is that'the miner is a rover. In nearly
every case he lives in discomfort. His home is a
temporary affair —he dare not make it otherwise.
He suffers, of course; but his wife and children
suffer still more. And that wife and those children
are calling loudly to the Victorian Assembly to do
this thing—this little thing that is within its
?ower —not to give stability to the industry (for
arliament cannot do that), but to make it less
unstable.

Mr. W. Young, New South Wales M.L.A. for
Bathurst —the place that no one voted for as the
Federal capital—froths at the mouth over the
fact: —
It was for this State to assert its rights and lay down
the situation for the Federal capital and tell the Federal
Parliament that they insisted on them taking the one
■chosen. If the Federal Parliament did not comply with
the proposal and accept the site laid down he would have
no hesitation in declaring that this-State should burst up
the union at the first opportunity. The proposal he
made was that they should offer the Federal Parliament
the Lyndhurst site—the only site in the State carrying
with it commercial and social benefits.
Lyndhurst is the site at Bathurst’s back-door.

Sydney’s craving for something new will
be pacified on Saturday by the production at
Her Majesty’s of “A Country Girl ” for the first
time in Australia.. The comic opera show is to
be turned on in full force, and part of
Williamson’s musical comedy co. has been
borrowed to strengthen the cast. Therefore
Sydney expects to see something large and
vigorous on Saturday, and it is buying its ticket
in a lavish spirit. It is on occasions like this that
the public realises how vast an opening there is
for that prospective genius who will devise
a compact theatre that will hold 3000 people,
and where every d.c. seat will be a front
Seat, and every stall seat will be close
up to the orchestra, and where there will be
six boxes, all on the ground floor close alongside
the stage; and the gallery, and the family oircle
and the d.c. will also be on the ground floor along
with the stalls, each of them in front, so as to com-
mand an uninterrupted view. A theatre built on
such lines, would almost certainly be popular,
especially if it had a first night every day, and the
idea is worth the consideration of inventors. Un-
happily, it can’t be worked out in time for the
“Country Girl” show, but the production pro-
mises to struggle along pretty well on the old
lines.

Miss Ada Crossley will appear in “Elijah ”
with the Philharmonic Society to-inorrow (Friday)
night, at Sydney Town Hall. Every seat, except
a cobwebbed one in the corner of the hall, is taken.
The contralto sings the “Angel” r6le; basso Peter-
kin trumpets for the prophet Elijah. Everyone
looks forward to hearing Miss Crossley’s rendering
of “O, Rest in the Lord,” as to-morrow night’s
appearance will absolutely be her last Australian
showing in oratorio.

At Sydney Lyceum “ Her Second Time on
Earth ” still rages, and by a careful attention to
details—such as providing a real cab with areal
horse for the bridegroom to drive up to church,
and a real brougham with tivo real white horses to
bring the bride —impresses big audience? with its
abounding merits. The quality of the horse-flesh
on the Lyceum stage is always good, and the
people judge the whole of the show by that stan-
dard and are profoundly satisfied. Perhaps, too,
there are worse standards.
With a couple of changes in the cast,
“The French Spy ” has been a more satisfactory
performance at Sydney Palace this week than it
was last —and last week it was a very good per-
formance in places, and especially in the places in
which Miss Fitzmaurice Gill held most of the
stage. A change will be made at the end of this
week, when “ The Bank of England ” will be put
on, presumably with the object of showing that
there’s quite a lot of money in the world that
Australia hasn’t borrowed yet.

After Friday night’s performance at
Sydney Criterion, Charles Holloway will put
away Father Dolan’s clothes where the moths
cannot get at them, and the Shaughraun will give

man’s tea. On the next night “Two Little

extra-special sensation. Still, “ Two Little Vaga-
bonds” is worth reviving in any case, for it was
the best thing the co. has done, and the vagabonds
are a pair of deeply-interesting youngsters, any-
how.

“The Great Millionaire,” which is Bland
Holts latest venture in Drury Lane drama at
Melbourne Royal, has all the virtues and faults of
‘ The Price of Peace, ’ and other plays composed
by Cecil Raleigh. In certain places it is better
than the best, or worse than the worst, of Raleigh’s
previous work. The attempts at character draw-
ing are more ambitious, the incidents are more
difficult to present in a convincing way, the plot
is more broken, the demands upon the actors are
more severe, and the necessity for an extra
stage-manager is more obvious than ever. Until
Bland Holt meets the difficulty by making* special
engagements for these exacting Raleigh dramas,
apd imports a stage manager who understands
the working of all their points, he will continue
to get more trouble than profit by them. “The
Great Millionaire,” as produced on Saturday,
merely offers suggestions of the show it is intended
to be. It amounts to an expensive failure; far
preferable to an ordinary low-down popular melo-
drama, but a failure, all the same, from a critical
point of view. Even the plot of the story was
not effectively revealed on the first night.
* * * *
The Millionaire is seemingly meant for a
ha,rd, elderly, combative speculator, a callous
misanthropic brute on one side of his character,
and something of a kindly gentleman on the
other. As a landowner he makes himself a terror
to trespassers by undermining his precious park
with fireworks and things which “go off” sud-
denly when walked upon or sat upon by poachers
and picnickers. Subsequently he takes a fancy to
a busted lordling and pays him an enormous
retaining fee, in return for which the young man
follows his patron around London and condemns
his actions in a quiet sort of way. They go to-
gether to the Guildhall, where the Millionaire, in
some official capacity or other, is prepared to
grovel to King Edward, but before the King
arrives the Millionaire is denounced for sharp
practices as a company promoter, and, being
shooed off the premises, he vows vengeance on
Great Britain. This audaciously original scene
terminates with the royal entrance of an absurd
Edward and Alexandra, the stage lined by a lot
of comic opera peers in flowing robes. The
Millionaire’s vengeance turns out to be a bread
famine. He works a corner in wheat, and
raises the price of the quartern loaf to five
“bob,” whereupon the hungry toilers are
moved to a feeble riot, and poor Miss Ross
is overcome by starvation. All this time the
Millionaire has been looking for a long-lost
daughter, and when he runs up against the Bread
Riot he discovers that Miss Ross, fainting for a
loaf, is the child he loves. Eventually she dies, a
victimised heiress to all his millions ; but in the
meantime his secretary bolts with his private
cipher-book, and the remorseful parent is
threatened with ruin because a rival mil-
lionaire intends to hoist him with his
own cipher. At this crisis the retained
lordling chases the absconded secretary. It
is a motor-car race between the pair, and the
sec.’s motor runs over a precipice. Many other
remarkable incidents, more or less probable, but
violently original as a rule, are held over for
future comment. Fifty-three characters figure on
the programme of “The Great Millionaire.”
Walter Baker plays the name part powerfully, up
to the point where he discovers his daughter, and
the other members of Bland Holt’s stock co. fill
less responsible positions. In some cases, the
acting is not equal to the character, but it can
have a week for improvement ere this paper passes
severe sentence upon it. Scenery by Brunton
reflects some more of the usual credit on that
respected identity.
** * *
“Sweet Nell” at Melbourne Princess’s
promised on Saturday to crowd the house every
evening for a week. With one exception the
people who support Nellie Stewart and her nut-
brown King are the same who appeared in the
previous revival. Harry Hill still plays the roar-
ful Jeffries with less roar than Gran put into
the part, and Vernon Steele is the same
tall and likely young Roger Fairfax as before
with an undiminished foreign accent. It is
said to be a Mexican accent, and as such it
seems to need careful medical treatment to
prevent it becoming chronic. On Saturday next
the Only Nellie will make her first Melbourne
appearance as Peg Woffington, the actress who
substituted her real face (in the play) for an oil
painting, and was mistaken for the picture. Miss
Nellie has already impersonated three various
actresses —Gwynne, Zaza and Dolores—with
happy results, therefore Woffington is expected to
increase her reputation by at least one Peg.
** # *
Mel. B. Spurr, the monologue entertainer
from London, took the floor at Melbourne
Athenaeum Hall on Derby night, and showed just
cause for coming a long distance with a one-man
show. The new-comer, who in his youth sat at
the feet of Corney Grain, is an efficient laugh-
maker, with a dainty touch on the piano, a poor
singing voice, and a rich repertoire of low-comedy
grimaces. In the department of mimicry, Mel.
B.’s work comes under the head of burlesque
rather than imitation. • His old general, half-pay
admiral, Mam'zelle at the fancy fair, etc., are
laughable impersonations, true to stereotyped
English stage traditions of military, naval,
and Parisian character. But for the pre-
sent, Australia is’'fortunately unable to appre-
ciate fully the monologuer’s caricatures of
Generals, Admirals, haw-haw chappies, or pomp-
ous M.P.’s prompted in their speeches by a
private secretary. Of such as these she knows
little and cares less. Mr. Spurr, roaming this un-
hackneyed Commonwealth, might do worse than
pick up some new models from the ranks of
suburban aldermen and wild-cat politicians. ’ The
programme, mostly humorous, gains an agreeable
spice of variety from occasional lapses into sadness
and cynicism. One of these, a recitation, styled
“The Game of Life,” deals with the four “suits,”
love, wealth, war and death, and the speaker softly
plays appropriate music on his zither, knelling
forth the “ Dead March ” in unison with the fall-
ing of spades. The Spurr show is for everyone
who wants a laugh and can admire the pluck and
go of one small artistic man in singing, playing,
moralising and speechifying, and generally keep-
ing the ball rolling on his own for a whole
evening.
Fitzgerald Bros’, circus continues to cirque
in its largest and most complete manner, and the
local horse festival feels grateful for the presence
of brothers Tom and Dan. Next Saturday night,
bv which time the personal popularity of Horse
will be waning somewhat, is to introduce Doctor
Gordon' to the arena. The Doc. is another
Sandow in a different kind of way. The muscles
of his tongue are splendidly developed, and he
talks freely whilst performing feats of strength in
a swallow-tailed coat and a hard-boiled shirt.

“ Are You a Mason ? ” holds the stage at
Adelaide Royal without any opposition, and has
had crowded houses, in spite of some early hot
weather. George Giddens has been accepted at
once as a first-rate comedian of the quiet type,
but much regret is expressed at the absence of
Cecil Ward, who is a great favorite in Adelaide,
and whose likeness has been freely billed in
various scenes from the play. George Majeroni
makes a fair substitute. The ladies are all good.
The Wild Cat Column.
People who don’t want Australia to think that
it is its own bad habits that have landed it in trouble,
are telling it to just wait till "the wave of adversity”
has rolled by, and it can start producing up to its old
standard again. And the fact is that there is no wave of
adversity in the aggregate, and it is producing up to its
old standard now. Of course there have been wheat and
wool disasters, but there is another Bide to the picture.
This year the Commonwealth will produce something
like £16,000,000 worth of gold. (Last year it produced
£14,811,991 worth, and in the first nine months of this
year the output was 349,8370 z. ahead of what it was to
Sept. 30, 1902.) Even last year’s figure was half-a-million
higher than the giddiest heights reached in the giddiest
year of the fifties. Comparing the figures of the past five
years with those of 10 years back we get these results: —
1889 .. .. £5,736,355 1899 .. .. £14,661,258
1890 .. .. 6,231,466 1900 .. .. 13,701,876
1891 .. .. 5,272,894 1901 .. .. 14,189,978
1892 .. .. 5,887,300 1902 .. .. 14,811,991
1893 .. .. 6,215,472 1903 (approx.) 16,000,000
Total ..£28,343,487 Total .. £73,365,098
The figures even for 1889-1893 were considerably above
the average for many years previously. In the last five
years, then, even if seasons have been on the whole in-
different, Australia has torn out of the ground £45,021,611
more gold than it did in the corresponding part of the
previous decade ; and that ought to be a pretty substan-
tial set-off against the reduction in wool and wheat. In
fact, so splendidly did the gold yield come to the rescue
that the value of all Australia’s products per head was
more in 1901, the drought year, than in 1891, the boom
year. So there was no wave of adversity on the
country, as a whole, to account for our troubles.
The wool and wheat business will get better, of
course, and the gold business will get worse, of course,
gold being a product which doesn’t renew itself each
year. But the lost boom won’t come back by mere
effluxion of time, for we can’t spend the borrowed money
over again, or sell the lands over again. A total produc-
tion (wool, wheat, gold, etc,) of £3O 13s. 6d. per head
meant great prosperity in 1891; an average production
of £3O 16s. per head meant dismal poverty in 1901,
because the best of the land had been sold in the interim,
and there was less loan money coming in and more
interest going out, and the I O U was coming home to
roost. And if the loan policy continues, an average
production of £3l per head will probably mean starva-
tion in 1911.

The S. Australian Treasurer’s loan estimates
“provide for an expenditure of £759,583 during the cur-
rent year." For a handful of 365,000 people who are sup-
posed to have, to a great extent, reformed and given up
borrowing this is modest, being equal to only about
£2 Is. 6d. per head of the population. To keep up to
that N.S.W. would have to borrow at the rate of
£2,850,000 a year. So far Queensland has had the Biggest
Debt per Head on Earth; but, with the northern
Treasurer sitting down hard on borrowing, it looks as
though the dishonor will pass to S.A. pretty soon. At
June 30,1902, the positions were
Queensland .. .. £76 8 6 per head.
South Australia .. 75 210 ~
Queensland’s borrowings have since then been slightly
the heavier, but if it knocks off now, while Jenkins
Iscariot’s State goes ahead, the latter—the poorest of
all the States in natural resources—will have got to the
top and be the holder of a world’s record by the end of
June, 1904. „ ,

The reserves consist of ordinary reserve fund £65,000,

dividends £7OOO, for the depreciation of investments
£lO 000, and undivided balance £14,256. The objection-
able reserve for the equalisation of dividends has again
been added to. It doesn’t matter what this money is
called • the fact remains that the directors apparently
contemplate some day paying a dividend, not
out of current profits, . but out of reserves.
A reserve for equalisation of dividends is chiefly useful to
make a business look presentable long enough for the
people inside to get out, supposing it should some day
start to decay ; and the United Insurance, not being a
concern of that kind, has no use at all for a fund for such
a purpose, and it should not set a bad example to other
less respectable concerns. The co’s paid capital is
£68,226 ; on that and the reserves as at the opening of

per cent, per annum. The interest and rents alone
(£5171) were equal to paying a dividend of 7* per cent.,

Brisbane Telegraph is the last paper to get
hold of the remarkable idea that there are capitalists
hovering round eager to snap up Australian railways at
the price of the national debt. “ The annual loss on our
(Queensland) railways,” says the Telegraph. “is about
half-a-million. Why keep such a losing concern l A
company would readily be found to take over the rai -
ways as a going concern at the price of our national
debt.” This idea, that the railways are good and nego-
tiable security for the whole of our debt, is encountered
very frequently, but it is never backed up by anything
better than a majestic sweep of the arm. And companies
with £40,000,000 or so to invest don’t regard lordly sweeps
as assets of much consequence ; they want to get down
to facts and figures. And here are a few to go on with.
The Queensland national debt was, on June 30, iskw,
£39,338,427— say 40 millions. A co., working on busi-
ness lines, would want at least 4 per cent. Also,
the money being invested in things that rot ana
rust, it would want to put aside at least
2.V per cent, to cover depreciation, renewals, etc. Die
allowance would probably have to be greater, hut it can
be left at that. The 6* per cent, would call for £2,600,0uu
over working expenses. But the gross receipts last year
were only £1,234,230. If, therefore, two tons of goods
could be carried for every ton carried now, and two
passengers could be crowded in where one is carried
now, and if the whole staff worked for nothing at all.
and nobody charged anything for coal or oil; or if
everybody paid the company twice as much as
he pays the State, and the company paid
nobody anything—then such a company as Brisbane
Telegraph talks about would be possible. In anv
other circumstances, it would not he possible. And
probably the Telegraph’s company would not take the
Telegraph's guarantee as to any of these matters it
would want the State’s; pretty nearly every State which
has handed over its railway affairs to companies has, in
fact, had to give guarantees of some kind or other; atm
that system has simply meant that when there were
profits the co. has got them, but when there were losses
the State has had to hump them. British com pan les that
made railways in South America generally diddled these
luckless Latin republics out of 6 or 7 per cent, guaran tee..
And the State can hump the losses quite as well without
making any agreement to let someone else have tne
profits. , , , .

The merits of the Wilfley Ore Concentrator
are now fully recognised throughout the Commonwealth,
and its use is extending every day. The new No. 4
pattern is doing excellent work at the many mines on
which it has been installed. Recent additions to mining
plants comprise ; 4 tables to the Great Fingall, 6 to the
Oroya Brown Hiil, 4 to the Ivanhoe Company, 4 to the
Sons of Gwalia, 3 to the Queen’s Cross Coy., Charters
Towers, 3 to the Long Tunnel Extended, 1 to the South
Moon, Bendigo, and many other mines too numerous to
mention. Upwards of 600 of the Wilfley Tables are now
in use in Australia, giving excellent results on gold,
silver, tin, and copper mines. Full particulars and a
useful pamphlet on concentration can be obtained from
the Sole Manufacturers in Australia, The Austral Otis
Engineering Coy., South Melbourne.*

British manufacturers have been beaten in an
open competition by an Australian engineering firm. We
have ascertained that the Mond Gas Co. of Staffordshire
have accepted the tender of Mephan Ferguson, of Mel-
bourne, for the supply of about 13 miles of Ferguson’s
Patent Lockingbar Steel Pipes, from 18 inches to 36 inches
diameter. The machinery for manufacturing these pipes
has all been made at Ferguson’s General Engineering
Works, Footscray, and shipped to Tipton, in Staffordshire,
where the branch works are being erected. The same
firm have also erected engineering and pipe works at
Wanganui, New Zealand, and have contracted to supply
the Wanganui Borough Council with about 20 miles of
Spiral Riveted Steel Pipes for the town water supply.*

“H.S.R.”: Re the Australian explorer from
Glasgow referred to in Bulletin recently, I may say that
gems of a sort have been got for years in Queensland right
up to the Cloncurry. As a rule, they are of little of no-
value. For instance, lots of what is called sapphire will
not take a polish as it is too slatey, and will not what is
professionally termed “ top.” It is corundum. Same
with ruby. Zircon and opal have been found also. The
reason why we have those “ explorers ” coming out here
discovering mares’-nests and exploring country that has
been in occupation for nearly-a century,-Is probably the
ignorance shown by the Australian press about Aus-
tralia. In the U.K. they consequently fariby that we
spend our time leaning against posts, or cutting each
other’s hair, whilst our wives take in jach others wash-
ing. Hence God’s Englishman, and the Devil’s Irishman,
and the unspeakable Scot come to see if we are still alive.
In the meantime Jimbill has wheeled a barrow hundreds
of miles and explored the country all the time he was
supposed to be keeping the post up waiting for Cohen to
come to the rescue.

In order to reduce the liabilities to depositors by £4384,
the item, “ advanced shares, properties in possession,
&c.”, was reduced by £4BBO. Presumably, therefore,
the writing-down has not gone far enough.
If the transactions of the year could be taken
as a guide, then something like £lO,OOO or £ll,OOO
have still to be written off before the market value
is reached. Still, a single year’s transactions are
not too safe a guide. Twelve months ago it was
announced that the society was about to return to active
business, but there were unseen difficulties in the way,
and the prod'gal isn’t quite back yet. Prospects are,
however, at least fair for it when it does make a start;
its business profits in the past year were equal to 11J per
cent, of its nominally intact capital. Certainly it is
helped to this result by getting most of its deposits at
something less than the current rate; yet, even if its
deposits carried 1 per cent, more, the business profits
would bo equal to 8 per cent. ; and even assets that are
not reckoned to be depreciated cannot always do as well
as that. In spite of all this, however, the deposits are
out of all safe proportion to the capital. \
» * * »
Here are some of the things that happened
while Thomas Bent was managing the Victorian railways
last year, according to the just-issued report:—
The receipts fell £320,985 as compared with the pre-
vious year.
It cost more per train mile to run the ricketty engines
over ricketty lines with a disorganised staff than in any
year since 1887.
The expenditure mopped up a greater percentage of
the gross receipts than in any other year since 1892.
The earnings per cent, on loan capital expended were
less than in any year on record, with one single excep-
tion.
The earnings per mile open have only once been lower
in 32 years.
The deficit per cent, on capital cost was more than
twice as great as the average of the three preceding
years.
In the past 32 years the working costs in proportion
to receipts have only four times been greater.
In all their history the Victorian railways have only
once earned less in proportion to capital cost, and that
was just after the bank smashes.
The receipts per train mile run were greater than in
any year from 1887 to 1900 ; yet there was only a single
year during that period when the net earnings were
less in proportion to cost.
The report furnishes a lot of other interesting proofs of
Bent’s fine capacity as a railway manager ; but these
ought to be enough to get Bent a job anywhere.

1902.. 8338 3913 7£ „ „ 7361 4890 21,321
1903.. 8844 2632 7£ „ „ 7361 4115 25,436
The results don’t make it much more certain than it was
last year that, the increase of dividend was a wise pro-
ceeding. The business profits have certainly increased
very satisfactorily, but the “net profit on property
sales ”is less than usual. The latter is, however, hardly
profit. The value of foreclosed property was written
down out of capital. It happens that it was written
down rather more than was necessary, and the profit on
property sales is merely part of the lost capital recovered.
The so-called profit of £2632 has been made in the past
year out of the sales of properties which were apparently
valued at £4259 ; the amount (gross) actually realised
was £6921. This is remarkably satisfactory realisation.
During the past four years the value of foreclosed pro-
perties has been reduced from £72,683 to £45,935, and
£10,975 net “ profit ” has been obtained ; that is to say,
properties down in the books for £26,748 have been sold
for £37,723 clear. At the same rate of realisation the
balance (£45,935) would bring in about £64,000. Still,
there probably will not be any violent hurry to realise
some of the properties. Their gross average return on
the present book-value is over 1-1 per cent., and even on
the £64,000 basis it is about 8 per cent. The society has
now reduced its deposits to £lo,6sB—they were up to a
giddy £190,000 13 years ago
* * * *
A hard fight which has taken place in Brisbane
shows up the meanness of the Loan policy. The South
Brisbane Council wanted to borrow £20,000, and there
was a referendum on the question of raising it. Three-
pence added to the rates would bring in the whole
£20,000, or very near it ; yet, rather than pay up the
threepence and be done with it for ever, there was all
this row. This “£20,000 loan money can be borrowed
without increasing the rates, as the interest can be pro-
vided for out of the general rate,” ran an apparently
official address to the ratepayers. In other words, what
W'as proposed was to raise £20,000, spend it in increasing
the present value of property, and pass the bill on to some-
body else to pay after the works have been worn out. It
is some consolation that this mean little scheme to dodge
taxes was beaten ; but it wasn’t beaten so badly as its
meanness called for.

_A co. has been registered in Queensland which
offers life and sickness assurance with a pound of tea.

The success of the Adelaide Co-operative
Brewery, run by the “free” hotelkeepers, has set the
same idea going in Sydney ; and the increase in the selling
price of beer in Melbourne has given it a start there also.
An Adelaide man has been whipping up the Sydney
Bungs, and it is said that the thing is practically fixed.

Melbourne Children’s Hospital Committee cuts
down the salary of its assistant dispenser from £lOO to
£6O a year —£60 a year for a young man or woman who
must have spent several years, and at least £2OO, in
becoming competent to carry out the duties. Charity in
Victoria, under the ’Pootra’s regime, is sweating, where
it isn’t plain robbery.
♦ * * *
It is turning out that “Janny” Leahy’s rail-
way “economies” in Queensland were mostly fished out
of the same place that Bent’s came from. Leahy “saved"
£129,369 in 12 months ; but as he earned £147,949 less,
the net results were worse than they were before Leahy
began. Also, the Chief Engineer reports that “ the limit
of reduction has already been passed, and the expendi-
ture may have to be raised if we have years of excessive
or even normal rainfall.” Further—
“ The average age of the lines is increasing, and has
not yet reached the limit at which renewals of timber will
approach a maximum, so that it will be increasingly diffi-
cult in the future to keep down the cost of maintenance
under this, one of the heaviest items of expenditure.”
This policy of borrowing to carry out public works,
and then borrowing more to keep the public works
going, has landed Queensland in a bog. It has a huge
public debt and no sinking fund, and now it is likely to
find that all its present sources of revenue are required
to prop up the lines sufficiently to carry traffic. The
same thing is happening in the Mechanical Engineer’s
department: “ if the traffic had remained normal the
greatest difficulty would have been found in handling it
with the present stock of locomotives.” So far
Australia, with its consuming thirst for railways, has
travelled on the plea that you couldn’t expect new
lines to pay from the jump, but that, if you waited
awhile, they would be all right; it forgot,
what it was just as necessary to remember,
that even if traffic increased expenses wouldn’t be long
in increasing too. Now the fact is coming home that
what was forgotten is more of an absolute certainty than
what was remembered; for, unfortunately, there isn’t
any certainty about traffic increasing, while no sort of
engine, or sleeper, or rail has yet been invented that
won’t wear out, and cost more for repairs and renewals in
the 10th year than it did in the first. .

It will probably turn out that not a penny of
the insurances on the foundered steamer Ovalau was laid-
off at Lloyd’s. A cable message mentioned that re-
insurances had been effected at 25 guineas premium ; bub
that was only a “ doctor’s ” quotation. At the time it
was offered the interested co’s. would have laughed at
that price.
* • * *
The trouble with the N.S.W. State clothing
factory and the Railway Commissioners, though tem-
porarily settled against the factory, is not yet done with.
The Commissioners called for tenders for the supply of
railway men’s uniforms for 1904, and as a private ten-
derer cut under the State factory by 25 percent., they
stated that they had no option but to let him the con-
tract. Last year the State factory contracted at 21s. a
suit. The lowest outside price was 20s. This year, with
wages firmer, materials dearer, and the uniforms required
being of equal quality, the factory again tendered at 21s.
The successful tenderer, if the Railway Commissioners
are correct, undertakes to supply trousers, vest, and coat
for 15s. 9d. Experts maintain that, barring sweating and
inferior turn out, the uniforms cannot be supplied at
15s. 9d. The successful tenderer’s shops will be closely
watched for sweating, and the style and fit of the uni-
forms undergo a sharp inspection, and if the firm which
has the contract makes it pay, the rest of the tailoring
trade will have learnt something.

* » * *
The row between Potter and the Broken Hill
Prop., the holders of the two best-known chemical pro-
cesses for the recovery of a zinc product, is still on ; it
will get into the N.S.W. Supreme Court presently.
Potter’s patent application was for an acid process ; he
fed the acid (any acid; even lemon-juice would do if
lemons were cheap enough) into the zinc-bearing pulp,
and the zinciferous portion rose as a scum. Delprat’s
application was for a salt cake (sulphuric acid) process ;
and Potter claims that that was covered by his applica-
tion—that and every other acid. As to the zinciferous
product, it is said to be worth £5 a ton at the Australian
seaboard. As Block 14 seems to have secured the right
to use Potter’s process, and is. now building a plant for
the purpose (the first on a commercial scale), it has been
supposed that this co. was interested in the patent ; but
it isn’t. The interest in it that Potter (a Victorian metal-
lurgist) doesn’t own belongs to the biggest of Melbourne
newspaper men and a Vic. M.L.O. This accounts for the
toughness of the fight which is being made against the
B.H. Prop.—a fight which the co. probably didn’t reckon
on when it first took un the business.
• * * *
B.H. Junction North gob ready to restart its
ramshackle mill when lead went to £l2, but the start did
not take place, and it is not likely to for some time to
come. Except for a very little while the co. was
probably never able to produce metal at under £l3, and
no developments of much importance have taken place
in the mine. Probably it will be able to make a trifle
some day by selling its large heap of zinc-bearing
tailings, but its best hope lies in amalgamation with the
Junction, to which its fine main shaft would be a
valuable asset. The co.’s cash balance is about £2600.
* * * *
“ M.C.” on the need for giving the zinc industry
a lift (B.* Oct. 18): Not only can a zinc concentrate be
produced cheaply and laid down at the smelter at a very
low price, but enormous quantities of waste fine coal can be
utilised in zinc treatment. Under present conditions local
works would have to compete with the cheapest labor in
Europe, working on Broken Hill ore. A bonus on zinc and
an export duty on zinc oi e would start an industry, and
galvanising would follow as a natural result. When the
time arrives that Broken Hill threatens to shut down (in
a few years’ time, possibly), then will the folly be realised
of fostering German industry .at the expense of our own.
The same reasoning applies just as much to the manufac-
ture of lead and copper as to zinc smelting. How about
white lead and copper .wire, with the local and Asiatic
markets ? The daily press seems almost violently opposed
to a bonus or an export duty of any kind ; also the Free-
trade section of the Labor party.
* * * *
The north end of the Kalgoorlie field (W.A.) is
looking better than for a long time past. The Devon
Consols (late Mount Charlotte East) is opening out so
satisfactorily that the proprietors (Oades and Son) have
bought another 15 head of stamps from the Ivanhoe, and
will very shortly have a 25-head mill in full swing. In
the same belt, on the west, several abandoned leases have
been pegged by such knowing prospectors as Jenkinson,
Davis, and Oliver Hancock. Hancock was the prospector
of the North Lead, Kanowna, and the Adeline Lead, Kal-
goorlie, and also discovered the lode on the Eclipse,
which is now giving such satisfactory returns. Appa-
rently a revival may shortly be looked for in a district
that has long been nearly dead
* * * *
Golden Horseshoe, Kalgoorlie (W.A.), appears
determined to lead the van of the State’s gold-producers.
Not content with having 670,000 tons of 25dwt. ore in sight,
and a great deal more not fully blocked out, instruc-
tions have been issued to sink the shaft to 2000 ft. and
open up another 10 levels, and so increase the reserves to
eight years’ supply for the mill. The shaft is down about
1100 ft., and is being sunk at the rate of 35ft. monthly,
and this can easily be improved upon if thought
necessary. According to the diamond drill the
No. 4 lode, which was reported to have gone
into Great Boulder’s ground, is only 47ft. east of the
shaft at 1300 ft. as against 183 ft. east at 900 ft., so that it
has a very distinct westerly underlay into Horseshoe
ground. The prospects of the mine, in fact, never looked
better. The foundations of the new 50-head mill are now
in course of construction, but it is not expected to make
a start till well on in the new year. The mine is again
heading the list of producers, its October product in fine
gold reading 15,3050 z. against Great Boulder Perse-
verance’s 14,3250 z.
• • * *
Captain Pleitner holds fast to his statement
that the Cumberland, Norseman (W.A.), reef will not pan
out more than an average of Joz. to the ton. Previously
it was said here that Pleitner found the reef barely 2ft.
wide ; but he is willing to allow that, though the reef
was only 2ft. wide in the widest part in Monday’s shaft,
in the other (Hall’s) it might he 4ft., though in some
places it is, he asserts, difficult to trace any lode at all.
Anyhow, the mill is beginning to talk now, and so the
controversy ought soon to settle itself. -
• * * *
Murchison Associated, Day Dawn (W.A.), is
getting a move on after being muddled for something-
like eight years. The old 10-head mill has been removed
from the Polar Star lease, a mile away, and re-erected on
the Rubicon, together with a cyanide plant bought from
the defunct Fingall Proprietary, and a new 12-drill air
compressor. The new main shaft on the Rubicon is being
hurried, and when this has been completed, it is proposed
to erect a brand-new 40-head mill. In the meantime ore
to keep the old mill in work will be drawn through the
old underlay shaft. This shaft is only down 350 ft. verti-
cal, which fact doesn’t say much for the Bewic.k-Moreing
control. When the shaft reaches 500 ft. vertical it will be
connected by a crosscut with the new main shaft. This
work will take several months to complete.
* * * *
White Feather Reward, Kanowna (W.A.),
after being unsuccessfully worked by the co. for nine
years, is to be offered to tributors.. In the very early
days—about the end of 1895—a 10-stamp mill got to work
and produced an average of 2Joz. to the ton ; but it is by
no means certain that some of the tons didn’t run to
about 80owt. Naturally the costs were very high, and, in
spite of the tall “average,” the co. went bung, and has
been in a chronic state of collapse ever since. A couple
of years ago £14,000 cash was raised to sink a new main
shaft to cut the reef at 500 ft. The reef was cut all right,
but the gold contents were practically nil; hence the
present fiasco. The mine never was any good, and
should have been labelled a wild cab long ago and
abandoned.
• * • •
Queensland Menzies (W.A. ), so far as is ascer-
tainable, is not in the position that one would judge
from the slump in the scrip. Returns have certainly
fallen away somewhat; for the rest, the movement has
been in sympathy with the Lady Shenton, which may
probably be abandoned soon. The Shenton is, however,
quite a 'mile south of the Queensland. The district has
always been patchy T , all the other mines on the line of
lode, such as Lady Sherry, Crusoe, Friday, Florence and
Alpha only yielding occasional dabs. During its eight
years career, the Menzies co. has distributed 14s. a share,
or £92,400, of which 3s. a share, or £19,800, has been
paid this year. The mill will shortly be doubled, and,
unless manager Ferguson’s statements and estimates are
hopelessly wrong, the mine is good for some time yet.
As, however, the deepest workings are only about 500 ft.,
people are not unnaturally nervous, as it was about this
depth that the Lady Shenton petered out. Somebody
must have turned on the tap pretty freely to have
knocked out the market to the tune of 50 per cent.
* * * *
South Kalgurli (W.A.) is now said to have a
dump of 40,000 tons of tailings, estimated to be worth
lOdwt. per ton instead of nil, as formerly asserted. If
this estimate is correct, it shows a very big leakage, and
would fully account for the very short shrift accorded
the Riecken process on Boulder 1. It is freely asserted
that all the despised dumps of residues, now covering the
various leases, will sooner or later suffer retreatment.
Anyway the mine is likely to get a much better show in
the future than it has had in the past, and may yet pay
divs.
• * * *
Devon Consols, Kalgoorlie (W.A.), has recently
struck several very rich dabs in driving south at
the 160 ft. level. This level has been opened up for over
700 ft., and, though the ore is low grade on the whole,
these rich patches, ranging up to 500 oz. in one bunch,
make the mine a highly payable proposition. The
property is now equipped with 25 head of stamps pur-
chased from the Ivanhoe and capable of treating 2500 tons
monthly. This should help to increase the Kalgoorlie
returned to a fair extent. Some of the adjoining shows
on the west and north, abandoned by Adelaide co’s, but
lately repegged, look like proving a success ; that held by
Davis has already yielded a 15dwt. crushing, with
6dwt. in the tailings. All these properties are situated
to the north-east of the Mt. Charlotte, and, though long
in the hands of co.’s, were very poorly prospected.
* * * »
Hannan’s Reward, Mt. Charlotte (W.A.), after
four reconstructions, looks very like being at the end of
its tether. Manager Nutman, also manager of the North
Kalgurli, has announced his resignation of the former
show, on the plea that the latter requires all his energies.
The Reward claim, barring producing an enormous
amount of alluvial gold, has all along proved an expen-
sive duffer. It is a good tributing, or working-man’s,
show, but is absolutely valueless for a highly capitalised
Bull co., and the; sooner Bull recognises it the better for
his pocket. The closing down of the mine may be looked
for.
• * * t *
Queen Margaret, Bulong (W. A.), is now in the
hands of tributors, and fossickers have so niuch faith in
its possibilities that close on 60 men are at work chasing
the nimble’weight. It is a mine of vast possibilities for
the working miner, as pockets of several hundreds of
ounces are not infrequent, and there is nothing that some
miners appreciate more than the chance of a fortune on a
stroke of the pick. With this continual drag off its back,
the co. may be able to make a mine of the South Gipps-
land show, of which manager Henderson still appears
very confident. Most recent crushings have not fulfilled
expectations; but, naturally, excuses are forthcoming
for the tailing-off of returns, and everything is to be
right when the extra 5 head of stamps and the cyanide
plant are s'- 1 - going.

Two of Ballarat’s best-known co.’s, the
Britannia United and the Last Chance United, which
have been using bad language to each other over the
back fence for years, have now decided to continue their
row where the public can hear it. The Britannia accuses
the Last Chance of stealing its gold, and demands £20,000
damages and an account of the working of certain
ground, which it says the Last Chance has wrongfully
removed. It is the biggest row that Victoria has had
since Bendigo enjoyed a famous quarrel over 20 years ago.

* * * »
In the last three months the 40 Charters Towers’
quoted stocks have shrunk nearly £29o,ooo—from
£2,355,000 to £2,070,000. The smaller concerns have
suffered most; 50 per cent, slumps have been common
enough. The Victoria group's disaster was the worst; a
typical case is the Victoria G.M.’s crash from £28,125 to
£lO,OOO. Marshall’s Queen, Mills, and the Rubies have
been among the slaughtered On the other hand, nearly
all the Brilliant stocks, the Queen Cross Reef and the
Queen Central Block have more than held their price.
Queen Cross Reef, at close on £650,000, is reckoned by
the market to be worth twice as much aS the Brilliant
Central, and five times as much as any other mine on the
field. The Brilliant Central and the Q.C. Reef have the
same area— 25 acres—but the Q.C. Reef has lately given
about five times the other’s return. It has not the
“ life ’’ of the Brilliant Central, though ; and at present
rates buyers will probably not get their money back.
* » ♦ *
One of Charters Towers’ most characteristic
stickers has just thrown up the sponge—the No. 1 West
Victoria. Its solid struggle lasted 14 years. The original
co. having exhausted all its capital, it was reconstructed,
and shareholders used to look for ha’penny call notices as
regularly as for the full moon. From first to last the
block (between Clark’s Worcester and Victory and the
Victoria) gave 23 small crushings.
* » * *
Nobody but the debenture-holders got anything
out of Ohillagoe (N.Q.) in the last half-year. The mines
themselves made a heavy loss ; the railway gave £18,530
above costs, and after paying interest on cost, about
£7BOO remained. In spite of that, however, the co.’s net
loss on its whole undertaking was £l5OO.

SOCIETY
The Universal Brotherhood and Theo-
sophical Society of Sydney gravely discussed, the
other evening, a paper entitled “The Bringing
About of a Better Understanding Between So-
called Savage and Civilised Races.” Tobacco is
tlie best medium for the purpose, but
turkey-red calico is not to be despised*
Soap, and tooth-powder, and stove-
pipehats, and Karma, and the
Wheel are quite useless.

Following sundry oilier shocked Justices,
His Honor A’Beckett, of Victoria, lately raged at
great length over the perjury committed by some-
body in a case of arson charged against a striking
Outtrim miner. Seeing that several persons flatly
contradicted each other as to one particular wit-
ness’ actions, there undoubtedly was perjury, but
when the 12 men in the box, by acquitting the
accused, indirectly but unanimously branded some
of the Crown witnesses as the perjurers, his Honor
quite forgot to use the power he had to commit
any of those persons for their crime. Also the
Crown itself has so far failed to file an indictment.
That is why the average citizen sticks his tongue
in his cheek when a Judge rails against perjury.
It is only the accused person’s perjury that is such
a heinous offence; the Crown witness’ prevari-
cation is a mere trifle.
The manufacture of new religions never
ceases in Canterbury (M.L.), the home of so many
freaky faddists and lily-winged purists with a tin
halo and a collection bag. Latest thing in the
way of home-made worship there is the “Cyclists
Church,” started by Professor Bickerton, formerly
of Canterbury College, in his own picturesque
grounds located a little way from the city. His
show is largely visited on Sunday afternoons,
mostly by the young man in the sweater, accom-
panied by tbe typical nice girl, who stroll over
lawns of green carpet, and repose in the grateful
shade of odorous trees. The service is something
of a cross between a pair of scissors and a hair-
restorer. The tiresome maunderings and groan-
ings about sin, the flesh, and the chap down below
are cut out, and bright sacred and secular music,
a little prayer, cheerful lecturettes on cleanliness
and godliness and the ethics of exercise, and
afternoon tea are substituted. Members of the
“church” pay so much a term; visitors, 6d. a
time. It is a fine, poetic, breezy, soul-satisfying
way of spending the middle part of Sunday, and
the show is likely to evoke many base imitations.

| ?It was recently proposed that St. Kilda
(Melb.) council should start a movement among
the metropolitan councils for the levying of a
charity rate of one half-penny in the &. The idea
was rejected with great speed, one grocer person
getting off the memorable remark that “the
question has not yet matured.” Perhaps it
hasn’t—but the aged person who so badly needs
assistance has matured, and the gospel which
says in almost every line “give to the poor
matured 1900 years ago, and according to religious
theory (most aldermen are religious) the oheol
that callous Bumbles go to is very mature indeed.
Apropos , poor rates in England vary from to
2s. 2d. in the £, and the alderman who doesn t
regard the example of England as good enough
to follow is a disloyal and insurrectionary person,
and probably a Fenian and a pro-Boer.

That unseemly shindy among the directors
of the Watchman, which ended in Parson Madg-
wick being cast out by his fellow boss-Orangemen,
who appointed another director in his stead, is a
curious instance of the parson’s idea of doing busi-
ness. Shareholders are supposed to appoint and
remove directors, and only a body of parsonical
directors would begin expelling each other in this
off-hand fashion. Of late, the Scarlet Woman has
been having something of a rest, and Parson Dill
Macky hasn’t shouted to any extent worth men-
tioning about the errors of Rome. Rome always
gets a bit ahead when the Orangemen start to
skull-drag each other. Pius X., it is reported,
invited his three sisters to dine with him, the
other day—an innovation that almost amounts to
revolution —and he wouldn’t have dared to do
such a thing if the Watchman’s eye hadn’t been
temporarily off him.

or the “ Prodigal Son ”), and send

Colonel-Senator Jawbone Neild made cer-
tain of at least one vote for the Senate the other
night. He and JMajor-Senator Nancy Gould had
gone together to some military affair or other and
were not given the precedence proper to their
allowance of military rooster-feathers, or their
senatorial rank or something, and went away in a
huff. They got down to the A.B.C. Caf4 for some
slight refreshment. Mr. Gould wore a grey coat
over his beautiful millinery, but Mr. Neild
flaunted his flagrant get-up unabashed—and the
gleam of his scarlet and silver and gold dazzled
the room as he threw off a chic thing in
the way of military cloaks, of dark purple
with orange-silk lining. There were several
ladies—mostly of the milliner type or thereabouts
—in the room, and Colonel-Senator Neild’s frills,
and torchon lace, and his chifton, and his passe-
menterie edged with oriental embroidery and
paillettes of cut-steel, finished off with a little
guipure, deeply impressed them, and Colonel-
Senator Neild took as long as he possibly
could to consume sixpence worth of food.
Then he made great work of resuming the
Persian rug thing that he wore over his point
lace and his artillery—such great work that one
of the womenfolk, fascinated with his mag-
nificence, rose and humbly helped the great man
on with his drapery. An ordinary man would
have been confused. But Jawbone Neild is no
ordinary man. “ I thank you, madam,” he said,
bowing deeply, “I thank you” (taking off hi 3
forage cap, in defiance of regulation A210|) ; ‘‘l
thank you. I can assure you, it is the most dis-
tinguished compliment I have ever had paid to
me in my life.” And the Colonel-Senator swag-
gered out. That poor little woman would go
through a field of mice now to vote for him at the
Senate elections, and would battle with wild cock-
roaches in his defence.
• • * •
A pretty picture of rural delights is mixed
up with recent news of harvest prospects in S.
Australia. ‘‘The crops,” says the local corres-
pondent, “are looking splendid, and the farmers
are in high spirits ’’ ; and that reminds him. A
young farmer, doing well and thinking of soon
setting up a dairy of his own, was having the
usual Saturday-night stroll with the usual girl.
Walking along a lane hedged with spreading
acacias, they fell over a bull, and the young man
impaled his pants on the bull’s horn. The.animal
left hurriedly, taking the young man with it. The
young man yelled and tried to climb down. The
girl he left behind him shrieked. Finally the
young man was brushed off the bull as the latter
burst through a gate, but the beast went away
with the pants. This is one of the horrors of a
good season in S.A.; the grass is so tall that
lovers come on concealed bulls quite unexpectedly.
In bad years a bull can be seen on the bare earth
three miles off.

The Methodist Church in Queensland
decides to refuse marriage to Chinamen who
arrive there as adults, the impression being that
the adult Chow has mostly left a wife behind in
the Flowery Land, though he says he hasn’t. The
impression is in hundreds of cases truer than most
Divorce Court affidavits, even though it carries
with it the implication that the converted Metho-
dist Chow is a liar and a would-be bigamist.
But John, when shut out of matrimony, will
become a worse Australian John nuisance than
before.

Melbourne lately struck the beginnings of
what will either be a notable farce or a gigantic
law case. Plaintiff—a pleasant-lookmg lady of
nice manners—name 3 two score of defendants,
including Iceberg Irvine, Justice A’Beckett, a
bank, a dozen lawyers, and several well-known
city firms, and charges them with robbery, forgery,
perjury, outlawry, boycotting, blackmailing,
bribery, and the passing of fraudulent accounts.
She came before Justice Hood, on a summons for
directions, and ran her part of the show quietly
and sensibly, incidentally getting his Honor to
administer a healthy snub to a member of the
Upper House. Said person, who is one of
the defendants, desired the Judge to sum-
marily strike out the case as farcical, but was
informed that thev didn’t do things in that court
after the fashion of the Upper House-the law
wasn’t going to be bustled for all the Landlords
in creation ; there were costs, and things would
be done decently and in order, with costs, of
course. So a statement of claim is to be furnished
and the matter is to be argued gradually and with
due regard to dignity.

“ J.” goes “ down the Castlereagh ” :
Walked down Castlereagh-street, Sydney, and breathed
in much strange atmosphere. Struck a white bootmaker,
who has a sign over his door written in Chinese charac-
ters, with a pair of slippers painted beside the symbols.
I stepped in to ask the meaning of the sign. The owner
of the boot-hospital seemed surprised at my ignorance,
and, pitying my lack of education, explained that the
spider-legged characters read: “Bring y°u r , r . e P a i r ? 1 j
here, he does them cheap.” I sneered, told him In an
been 16 years in China, and the characters stood for He
can’t make slippers at all.” I left him filled with grg\e
doubts. Went on, and struck a sixpenny restaurant
kept by an alleged white man. It had three Chinese
characters written on the window in whiting. I presume
they meant that puppy-dog, or some such luxury. on
the menu. Went farther along, and stopped to discuss
preferential trade with a livery-stable proprietor. Glanced
into the yard, and saw a white man loading a Chow s cart
with manure. The keeper of the horse boarding-house
said that the white man was regularly employed Dj our
yellow brother. Tried to get up on the .fence to hooray
for a white Australia, but a policeman pointed to a notice,
“ Billstickers beware,” so I desisted. I didn t go anj
further ; the rest of Oastlereagh-street is mostly taken
up with registry and loan offices, and I was frighten

God gathered a handful of flowers,
My Sweet,
His purest and brightest and best,
And placing them into thy heart,
My Sweet,
He turned to the glowing west.
He dipped His hand in the gold,
My Sweet,
And scattered the drops o’er thy curls,
He rifled the depths of the sea.
My Sweet,
For thine eyes and thy teeth of pearls.
God dipped His hand in the dawn,
My Sweet,
Then touched thee on cheek and lips,
For He loved to finish His work,
My Sweet,
From thy soul to thy finger tip 9.
God took of the spirit of sunshine,
My Sweet,
The starlight and deep blue sea,
The essence of things He loves,
My Sweet,
And with them He made thee—for me 1 ”

If the N.S.W. Government had gone out
last week it would have had the credit of making
a bright remark in its last moments. Dr. Ross,
M.L.A., put on the notice paper one of his usual
frantic questions; he wanted to know, in effect,
what was the cause of weather. And the Minister
replied that he had heard that God had something
to do with it.
TEE IMPROVED DOG.
“ Why, what’s the matter with poor Fido?"
“ Isn’t it just too bad, dear? I gave him to the
laundress to wash, and she starched him.”
Etiquette seems to be a thing of some size
at Rome. The new Pope has broken up a
mouldy precedent by inviting his three
sisters, the Miss Popes, to dinner, and his Head
Chamberlain, after vainly remonstrating, is
reported to have wept bitterly. Hitherto, it
seems, the Pontiff has never dined with anybody;
in the dead weight of his size and pomp he has
been expected to moon over his lonesome egg in
blank solitude. To be the only Pope in the
world, quite unable to rush out and hunt up two
or three other Popes when he felt lonely, and
then to have his loneliness rubbed in by not
being even allowed to ask a Cardinal to have
a snack with him, has apparently been too much
for Pius X., and he has rebelled. Pomp, when you
come to think of it, is very much like solitary
confinement without the option. Some of the
old Persian kings went mad for want of the sight
of an ordinary perpendicular human being; their
subjects all fell down when spoken to, and the
King of Kings went through life seeing only the
backs of horizontal grovellers till he began to see
visions as well. The old Jap emperors had a habit
of' going mad too. The old Jap emperor travelled
in a dark, closed-in coach lest he should be defiled
by a common person looking at him, and at home
in his palace he was expected to sit all day in his
chair of state with courtiers in a humble attitude
round him on the floor. He did this because the
vastness of his own mind was supposed to make
him independent of all ordinary amusement. A
species of tent was provided in his hall of state,
and when he felt extra miserable he retired there
and cheered himself up by sitting alone in the
gloom with his awful majesty for company. On
the whole, The Bulletin feels glad that the Pope
has asked his sisters to dine with him, so that he
can have his tea poured out for him like an
ordinary human being.
**. * *
One beneficent side issue of the Arbitration
laws in Maori land has been to knock out most of
the enthusiasm from Eight-Hour Day and similar
demonstrations. In the old days the unions had
to impress the crowd with their strength and their
solidity; so once a year they threw out their
chests and trailed along behind a quantity of
painted bunting. But painted bunting doesn’t
count these times, when one argument is more
powerful than six banners and two drums ; so the
disposition is to stay at home and think up argu-
ments to heave at the Arbitration Court and let
the Salvarmy have a monopoly of the banner and
the trombone business.
Manager Teece, of the A.M.P. Society,
has had a new taste of socialism, and he owns
that he liked it. While he was in Westralia he
stayed at the Gwalia State p»ib. “It is a
splendid hotel,” he says, “and I think it was an
excellent move on the part of the Government to
establish it there.” But this admission of the
success of Public Enterprise evidently made Mr.
Teece uneasy, for he hurried on to add that,
of course, the scheme would break down if
applied on a large scale—in a city, for instance.
“Government supporters would perhaps make
it a condition of their support that the Govern-
ment should purchase liquor from some of their
clients, and thereforeone does not know what would
be foisted on the public.” Tjp to now, nobody
has suggested that Government supporters have
made it a condition of their support that the
Postmaster-General should give their clients the
run of the telegraph office ; and if any attempts
have been made by merchants to get their goods
carried for nothing on the railways in return for
political support, they apparently haven’t come
off. And why the liquor business should not be
as honestly managed as the post and telegraphs
and the railways, or as the Maoritand Life Assur-
ance Department, or the law courts, or the patents
office, or a hundred other things, Mr. Teece doesn’t
mention.
* * • *
There are good times ahead for the devil’s
brigade in Melbourne. The great Heaity case is
to be argued again before the Full Court. This is
the case that occupied C. J. Madden for 43 days a
while back, his judgment consisting of 1000 folios
and taking many hours to deliver. And the costs
will, as before, be monumental. An idea thereof
may be gained from the appeal books delivered to
the associate ; they are four, beautifully bound,
and comprising 7984 folios altogether. Vol. 1
contains pleadings, reasons for judgment,
judgment and supplementary judgments; vol. 2,
evidence and depositions; vol. 3, exhibits and
documents ; vol. 4, correspondence. The appeal
is expected to last three weeks, at the end of
which time there will probably be precious little
of the estate left to fignt for.
♦ * • *
The Harbor Trust offices, recently opened
on Circular Quay, Sydney, ocoupy an historic
site. Something like 100 years ago surgeon
Thomas Jamison lived on the spot, it being
then a water-frontage. In Macquarie’s time
it passed into the hands of Mrs. Mary
Reibey, the lady who was not Margaret
Catchpole. Next door, north, Isaac
Nichols, the Postmaster, had his resi-
dence and office, and later on, a little
further north, the famous Rum
Puncheon public-house, frequented by
whaling men, lived in an atmo-
sphere of rum, whale oil, and early-
day profanity.
Candidate Alexander Hay, defeated the
other day at Glen Innes (N.9.W.), affects the
English system of landlord and tenant in his
farming business, and when he was in a tight
place at Glen Innes, tenant A. Woods rushed all
the way from the Berry estate to assure the voters
that it is due to Mr. Hay’s generosity that he (Woods)
and neighbors have now happy homes, and are in a pros-
perous, independent way of living.
“ Generosity ” has the real old Hinglish ring about
it. The properly-instructed Hinglish peasant
considers it is by his landlord’s “ generosity ” he
is allowed to live at all. Generous squires who
graciously permit their tenants to exist in con-
sideration of paying a high rent to the useless
parasites at the head of affairs, and wormy tenants
who learn to pour the grease of adulation over the
landlord for fear they should get their rent raised,
are twin evils.
* * * *
N.S.W. Attorney-General Wise has done
another bad turn to the newspapers by quashing
the prosecution of those three persons accused
of conspiring together to force a man “ to marry
the girl.” The trial would have had a healthy
influence on various circulations. The “society”
column of the D.T. gave the first news to the
world of the marriage—describing it as a “quiet
and interesting” affair, at which “the bride wore
a travelling costume” and “only relatives were
present.” Later, the bridegroom called on the
police to allege that he wore a sprinting costume
(having been dragged to the ceremony as he
endeavored to escape) and a worried look. And
now the public is refused further information
about that quiet wedding, which the bridegroom
alleged was quiet principally because he was
afraid of being killed if he shouted for assistance.
It is not known whether prosecutor is proceeding
with his suit for nullity of marriage. If he is,
then the evening papers may get their harvest
after all—but the delay is aggravating.

Anyone except Josiah Symon might have
been badly taken aback at an incident in Adel-
aide police-court last week. The K.C., defending
in a tote shop prosecution, made much fun of last
year’s Lottery and Gaining Act. “I don’t know
who drafted it,” he said. Police-magistrate
Gordon smiled sweetly and said “I did ! ” Symon
never turned a hair. “In that case,” he said,
“it must be the tinkering of the Legislature that
accounts for its present ridiculous condition.”
But the Act held together well enough to get
Josiah’s client a nearly-record fine of £5O.

“Periwinkle,” from
the Victorian back-
blocks—as far
back as a man
can get with-
out backing
into 9. A.
Consider the
widely divergent
tactics of local
creeds in regard
to the manner
in which they
carry out their
annual picnics.
The Pres b y-
terians invite all
and sundry, en-
gage traps (and
pay for them) to
cart all-comers to
the rendezvous,
a green spot
three miles out;
give three
“blow-outs”—
viz., at 2 p.m.,
4.30 p.m., and 7
p.m. ; take no
payment what-
ever, and ask
you how you
enjoyed yourself.
They make it a .
point to give the
poorer folks pre-
ference over every-
body else. The
Anglicans collect
from their own
“ color ” only, but
invite all and sundry
(you find 3’our own con-
veyance), and give you
a “blow-out” after
their own lot have
been satisfied. The
Methodists collect
from all and sundry
in the town, and
charge strangers
Is. each at
“ blow-out ”
time, which is
once, at 6 p.m.
They carry their
own people to
the grounds,
and any others they
can’t dodge. Don’t care whether you enjoy your-
self or not—if not, that’s your own look out.
Sing two hymns and grace before the devasta-
tion begins. The Catholics collect from their own
people, and invite everybody who cares to come. If
you stay away they like you better. Their own people
help themselves. Strangers are waited on, especially the
financial friends who help when a bazaar is in the air.
To get out to the picnic ground you hang on by your
eyebrow behind Tim or Mick’s family conveyance, which
is filled to the splash-boards, and shout for the driver and
occupants at the first pub you pass! The Salvarmy
people collect from everybody twice over, when possible,
and issue tickets at so-much a piece to all who don’t
wear Army uniform. They borrow vehicles and charge
“ fares ” to the meeting place—give you one poor meal of
home-made pastry, sing a hymn in between the mouth-
fuls, and take up two collections.

N.S.W. Judge Rogers last week sentenced
two pretty sisters, aged about 21, to two and years
imprisonment respectively (with a prospect of
release after six months if security for their good
behavior was forthcoming) for a series of thefts
from houses where they were employed. One
probable reason why his Honor fell with rather
an extra hard thud on the accused was that they
had resumed their course of larceny while out on
bail awaiting trial. The fact of being on bail
should be a sufficiently broad hint to any good-
looking girl, and this resumption of hostilities, so
to speak, at such an inappropriate time, naturally
made the Law arch its back somewhat.

in progress it is proposed to chip off the hideous
“John Harris, Mayor” bas-relief lettering which
disfigures the western wall.

“H.”: Nothing has interested me so
much for a long while as the Tit-Bits account of
its buried treasure. A tale called “Hidden, Not
Lost,” has been running in its pages. Assur-
ance was given that the Tit-Bits proprietary had
planted 500 sovereigns in an accessible spot, to
which unerring clues would be given in the story.
Acting on these, Hubbard, an engineer, of Leices-
ter, found the money, and Gowan, a Glasgow
attorney, narrowly missed it. The swag was in
two long, metal cylinders, each containing 250
sovs. Newnes, jun., and the novelist buried it
near Hitchin, in Hertfordshire. They went to the
place a week later and could not find the plant!
This was through a slight error in measurement,
duly rectified in the explanations of the tale.
Thousands of people have been bashing their
brains and wondering how they missed seeing a
thing that was quite obvious. It is remarkable
what a variety of interruptions both the hiders
and finder met with in a lonely spot.
Westralia has the habit of appointing
medical men as resident magistrates out back ;
the man who has given you something to pull you
together in the morning after you have painted
the township more than ordinarily red will, later
on, fine you 50s. for doing it. The plan gives
Mulgaville both a doctor and a magistrate, and
it couldn’t afford either if they had to be two
men ; and generally it works out all right except
when somebody takes it into his head to appeal.
Then as a rule the best thing is to take the
magistrate’s notes to the chemist and let him
make up the prescription. Most times only a
chemist can read a doctor’s writing.
THE BROWN HAIRS ON MY COAT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Before the mirror in the hall
There is a deed I do,
For io 1 the girl warm tresses wears
That covertly I woo,
And O I the woman is a Fiend,
That I am married to.
Therefore I stand and scan the glass,
As any maiden might;
And from my shoulder I remove
The brown hairs shining bright;
And O ! the cat doth rub my shins —
The cat that walks by night.
For she from whose dear head they come,
What time we cling and kiss,
Is, in the desert of my life,
The one green oasis.
And I would rather lose my soul
Than lose that bit of bliss I
For she is only twenty—see ;
And I am forty—note :
The bond between is fatherly,
Just tinged with table d’hote !
Ah 1 ’tis my life’s one joy to pick
Those brown hairs from my coat !

THE DAGGER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Dear Hilda, once, for love of me,
Fresh from her flashing throat
Did pin—O precious jewellery 1—
This dagger in my coat.
Nor wot I of the cruel part
The wicked girl had play’d
Until I felt within my heart
It’s biting little blade.

HUGH M'CRAR.
A ROUNDEL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Once in a while the skies seem blue,
The way grows pleasant for a mile ;
Fair blossoms spring where no flowers grew—
Once in a while.
We leave the road—and mount the stile,
And hear the throstle’s song anew—
An anthem in a vaulted aisle.
Grief loses somewhat of its hue,
Tired, tear-worn eyes look up and smile,
When God’s sweet sunshine stealeth through
Once in a while.
M.L..
W. FRANUIS CHAMUERS.
“A FRAGMENT.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Roses white, and roses red
Grew in clusters near her head ;
Alice in her snow-white gown,
Reached a crimson blossom down,
Lingering there beside the creek
Heard a step, and to her cheek,
Crept the blush of roses red,
As she slowly turned her head ;
And encountered eyes of grey,
Where love held undivided sway.
What he asked, what Alice said,
None heard, save roses white and red.
