A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, November 3, 1903.
Mr Deab Arini.—
Two dress horrors that women thought were
dead and buried threaten resurrection. They are
the starched white linen collar and the crinoline.
In fact the first is in vogue already m New York
and Paris ; the latter is yet in embryo, as a single
circle of crinoline wire round the foot of the dress.
It will probably develop no further, for women of
to-day are more aesthetic than were their crinoline-
wearing forbears. As for the collar, it will be the
cause of more work for the beauty doctors,
for it was chiefly on their advice that women gave
up some three or four years ago, wearing linen
chokers, which coarsened the cuticle, impoverished
the muscles ' and caused a broad brown mark
around the throat. 1 saw one of the new linen
collars on an arrival by the Ventura last trip. It
fastened at the back, and was collar and stock all
in one. The stock took root at the top of the
collar under the wearer’s chin, and lay on her
chest in hour-glass shape. It was worn with a
tailor-made of thin tweed. Another threatened
revival—this time a picturesque one—is the jersey.
Judging by the pictures of jersey women, it will
give good figures a great show.
Whilst talking dress—why haven’t Sydney
dressmakers got the little Yankee notion known
as the skirt gauge ? I have seen a poor skirt-titter
kneel for nearly 20 minutes on the floor and get a
rush of blood to the intellect, and the fitted one
like to faint with weariness, while the skirt
was being gauged six inches from the ground all
round. Securing a perfectly even, round skirt
is more difficult than most persons think.
Some women have an “advanced” figure or one
hip ever so slightly higher than its sister, and
every inequality shows in the hang of a skirt.
The skirt gauge takes the measurement in a few
minutes. Woman to be fitted stands erect whilst
the fitter runs the gauge around the end of the
skirt, an indicator marking the height with chalk
all the way.
Some day, perhaps, after we who live now are
all dead, this great country will start to hold
flower feasts as they do in Persia, India and
Japan; and the people of the yet-to-be wdl
have all the fun. Someone writing in 0.M.r1.
entreats the horticulturists of Sydney to wake
up and hold a general exhibition of flowers
and plants, and publish such fruits and vege-
tables as are in season. It is rather late to do
anything in the way of a big floral exhibition
this year, except, perhaps, for a lightning
organiser. The reign of the roses is sq nearly
over. At present the Royal Horticultural
Society holds three shows a year—daffodils in
August, chrysanthemums in the autumn, roses in
October; but it says that the larger, general ex.
cannot be undertaken for lack of support and
scarcity of funds. Well, the hospitals at
present are being very badly supported. Prince
Alfred Hospital, for instance, is passing through
what the newspapers call “ a very critical time.
In June last its income was £832 less than at the
same period the previous year, while the expendi-
ture, despite rigid economy, was .£836 higher.
At this period last year there was a surplus of
nearly £BOO ; to-day there is a deficiency of close
upon £9OO. If a feast of flowers were organised
by capable persons—persons who knew the value
of having pretty society women and actresses, to
sell buttonholes and squashes and wreaths, who
would secure good bands, a kinematograph or
two, and a few other side shows, and hold the
exhibition at the Cricket Ground in aid of the
hospitals, I’m pretty sure an attendance as nume-
rous as that which went to look at the ladies
cricket mateh would result. That same cricket
match drew the people by thousands—grand
stands and lawns were packed, and a clear £5OO
resulted to the theatrioal charities and Children’s
Hospital. Sydney-siders seem glad of any excuse
to rush the Cricket Ground.
The “Feast ” might begin at noon and continue
till near midnight. Teas could be served on the
lawn, and the electric light could shine upon arches
of roses and the beautiful exhibits of our Botanic
Gardens—palms, orchids and ferns, as well as
loan collections from private conservatories!
Why, there’s millions in it! And the Royal
Horti. Soc. might (as the quality of charity isn’t
sieved) co-operate.
Caradoc Kerry, of the lost yacht Ariadne, has
got his £lO,OOO insurance money in London, and an
apology, and much costs. Since the morning Detec-
tives Goulder and Hoskisson fished the little man
out of bed in a boarding-house in Upper Fort-street,
Sydney, he has been more or less on trial. Kerry
is a Maorilander, with sailor boy experiences.
He was in the thick of the first rush to New
Guinea. He started a company which wanted
1,000,000 acres from the authorities of the day.
He started another co. in Brisbane for gold-seeking
alone. He was at that time a mild-faced, ginger-
whiskered enthusiast who looked too innocent for
rough diggers and Papuan cannibals. His next
departure was the opening of an academy in
London —Dashwood House it was called—where
he undertook to teach, for substantial premiums,
the sons of the nobility all the ins and outs ot
Australian gold-mining, which sounds very like
teaching them how to pursue the wild ass theoreti-
cally in Piccadilly. And then came the sailing
yacht Ariadne, her wreck, the bitter wrangling of
himself and Mumford, his captain, the charges of
blackmail and conspiracy, and the collapse of
Mumford, who is about the most remarkable
figure in the whole story.
A correspondent writes to me that Miss Maie
Saqui, well known here with Mr. J.C.W.’s Comic
Opera co’s, was married on September 23rd to
Mr. Hope Travers, of the Grenadier Guards.
Surely the bloque is noble—an heir-apparent, or
something like that; otherwise Maie will not have
married as well as Rosie Boote, who became
Marchioness of Hertford. Singular thing, but
within a few weeks five Gaiety girls were married
to Guardsmen in London. Not one old G.G. is
now left to George Edwardes.
Presentation of a ring to Miss Crossley will be
made to-morrow —“Elijah” night—at the Town
Hall. Presentations to singers nave been remark-
ably rare in Sydney. Melba did not receive any
gift; in fact, since Catherine Hayes, the Irish
songstress, who got a guinea for concert seats here
and in Melbourne well-nigh 50 years ago, no
artist, so far as I can learn, has taken away any-
thing save cash, unless she did it when the public
wasn’t looking. In the case of Catherine Hayes a
magnificent case of jewels was presented on the
eve of her departure from Sydney ; all the not-
abilities of New South Wales contributed to the
testimonial, which was presented in the old Vic-
toria Theatre.
By next Cup day I intend to attempt a little
enterprise. I shall hire a cargo tramp steamer
for the Cup excursion with accommodation for
4000 passengers at 10s. each. Of course my 4000
must bring their own bedding and vittles. On
second thought I shall not travel on my tramp,
because if it were still being rocked on the cradle,
of the deep, owing to engine breakage, whilst, the
Cup was being run, my tramps might break things
—one’s head is breakable. The passengers
wouldn’t be quite of the status of those who went
by the Oceana —they made a diamond show every
night, and wore their Derby frocks at breakfast
and their Cup gowns at lunch.
Some Sydney shopkeepers have been telling a
local paper that this is one of the best seasons
ever experienced for the sale of Cup finery. A
number of 25-guinea dresses have been sold;
many ladies bought five costumes at 18 guineas
each, and one charmer bought six hats at not less
than four guineas each. Now, if she would wear the
whole six at once, there would be something
like a hat show, and she would be certain
of being in all the papers. An evening paper
boldly states that “the following hats were pur-
chased ” by Mrs. Jehoram Jehonadab ; the list is
enumerated, and the Society for the Protection of
Birds will regret to hear that one hat was trimmed
with “a whole bird of Paradise,” and another
with humming-birds’ wings. Mrs. J. J., may not
be able to wear those hats if it rains, or the dress
chroniclers may miss her, but at least the fact that
she has purchased the clothes has been published.
The Ovalau is found —the oinders thereof are
resting at the bottom of the sea. The passengers
and crew are safe above ground, so the only piece
of crape is that tied on the handle of the insurance
office. A Sydney Telegraph man tried to inter-
view Lord Boringdon, one of the Ovalau’s pas-
sengers, whom he found asleep in a bunk on the
rescuing steamer. The young man was disinclined
to give any information. Perhaps he said “Ha_w”
—perhaps he didn’t. It’s not his fault that he is a
lord, and he doesn’t see why he should be pestered
with interviews when he is just doing a holiday
tour. Besides the habit of expecting lords to
fiart with information ought to be discouraged,
n many cases it is like confiscating the widow’s
mite.
A novel floral trophy was given to Miss
Ada Crossley at Adelaide. It was a white
witch-elm wand, having close to the top. a large
cluster of lilies of the valley. From this hung
three white ribbons printed in silver with some
appropriate verse, and finished with three per-
fectly tuned silver bells, bearing the three notes
A, D, A. The top of the wand bore a silver star
about six inches across it, and it made a support
for Miss Crossley when her damaged ankle com-
pelled her to lean upon something.

when one thinks of the coy command which she

Writes my Melbourne correspondent: —
“ Lad y Tennyson gets off a neat little speech
now and then. One of her best efforts was at the
opening of the Gentlewomen’s Work Association
at Prahran Town Hall, the other day. ‘lndigent
gentlewomen,’ who are skilful with the needle on
plain or fancy fabrics, represent "the good cause
for the society’s championship. Sales at the
stalls were brisk. The impoverished lady, or the
just hard-up society girl (the work of all was
represented in the sale) may thank their stars for
the excellent results of the show. Bibs, now that
babies are fashionable, were snapped up eagerly,
and white collars, cravats, and jabots in point lace
commanded boom prices.
“In the wild rush preceding the junkets last
week, one collided with various groups of females
representing the Australian branch of the National
Council of Women, who were congressing on the
very threshold of the Cup carnival. On Wednes-
day, Lady Silverlocks opened ‘ Stonington ’ to
the thoughtful sisterhood, and turned them out
to grass. The weighty-browed women found it
pleasant to argue and air their gravity on the
shaven sward. High tea and a tall concert
followed that evening. Next day the Congress
settled down into its collar, and set to work on
heavy matters concerning the status of the sex.
How many of the delegates stole away to the Cup,
just to see how wicked it was, I dare not guess.
“On Derby day, the most phlegmatic band of
racegoers on hand occupied the two vice-regal
pens, and wore the quiet hues of a retired life.
'The Gov. -Generaless, cobl of aspect in unremark-
able cream color and black millinery, looked the
typical Englishwoman. Erect of bearing, quiet,
yet confident, ‘correct’ enough in every way
to fill creditably any position in life, she fails
spectacularly, nevertheless, through her lack
of charm, of light and shade, of color.
Readers of Anthony Trollope always recall
his clerical matrons, in connection with
the liege-lady of his Ex. T. According
to Trollope, the parsonage inarms of old England
rise early, look after their children, hear the
junior parish its catechism, and reward it with
horne-baked currant buns. Good, dutiful sort of
women, I take it, who stoop not to be charming
because they say it’s ‘ deceitful.’ When people
like that die, their widowers generally choose
quite different women to succeed them. ‘ Duty
first and pleasure afterwards.’ Her State Ex.
Clarke wore all black; she goes mourning
for that good old man, the clergyman,
her father-in-law. Lady Rawson (N.S.W.) and
Admiraless Fanshawe seemed a dove-quiet
pair in plumage of Quaker grey. The Rawson
daughter, in simple creamy frock, and small pink
roses in a flat hat, took after her unobtrusive
mother. Miss Fanshawe, in the_ simplest of
French grey muslins, is a tall, fair Miss with
sunny hair, and more small talk than the rest of
the group. Mrs. Ewan Frazer was one of the
best-dressed of visiting Sydney-siders. Poor Lord
Dick Neville worked hard to keep things moving,
but in the end left off trying to sparkle, and
devoted his time to pointing out to the Gov.-
General the ought-to-be-noticed, whom he then
chased and retrieved into the vice-regal presence.
“ The Derby parade was up to expectations.
The new dress notions are fashioned much in the
old modes of 1830 and thereabouts. The glories
of several recently returned globe-trotters were
great, notably in the case of Mrs. Pursey Chirn-
side, Mrs. Thornley, Mrs. Stuart Brunton and Miss
M‘Cumming. The first named lady supplemented
her toilette by a big fussy boa of black marabou
feathers—a hot-looking arrangement, albeit latest
London novelty. The millinery of all these
tourists oalled for notice. They all ran to. the
French poke or scoop shape, bordered with minia-
ture rosebuds or overlaid with delicate tiers of
lace. In the more emphatic Dolly Varden
examples and the coquettish chin knots, the
Misse3 Landale were first past the sartorial post.
They were in rainbow-hucd toilettes, fluttering,
fussing, swimming in the skirt parts. There was
a contrasting meagreness of chiffon about their
swan necks.
“ From the show of dresses there are one or two
conclusions to draw. The summer girl must billow,
droop, slope, and must curl her skirt-folds round
her bounteous hips when they are not billowing.
She must have a demure smile-under the shelter
of her slightly poke bonnet. The chic charmer is
no more. Pertness, Sauciness are out-of-fashion
affectations. The summer girl’s complexion, too,
has become a serious care. When it is not set off
by a cunning double mesh guaranteed to beautify,
it must be doctored so subtly that the naked eyes
must only detect a sweet tinge of hoalth —a mere
suggestion of bloom.
“A white linen sac coat, suspiciously like a
night-dress, is being sported by the extra chic.
It has a broad, plain, roomy back, and two side
gaps for sleeves that about complete the illusion.
Strange how one-half the sex runs to the extreme
in fresh-air blouses and a show of flesh tints, while
the other is pretty well starched to the ear-lobe
and encased in an unyielding coat of crash or linen
as hard as a frozen sheep. The golden or decent
mean is grasped by her who dresses in cool, soft
muslins, perforated very slightly about the neck
and wrists. She is the ideal Summer girl whose
presence is a solace.

French muslins. The accompanying head-gear
ran to chip and white plumes and a general air of
half-repressed coquetry. Lady Tennyson, with
her three boys, to say nothing of the Gov.-Gen.,
and a trio of sad aides, drew the creamiest to her
side.

“ T’other night (Oct. 29), at the Burwood Boys
Home demonstration, in Melbourne Town Hall,
beholders felt their hearts warm within them
towards the boyish inmates of the * Home. Such
natural kids they looked, marching and singing in
deadly earnest, not a scrap self-conscious about
anything—well, just a wee bit, perhaps, re the
muscles that they fondly fancied were swelling
manfully in their bits of bare arms. A mild argu-
ment was overheard, too, between two mites as to
whose chest was the best ‘sprung.’ One of the
disputants was so small, and his uniform trousers
had been kilted up so high on his figure to save
the hems from trailing on the ground,
that he was nothing but trousers nearly
up to his chin, not an inch of ( ‘ chest,
being on view to show whether it was ‘ sprung
or not. They only admit 30 boys into the home.
More would be unhomelike. _ A Mr. Edwards
gives them an airy country residence and 20 acres
of orchard rent free. The inmates call the couple
in charge ‘Dad’ and ‘Mum’ and suffer from
homesickness when they are sent out into the
world at 18. Four of the old boys now own and
work a dairy farm in common, and get on better
together than the ordinary quartette of brothers
born. The promoters, finding situations for the
18-year-olds, constantly knock up against the
same difficulty. People, posing as philanthro-
pists, are willing to employ the lads, but at
reduced rates —dirt cheap, in fact. ‘ Dad and
‘ Mum ’ and those behind them don’t see the force
of this. ‘Our boys,’ they say in effect, ‘aren’t
going to be given away with a pound of tea for
the rest of their lives, just because, 10 or 12 years
ago, they fell upon thorns.’
“Some ladies turned up at a deputation to the
Brunswick (Melbourne) council the other day,
and Councillor Fleming raised the fire of the
deputationists by opposing a request for the use
of the barn-like local town hall for ‘ pleasant
Sunday afternoon ’ purposes. He declared that a
lot of new churns had been invading Victoria and
running the place down. Then the daily paper
reports this: —
The female members of the deputation were so nettled
by Or. Fleming's remarks that, although it was nearly
midnight, they waited outside the town hall to hoot him,
while several of them held their hat pins in their hands.
Or. Fleming, however, on finding.that he was hooted on
appearing on the town hall steps, returned into the
council chamber and made hisexit by the back door,
leaving his fair opponents anxiously watching for his
re-appearance. ,
That is how our sex’s impetuosity defeats its
purpose. The real political genius—the man or
woman who makes history—sticks the hat-pin into
the alderman first and hoots afterwards.

The Case of Mrs. McMullen.
«No one knows better than I do the priceless value of good
health,” says Mrs. Andrew McMullen, of Wagga. “It was just
after the birth of my little girl that I started to go down hill. No
matter what I took, I couldn’t get up strength. And, worst of all,
I had some terrible internal disorder that doctors did not seem to
understand. They told me it was caused by the birth of my little
girl, and the pains that shot through me were just like the pains of
labor. All the hot flannels, turpentine, and pain-killers failed
to ease me. A mouthful of food or even a cup of tea brought on an
attack, and nothing gave me relief except vomiting. I couldn’t
help crying out, and I am sure people passing by thought I was
being murdered. My eye-sight began to give way, and black
specks danced before my eyes. It was plain that I had something
terrible the matter with me. To add to my suffering, my nerves
„ave way I was crippled one day by the sharp shooting pains of
Sciatica in my hip, and then Neuralgia started to nag at every nerve
in my face. After this I was seldom out of bed. Often I was
taken with a shivering fit, and then the sharp cutting pains would
dart across my shoulders and pierce my stomach. My bad attacks
lasted for six or eight weeks at a time, and it was no good trying to
get about. When I did get up, I was too dizzy to stand alone. In
fact I was a wreck, and never hoped to be strong and well again.
But at last Mrs. Tom Callaghan, of Gundagai, made me promise to
give Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People a fair trial. She
said they actually made new blood —and that is just what they did
for me. From the very first, they did me good. My appetite picked
up, and I had no more shivering fits because my veins were filled
with warm, red blood. Gradually the Neuralgia and the strange
internal pains stopped, and I felt a different woman. My heart
and nerves and stomach became sound, and in a little while I
enjoyed perfect health. I do all my own house-work now, and
recommend Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills to all my friends as the
grandest woman’s medicine in the world. They saved my life
without a doubt—as everyone in Wagga Wagga knows.”

“F.C.":Let me say the one good word
that can be said for the late Duncan Gillies. He
was clean. He died in his bath, washing himself
to the last.
They say that one member of N.S.W.
Assembly makes an honest pound or two by

celebrities.
Facially, Australian Governor-General-
elect, Northcote, will pass as a double of Tommy
Walker, ex-N.S.W. M.L.A., lately editing a
paper in Westralia.
N.S.W. Premier See, now 58 years of
age, looks ten years younger and feels only 40.
He has been 23 years in Parliament, all the time
for the same district.

men of committees in the Commonwealth : R. W.
Best, in the Federal Senate, and Jonathan Best,
in the Tasmanian Assembly.

to the extent of becoming a member of the
Sydney board of a Victorian life assurance
society—the National Mutual.

is making strenuous efforts to secure the Flinders
(Vic.) seat in the House of Reps. His pretty

A. G Rales is representing the London
Daily News in Macedonia. The dare-devil
Australian has taken on the risky job of watching
things from the rebel side ; every other corres-
pondent is with the Turks, where the climate is
healthier.

Clement Wragge’s last official moments.
He wrote: “This, indeed, is a red-letter day in
the history of the Weather Bureau,’’ in allusion to
news just then received from Norfolk Island. He
broke off to read a letter just delivered It was

Adelaide is raising a statue to explorer
J. McDouall Stuart. Mr. White, of Annandale
(Syd.), has the contract, and expects to have the
effigy ready in seven months. The statue will
be in marble, and is to cost £12,000. Sydney has
commemorated this explorer in a freestone
statuette at the Lands office.
Notwithstanding the old prejudice against
the promotion of minor judges, all three of the
last-appointed State Chief Justices of Australia
have “worked their way up” from inferior
judgeships—Stone, of Westralia ; Dodds, of Tas-
mania; and now Cooper, of Queensland. The
other three older Chief Justices—Darley, Madden
and Way—went straight from the Bar.
Mr. Terry, who used to be leader of the
Independent party in N.S.W. (better known,
perhaps, as the Dependent party, because its
strength was dependent on the degree of
generosity with which Mr. Terry would put up
drinks), is not, so far, mentioned as a Federal
candidate ; but it is understood that if any con-
stituency really wants a statesman to represent it
Mr. Terry is ready to step into the breach.
“Manuka” : A certain Parliament had
a grievous scandal lately. A rather important
official was found just as the House was closing up
for the night, sleeping a large beery sleep in an
ante-room, and the discoverers took his clothes,
hung a necklace of empty bottles on him, and left
him on the premises. A charwoman found the
apparition in a waste-paper basket, and as it had
nothing upon it with which to buy her silence, nO
negotiations were possible.

In the list of officers of the Imperial
Federation League of Victoria appear the names
of Justices Holroyd (president), Hodges (vice-
president), and Casey (executive). Judges should
not meddle in any politics—“imperial” or local—
and it is the duty of the Victorian Legislature to
call on these gentlemen to retire from positions
which might conflict with their judicial duties.
“ B.” writes :
The bequests of the Rev. Dr. Backhaus, amounting to
£120,000, to the Bendigo R.C. church, are now on the eve
of maturity, and gala events are planned. Old Dean
Backhaus’ death raised a monetary war in the far-back
times. A good slice of his fortune went to his house-
keeper. The church’s share was locked up in the hands
of trustees, but, notwithstanding, sums have been from
time to time raised on the strength of expectations under
the will.
Alderman John Pigdon, who was Mayor
of Melbourne in 1877-8, died recently, aged 71
years. Mr. Pigdon was in office when an
imposing envelope arrived, addressed to him. as
Lord Mayor of Melbourne. The Mayor had high
hopes that Melbourne had been distinguished
amongst cities, but the document proved to be
only a begging letter from the Lord Provost of
Glasgow.
Dr. Green, the new Anglican Bishop-elect
of Brisbane, is a sort of ecclesiastical gipsy. He
was English born, but popped up in Melbourne
as a precocious boy. He flashed through Mel-
bourne University, and then through Sydney
University ; next he was a cleric at Ballarat, then
Bishop of Armidale (N.S.W.), then Bishop of
Ballarat (Vic.), and now he is offered the position
of Bishop of Brisbane (Q.). He also tried to get
to the Boer war as chaplain.
M.L. Minister of Customs Mills inadver-
tently referred to his chief as “ Sir Richard John
Seddon ” when yabbering at a country political
razzle lately, and Dick’s democrats are wondering
if there is really anything in it. Most people
labor under the idea, however, that when the evil
day does come, Seddon will be known as Lord
Diggings, or Baron Kybosh, or Count Candle-
grease, or something of that kind.
Mrs. Deakin, the wife of the Common-
wealth Premier, is a tall, stately woman. She is
the daughter of distiller Hugh Junor Brown, an
enthusiastic Spiritist. Mrs. Deakin’s meeting
with Alfred was romantic. Both were mediums —
Alfred was supposed to have the spirit of John
the Baptist in him when “ under control ”; the
lady had that of some ancient prophetess. Kin-
dred spirits in mediumistie work, they married
quite naturally. Now neither fraternises much
with spirits, but old man Brown sticks to it, and
recently gave £5OO towards lectures on Spookdom.
Amongst Maoriland newspaper scribes
Kiug Dick Seddon is a rather unpopular figure.
He has a habit of sending for a reporter, and
keeping him waiting for hours on the doormat.
Some time since one of Auckland’s smartest
journalists was hurriedly summoned to the Czar’s
office. After waiting outside half-an-hour, he
opened the door, put in his head, and asked how
long he was to be kept waiting. Then remarks
flew on both sides as thick as eggs at Vallombrosa.
Some time after, the pressman leased a country
paper, and the leaders of that journal now lash
chunks out of Seddon’s hide. The row which
started on Seddon’s doormat seems as if it would
only end with the death of one party or the other.

Victorian Senate-candidate Findley con-
fesses to being superstitious about the number 13.
He was the 13th President of the Trades Hall
Council, and during his whole term of office he was
out of work. When he was elected for Melbourne
he became the 13th member of the Labor party, and
as such he was expelled for “a seditious libel” in
the Tocsin. Now in the recent Labor plebiscite,
which closed on the 13th of the month, he was
chosen out of the 13 candidates to represent the
party. Seems from this that Findley will be
elected to the Senate by 1300 majority, and hold
his place for 13 years.
Edwin Smith, one of the most popular of
Adelaide citizens, on October 10 celebrated the
fiftieth anniversary of his arrival in S.A. In
Staffordshire, as a young man, he took
a leading part in politics on the side
of Radicalism, and when he landed in
Adelaide he fought on the same side and
had much to do with getting the prin-
ciple of one - man - one - vote into the
original State Constitution. He was
then a poor ironmonger, but he went
into beer, made a heap of money,
became a mayor and a legislator, was
knighted, and embraced Conservatism.
The Jubilee Exhibition building in
Adelaide came through Smith’s
“loyalty” in 1887 ; the Torrens Lake
was a result of his mayoralty over 20
years ago; and he gave the city a
bronze statue, the first it ever pos-
sessed. Also he is president of the
Cricketing Association, the Football
Association, the Commercial Travellers’
Association (once he was head of the
whole race of Australian bagmen) and
of many other organisations. He is reputedly
wealthy, though he has lost much money in
N.S.W. and Queensland pastoral properties.
John Smith, of Winton, aged 7L got lost
recently for a spell of nine days. Was picked up
at the end of that time by a search party not
much the worse, and he refused any aid from hos-
pital or doctor. Was nearly done, he owned up,
but what encouraged him to hang out was the
reflection that “ people in Winton owed him
money, and would get out of paying it if he did
not get home.” Smith had only a pigeon to eat
during the nine days. His debtors are going to
have a hot time, apparently.
“ Joey ” Bell’s retort to ex-Minister
Foxton, in Q. Assembly, that he (Joseph) “bad a
deal more sympathy with the aborigines than the
ousted Minister, but advertised it less,” calls to
mind that the nigger tribe which inhabited Jim-
bour run when the Bell family first sat down on
the station half a century ago, was never “ ordered
off.” Until recently the remnant, including
Charley Samuels, Combo, and some more roamed
peaceably there. Old hands tell of a strong
attachment between old Joshua Peter Bell and
Warabah, an original member of the tribe. When
Warabah pegged out he was buried near the Bell
homestead in shroud and cedar coffin, and Jim
Crowe, the chief mourner, got into the grave and
lay down in it at full length to make sure that it
was large enough to hold old Warabah without
crowding him.

Re par. (8., l/10/’O3) about retiring Westralian Auditor-

Gar liner’s retirement from the James Ministry is not

S. Australia’s third and youngest Judge,
W. H. Bundey (probably the only Australian
Judge, by the way, who was a Mayor before
getting up to the has been specially per-
mitted to retire within the next month or two, six
months before the expiration of the 20 years’ ser-
vice necessary to entitle him to a pension. Mr.
Bundey really had too little confidence in himself
to be a Judge, and he has worried and fretted
himself into chronic ill-health. It was thought
for years that only two men were in the running
for the vacancy—Attorney-General Gordon and
Commissioner Russell, of the Insolvency Court ;
but now John Downer has quitted Federal politics,
and Adelaide is thinking that that may mean some-
thing. It is 20 years since S. Australia had to
appoint a Judge ; but as one of Bundey’s colleagues
is well past 70 and the other close up to it, there
will probably be a rush of appointments soon.

T. B. Clegg, promoted from N.S.W.
Labor and Industries to Friendly Societies;
Department, is an old pressman, a S.M. Herald-

An interesting document was laid on the
table of the House of Representatives on the last
day of the session, showing the number of times
each member had attended. Spruce Myth was
absent no fewer than 147 days out of 215, but no-
one will deny that he was as useless on 68 days as
most men could be in the whole time. His
constituents will probably see that he is absent
215 times in the future. Lang (N.S.W.)
foreign trade representative (F. E. M‘Lean)
only turned up on 74 occasions. George Reid and
his Dry Dog were absent 132 times out of 215.
Groom (Q.), O’Malley (T.), and Mauger (V.) were
the only members who attended every sitting. If
the system were adopted of deducting a day’s pay
for every, absence where no valid excuse was-
forthcoming, Australia would save a proud dea! of
money.

LIFE IN A N.Q. BUSH TOWNSHIP.
Police raiding an opium- seller's hut.
“War E.” : Re the laconicism of the Chow.
Knew a cook of that race who thus summed up
shearers: “Firs’week, all say ‘welly good cook,
him boshter ’; nex’ week begin smell ’em tucker ;
thir’ week tell me go hell, no good; then sack !
Shealer blanky cow ! ”
“ Thula,” about a fowl :
Ohangwas only a dissolute old Langshan rooster, yet
he helped to enliven many a dull day in our slow little
town. Chang was the only surviving chick of a large
mcubatored clutch. Mrs. Publican O’Mara had reared
him tenderly, and he showed his gratitude by following
her around, like a faithful dog ; and would thump all and
?}?hdry of the fowls at feed-times, if they crowded her.
VVith this one exception, Chang waged a vendetta against
all women, and cats and dogs. Against Peggy, the
half-caste general, he bore a heavy grudge. Finding
her one morning at her devotions, scrubbing the passage
he commenced a flank attack, and with a wild gro«gv
rus j l an< f a jump, he sprang over her head, and got
wedged into the scrubbing bucket, where he was at the
mercy of his enemy, who proceeded to baste him with a
J no P- , I rescued him from this undignified position, and
locked him up in the fowl run, where he was supposed to
be always kept. But Chang was seldom a prisoner, for
he had so many admirers and friends amongst the boys
that he was always getting out. And when loose, there
was not a dog in the place that would face O’Mara’s fowl.
Chang wasted no time in tactics ; he would rush at his
opponent with his chest, and if the other animal got
underneath he stayed there till the end of the chapter.
Put Ohang never learnt to discriminate. One Saturday
he was having a quiet dust-bath at the wood-heap when a
stray dog sneaked up to the kitchen door just as a Syrian
basket woman came in through the yard-gate. This
double invasion raised the old fellow’s ire, and with an
angry crow he threw himself at the dog, which to avoid
the onslaught cannoned into the woman and upset her
and her load. After escorting the dog well off the pre-
mises Ohang returned just as the Syrian was stooping to
collect the scattered goods. With a mighty leap he lit
fair between her shoulders and drove her head first into
the dust, and then commenced his usual war-dance upon
the prostrate victim. The now infuriated woman
managed to seize his neck with both hands and com-
menced to belt the atmosphere with his struggling
body. Old Joe, the groom, up to this time had
been an interested spectator, but now, fearing for his
friend s safety, he attempted a rescue. He was swept off
hIS f j., by the mangled remains that were whirled
around the woman’s head. He eventually recovered the
corpse, and Peggy and the cook helped‘to pick up the
fallen treasures, and out of sheer joy they bought a
crown* worth of gew-gaws. The sad news soon circu-
lated, and when I came to dinner the stable yard was full
of boys and disreputable villagers, who had come to pay
their respects to the old warrior. Not a dog was visible,
though. At sunset Joe carried a box down to a hole in
the horse paddock, and there we interred Ohang like a
Christian.
* * • .
Says the Riverina Recorder “ There
is a splendid river at Wilcannia, which is likely
to last through the shearing set.son. ’’ A river
that is likely to last through a shearing season
out there is something to enthuse about. When
it rains, the oldest inhabitant trots down and
puts in a stick at the water’s edge, and when the
shower is over he slides down again to see how
many inches the river hasn’t risen. In flood time
he puts in a great many sticks, following the
water up the bank. By. this means he records
how much it rises or falls in an hour—or a week.
They watch their ephemeral river very closely
about Wilcannia.
• * * *
“ Twistee.” : To me, Nature seems to have
no. mood which distinctly predominates. The
weird melancholy spoken of certainly is there, but
not predominating. A very intimate acquaint-
ance with Nature must, of course, lead us to her
moods—to change when she changes. Therefore
we say “ a child of Nature.” He who thinks that
a weird melancholy or a weird expectancy pre-
dominates must be just a little either way off the
mental balance. I may lay some claim to an
unbiassed view, having lived for the last 10 years
away from cities, noise and strife, and for five
years, off and on, in a tent (as now), a bachelor-
adventurer’s existence. I have studied Nature in
all her moods. She suggests melancholy, expect-
ancy. power, peace, love, hatred—all the virtues
and all the vices, and exaggerates only to impress
the more. She never lies. She bids us hope, she
bids us fear, she bids us laugh, she bids us sorrow
—and she bids us sleep.
A barely probable yarn by “ Yarrum ” ;
A few years ago, a man was driving cow and calf along
the Western, or Gundaroo, shore of Lake George (N.S,W.)
when a native bear fell from his perch on a eucalyptus
and broke the calf’s back. Not long after I was at a
picnic near the same spot, and another bear fell about
60ft. into the tucker basket, breaking all the crockery
and doing considerable damage to the tarts. Since that
a swagman was found near Yass with his neck broken,
but with no other visible marks of violence. As he was
spread under a gum-tree I guessed a bear, but didn’t
like to say so. Since I have known of bears and their
falling habits, I give trees that harbor them a wide berth ;
and I always advise my friends to do the same. My
enemies I urge to go and look up at the interesting
animal.

There are a dozen N.S. Wales sheep-
stations bigger than the “enormous area” of 1000
square miles which John See and the provincial
crowd declare they won’t yield up to Australia for
the purposes of a Federal capital. On Momba,
held by Peter Waite and other S. Australians,
two such areas could be dropped. Tarella, owned
chiefly by Edward Quin, ex-M.L. A. for Wentworth,
and Mount Gipps, on which Broken Hill is built,
could both hold it comfortably, and have plenty
to spare for a few outer suburbs. And you could
almost put the site on that part of Corona which
is not stocked.

“ Yigilans ” about Westralian lands :
A Westralian land boom is the order of the day. “ Land
for nothing in Westralia : 160 acres free selection,” etc.,
etc. These things are not all they seem. Few things
are. The so-called agricultural areas all over Westralia
are “patchy.” There are no districts, as in the other
States, that would make close settlement possible or
large townships probable. A man might take up 1000
acres, and it’s more than likely he would not get 200 acres
suitable for agriculture. In the agricultural areas the
timber is heavy, consisting of jarrah and red-gum
chiefly, and costs from £lO to £2O an acre to clear. The
country far and wide is covered with useless shrubs,
flowers, black-boys and poisonous weeds; consequently
you rarely see stock depasturing on large areas unat-
tended. Sheep are shepherded and paddocked at night
There is no doubt, however, that when this heavily-
timbered country is cleared it will be found suitable for
fruit-growing. Vegetables, with the aid of manure, will
also thrive.
The pick of Westralian land from an agricultural point
of view is undoubtedly the Katanning and Narrogin
districts (south-east), bar, of course, the settled districts
of York and Northam. Cereals, with the aid of super-
phosphate and a drill, are grown here with good
results, as much as 40 bushels to the acre being
reaped. But no cereals will grow without manure.
The same remarks apply to the south-west. As an
illustration, there is no township from Perth to
Bunbury, a distance of 115 miles. The best land
is from Bunbury to Riverstown, some 60 miles.
You might get a good bit of from 500 to 600
acres here. The rainfall is heavy—from 30 to
40 inches. The land is hardly suitable for
cereals. A patch here and there, and then
miles of sandy useless country is the order
of things all over Groperland. Mixed farming
one scarcely ever sees.
Grazing as known in the eastern States is
not found here. There are few natural grasses,
except where the country has been ring-barked
and burnt. By burning the undergrowth a thin
kind of spear grass grows, but only a few inches
high, and of little nourishment. Grazing is, there-
fore, carried on in a very primitive manner, a man
with three or four thousand sheep being styled a
“ squatter.”
As regards land for [settlement: The eyes have
already been picked out of the country within the
immediate neighborhood of the railways, and you have
already to go miles back. To use a mining term, this
is essentially a poor man’s field. On the Nor’-west line
there is some fairland, but it belongs to a syndicate.

“ Scrip ” : A unique specimen of
the porker—a half-grown one, and a
greyhound in appearance—is owned by
a farmer at Warea (Taranaki, M.L.).
Piggy scraped up an acquaintance with
the farm horses, and accompanied them in their
jaunts as a matter of course. Its first notable
performance was a spin of 14 miles behind a
trap, and despite occasional bursts of fast trotting
it couldn’t be shaken off. This is the first example
of the express pig, or long-distance swine, or
flying porker that has come under my notice.

“ Cecil P.” . “ Why do the heathen rage
so furiously and the people imagine a vain
thing ? ” asked the Psalmist, some years ago.
There are various reasons. Here is one of ’em.
Ah Chow, the storekeeper, ordered some bill-heads
from the local newspaper office. On getting
them, he tore up to inquire frantically “Whaffor?
whaffor?” He further remarked, “No blanky
goo’! No f-e-e-ah ! ” Inquiry showed that the
haughty heathen objected to plain “Ah Chow”
on the bills and wanted “Mr. Ah Chow.” I’m
awaiting developments. If only I could see Sum
Fat, Esq., or Dam Foo, J.P., I feel that, young
and beautiful though I be, I could sink into the
silent tomb without a blessed murmur.

Since the rains, the brown sandy plains
around Broken Hill have become clothed in a
beautifully green carpet, plentifully besprinkled
with flowers. In some places, the grass is nearly
2ft. high. Wild turkeys, pigeons, duck—of late
years only a memory —are again appearing near
the town. Where they come from it is hard to
say. They must have been hibernating under-
ground.

“J. 0. G.” : Travelling near Ashford
(N.S.W.), some time back, I was passing through a
cocky’s estate, and on top bar of his gate, on the
main road, found this written in red paint:—
Smiled on from heaven and blessed by fate
Is he who shuts the cocky’s gate.
Surely this is a humorous departure from the
orthodox “ Please shut the gate—poison laid.”
THE HANDS OF VENUS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I was welcome in a palace when the ball was at my
feet;
I was petted in a garden, and my triumph was complete ;
But for me, above the alleys, there for ever shone a star—
Where the third-class public-houses and the dens of
Venus are.
Henry Lawson (8., 24/9,’03).
Ah, my candid, worthy brother, you’re a stranger to
deceit,
I have met you in the palace “ when the ball was at your
feet; ”
When you sipped the living nectar from the very choicest
bloom,
In the garden of the palace and within the gilded room—
In the garden of the palace,
From the figured glass and chalice,
When you sipped the living nectar from the very choicest
bloom.
I was born within a palace—bred within a realm of light,
And was barred from lusty love-dens and the tempting
haunts of night;
I was pampered, praised, and petted, and was shown a
path to tread,
That would lead me, so folks told me, from this world of
reeking dead—
That would lead me, so they told me,
To where angels would behold me,
To a bright and heavenly kingdom that is free from
reeking dead.
But in spite of all the praying and the pious palace role,
And the lengthy exhortations ’bout my future and my
soul,
There was something ever gnawing, gnawing, drawing
me apart—
Ever gnawing, ever drawing at my very inmost heart,
’Twas the hell-born hands of Venus
That so often come between us,
And the bright paths we have trodden, that were drawing
at my heart;
So I left the dear old palace, wandered forth into the
night,
Shunned the old friends of my boyhood and the faintest
ray of light—
Launched out on a sea of darkness, plunged into the
blackest cloud,
Sought to quell my craving hunger, ’mid the ever
shameless crowd;
Sought to quell my craving hunger
In the days when I was younger—
Plunged into a sea of darkness 'mid the ever shameless
crowd.
I have struggled, and I’ve striven, and I’ve tried in vain
to rise,
But the people of the palace will not harken to my cries;
I am kicked, and cuffed, and scoffed at, and am trodden
under, feet,
And I’m longing for the garden where your triumph was
complete ;
Oh ! I’m longing for the palace,
From a heart grown cold and callous,
Ever longing for the palace where my lot was not com-
plete.
I have quitted the dark alleys and the dingy dens cf
love,
And have found a situation on a platform just above ;
And I mean to struggle upward through the sunshine
and the rain.
Till within the gay old palace I am safely housed again ;
Aye 1 within the gay old palace,
When our hearts are no more callous,
We may drink the sparkling vintage, and be honored
once again.
YIO.
HOPEFUL.
TO THE SOUTHERN CROSS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Hail! glorious cross which shines above
This land of drought!
Worthy art thou of all our lore.
There is no doubt
You are the bright, grand Queen of Night.
(Mate, point it out!)
The other stars which shine on high
Seem little fit
As tenants of the far-off sky
By you to sit 1
Their brightness seems to fade and die.
(Mate, which is it ?)
Yea ; other lights are poor things that
Your beauty mars;
Bright is your glow. I doff my hat
To your bright bars.
(Then find—Great Scot! I’m looking at
Some other stars!)
N.S.W.
RIVERINA.
THE LAMBKINS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Gambolling out on the grass to-day—
Life a pleasure beyond complaint ;
Gaily frisking in sportive play—
This is the future the writers paint.
Perishing out on the plains to-day,
Faintly bleating their final bleat ;
Birds of black and an icy spray—
This is the scene that the trampers meet.
vio.

“J.”: Travelled in a train, t’other day, with
a huge woodchopper, who was in convul-
sions on account of a back tooth being
oil a razzle-dazzle. A believer in homceo-
pathy, sitting opposite, offered to
cure him, and carefully placed on
the woodchopper’s extensive palm
two small white specks, that looked
like two bits of manna in the wil-
derness. The chopper examined
them keenly, then looked round
the carriage suspiciously. A
fool in the corner giggled
—the sight of those two
tiny pills on that
billiard-table of palm
was ludicrous, and
so was the idea of so
littlemedicine curing
so much woodchop-
per. The next
minute the homceo-
pathist and the wood-
chopper were strug-
gling on the floor of
the car, the woodman
endeavouring to put
the pills down the
good Samaritan’s throat.«
Wepulled him off and sat on
him until we convinced him the pills
were not “hundreds and thousands,”
and that the proceedings weren’t a
parody.

to the pickel bottles. The Families of your Farming
people that have been making money out of the land for
30 years. Also your families of the local Freehold

all with the land act. They seems like an associated
gang of drunken Land Sharks dear sir There is people
in this district that are not able to ride wild horses that
were working on railways mines and Road contracks
very much annoyed and inconvenieced with this Barb
Wire fences. The Families of Your Farming People of
this district once the common are erecting.
Also the majority of those People are relatives
of very Ferocious Drunkards. Dear sir This
grog drinking Horse Breaking Farming and Grazing-
People of this district besides being Drunkards are ter-
rible liars and Backbiters. In fact they spend the greater
part of their time in poor People places playing Euchre
for a Few Shillings or Travelling round collecting Sub-
scriptions. Also those Farmers that are paying income
tax are complying severely of some of Your Land Proprie-
tors of their Families low moral character and Ferocious
Disposition, and are far too ferocious when sober without
erecting those Barb wire fences. When your Farming
families of this District gets intoxicated they seem a very
treacherous People and are a very grasping covetous lot
of People when Sober and seems to think People should
work for them almost for charty, and the Bread they
Bakes is very inferior and Corn Beef very inferior also.
Also very inferior Posts your People that have draught
Horses are Stapling their 3'Barb Wires on.

HISTORICAL SKETCH OF THE DAYS WHEB MISSIONARIES
WERE WELCOMED TO FIJI.
In answer to fervent-prayer the missionary schooner arrives.
my pitch and side-tracked my enterprise. I tried
to grow large, vigorous, and .prolific rabbits.
Hoped, by careful breeding, to produce a kind
having young 24 times a year. In two years
figured to have 142,000,000 long-woolled rabbits,
and to shear them four times a year. The Rabbit
Acts stopped my scheme. I’m leaving this
socialism-blighted country, and taking my capital
to a land where private enterprise has more free-
dom from restrictive legislation.
* * * *
On the 25th October a fishing-cutter of
nine tons was rammed by a shark 20ft. long in
Investigator Straits (S.A.), and sunk. The occu-
pants escaped in a dingy. The force of the im-
pact apparently killed the shark, otherwise it would
probably have rammed the dingy also.

SPORTING WAITER : “ Yessir. Boiled mutton,

hurdle; cheese bolted; stewed tripe still run-
ning, sir.”
The life of a red gum block when buried
seems to be longer than hitherto suspected.
Workmen tearing up Flinders Lane West (Mel-
bourne) lately discovered, two feet below the
surface, several logs of this timber, which had
evidently been used as a foot-bridge in the long
ago, when “the Lane” was a mere track through
a paddock and Elizabeth-street a damp, snake-
haunted gully. The logs were still so sound that
they had to be sawn through.
* * * *
Old North Australian traveller Christie,
moving along the N.T. coast lately, sat down to
see a gory conflict. It was on the Finniss Plains,
where representative warriors from four different
tribes had gathered to wipe out some old scores.
A fifth tribe was interested, and was to also have
the chance of having its collective neck dislocated ;
but it was delayed on the road, and while the
other four waited they smoked, and smoked, and
smoked, and finally the horrible discovery was
made that the tobacco had run out. That broke
them up ; a common disaster had drowned their
differences, and they faded out of the landscape
without breaking a head.

Vic. policeman is fond
of toutihg for drinks,
and, the other day,

■ party, who shouted

had to bo locked up. This was hard luck for Mr.

denied a final cup, he took his prisoner out of the
lock-up, accompanied him to the nearest pub., had
another at his expense, then locked him up again !
The Phil May Fund.
Even after the 15 years which have elapsed since
the departure from Australia of that gifted artist,
the late Phil May, there are many here who
vividly remember not only his unrivalled powers
as a delineator of character in black and white,
bub his genial nature, and the charming
personality of his hospitable and ever-devoted
wife. Genius, as a rule, has no time to look after
its domestic affairs; and Phil May was no excep-
tion to this, for he died leaving his widow practi-
cally penniless. A movement to raise a fund to
buy an annuity for Mrs. May has been started in
London under the auspices of a committee includ-
ing editor Burnand, of Punch, playright Pinero,
and Shannon, the celebrated portrait-painter.
To assist it, The Bulletin, which will start the
local list with a donation of =£2oo, asks for sub-
scriptions from Australia. Any money sent to
this office for the fund will be duly acknowledged
and remitted to the proper quarter in London.
The Phil May Sale.
The Melbourne sale of Phil May “ originals ” was
marked at the outset by a charge for admission, a
two-edged protective innovation which cut both
ways, but doubtless cut deepest in the right
direction. Gemmell-Tuckett’s art gallery, where
the drawings had been displayed to advantage
what time the multitude rolled up in hundreds to
the show, is fully large enough for any ordinary
picture-sale gathering, yet not sufficiently
spacious to accommodate an extraordinary
rush of sightseers. Under the circumstances
the remarkable popularity of the Phil May
exhibition was alarming. Something bad to
be done to safeguard the sale from the
threatened mobbing, and as the event had been
fixed for the Thursday in Hospital Sunday week,
the suggested policy of a shilling admission fee
was adopted, and a Melbourne Hospital collector
took the money at the door. The result was a
satisfactory attendance of buyers who appreciated
their elbow-room, whilst the rest of the world and
its wife stayed carefully away.
“ On the Melbourne Block” was bought for £2l
by Allan and Co. “Jumps and Jim Jams ”at £l5,
together with a political cartoon subject (£5) and
“Lock and Key” (£6), went to Melbourne Art
Gallery. Bidders showed pluck, tempered by
artistic discrimination, and several of the smaller
things fetched substantial prices, but the large
portrait drawings went well, or otherwise,
according to circumstances over which the artist
had no control. That of the late Baronet
Clarke was secured for “the family” for £8 10s.
Chief Justice Madden and Pan ton, P.M., realised
a “fiver” apiece, Bard of Works Fitz-Gibbon
being the purchaser of these and other “ identity ”
subjects, whilst his own portrait was included
among the several lots that went to Theodore
Fink. Melbourne Grammar school got its old
friend, Dr. Bromby (a speaking likeness), for £5,
and the historic picture of Peter Lalor went a
bargain to a respectable bid, but for some
of the most talked-of portraits the com-
petition was very weak. They proved to
be the surprises of the sale, going “dirt cheap”
at a pound, or two, or three at the most. L. L.
Smith and David Syme fell below the dignity of a
“fiver.” Old Jimmy Mcßain touched the mini-
mum “ quid ” merely because the original happens
to be dead and buried. What’s the odds 1 In the
case of these portraits the surviving local notoriety
or utter forgottenness of the original matters
nothing to the purchaser, whereas the fact that
Phil May’s name is attached to each drawing
gives it a value which will soon be very evident to
everyone who knows aught about the world of
black-and-white art.

W.JiO. (Wellington): Exports sbould .be sufficient to
pay for imports and vton-pay interest on foreign
debt (if any), also to pay something off the debt itself. If
our imports are greater than our exports it may mean •
(1) That our country has money invested abroad and that
the surplus imports represent interest paid to us—in that
case we are rich. (2) Or that our country has money
invested abroad and that the surplus imports represent
interest paid to us and also part of the principal being
returned —in that case we are rich, but are getting
poorer. (3) Or it may mean that we are borrowing
money abroad to pay for the surplus of imports—in
which case the surplus shows that we are on the road to
bankruptcy. A surplus of exports may mean that we are
investing money abroad and growing rich, or that we are
deep in debt and the surplus is required to pay-interest
on the debt. And if the surplus isn’t big enough to pay
the interest, then we are getting still deeper in debt....

It is alleged that the manor of Kenner-
leigh, belonging to the Northcote family, of which
the coming Governor-General is a member, was
won by the ancestor of the Northcotes in a game
of cards from the previous owner, one Dowrisb.
Few men have a better knowledge of the
working of the Lands Acts than has Land Agent
W. N. Willis, of 21 Bligh-street. Payment by
results is not only reasonable but also novel.*
The fifth Duke of Richmond, who died
recently aged 85, was descended from diaries II.
and the French spy Louise de Keroualle, Duchess
of Portsmouth, and for years practically Queen of
England. The duke received a pension of £19,000
till 1886, when it was commuted for a sum of
nearly half a million sterling. Forneron’s
interesting life of Louise de Keroualle, which
frightened the duke into seeking the commutation,
sums up thus: —“ The British Parliament, which
thinks .£1200 a year enough to relieve the
distressed families of authors, artists, scientists,
and other benefactors of their country, continues
to pay the Duke of Richmond £19,000 a year in
virtue of the seoret services rendered to Louis
XIV. by his' ancestress Louise de Keroualle.”
While making things easy for her descendants,
this daughter of the horse-leech did not forget)
herself. In one year she drew from the English
Treasury £136,668. Not satisfied with this, she
trafficked in royal pardons to rich convicts.
Louise reached the age of 85. Like others of her
species she became very pious as an old woman.
OPEN SESAME.—Hunyadi Janos Water is
the “open sesame’’ of the human organism.
Speedy, sure, and gentle, it fulfils every indication
in health and disease. It is a therapeutical
arsenal in itself. Indispensable to millions of
human beings all over the world.*
The following verses dropped in from
Queensland the other day. They are published
on general principles. The name of the lady who
wrote them is suppressed. She may be a nice
girl, and it would be a sin to arouse ill-feeling
against her:
It is often I do see
The Limb of the Law
Astrolling with a Lady fair
Is she his Decoy ;
Is she his future Bride.
Or is she his prisoner.
Oh the Bobby and his Girl.
Some would be wise ones,
Shakes the Head and ponders ;
Looks rather strange,
To see Miss Twenty, strolling ;
With the Local hobby
A Hint goes round and says ;
She will be betrayed with a Babe in Anns.
Oh the bobby and his girl.
Let us hope that in Truth ;
She will be his Bride.
His Prisoner for life and Death
No more to flirt
And Break the Hearts of men
Oh the Bobby And his girl
And when joined in Holy wedlock
May she be a model wife
Of Purity and Justice
Help the Limb of the Law
To Perform His Duty
In his Beat upon the street
Oh the bobby and his girl.
Let the bobby show
The scandelmongers vile,
His intentions are pure and
Above suspicion of Anything vile
Like a Libertine
Oh the bobby and his girl.

“ Gr. H.” : Herr Carl Schmidt occasionally
takes a turn at navigating the bier-shop of which
he is the proprietor; but except in very stormy
weather Mrs. Carl walks the bridge and makes up
the daily financial dead reckoning. Recently the
advent of a pocket-edition Carl necessitated Herr
Schmidt taking several watches in succession; and
at seven bells the other evening a tired publican
quarrelled vehemently with one of the house’s best
customers, who, being temporarily insolvent,
wanted a drink on tick. Carl, senior, repulsed
the thirsty one thus : “ Ach, py crigey, no; af I
drust a mans elefenteen timeness pefore und he
bay ub dat is alride ; pud af I drust heem again-
ness und he doand bay, den dat is all wrong, und
I go de insolvency gourt mit. Ach, py crigey,
no ; I tole you af you aind got some money I aind
got some bier ; und af I aind got some bier I aind
got some brofit, und af I aind got some brof ’’
The thirst swore violently just here, though, and
left, and the disquisition broke off through its
own weight.

Queensland’s is the second Chief Justice
Cooper that Australia has had. The first was the
first C. J. of S. Australia —in fact, S. Australia had
only one Judge in those days. Sturt named
Cooper’s Creek, in Centralia, after him.
Queensland’s new Chief Justice, Cooper,
is a couple of inches taller than Dailey, and prob-
ably the longest of Australian Supreme Court
Judges. His list of death sentences is also the
longest.

POLITICAL
POINTS
She has come. A “Professional engage-
ment ” advt. in Melbourne Age :
TjiEDERAL Elections.—Lady, stirring political
x speaker, would organise and address women’s

The effrontery of Philp. Having had the
management of Queensland for the Lord knows
how long, he now calmly rises and tells Morgan
that associates to Judges are unnecessary luxuries
and should be rubbed off the pay-sheet!
Sydney Telegraph devoted a leader last
week to considering what England should do with
its embarrassing increase of wealth so as to avoid
being smothered in gold. ■ Well, it might feed the
12,000,000 citizens who are always on the verge of
starvation, for one thing.
The electors of Swan Hill (Vic.) heckled
Yes-No Reid so much at his campaign-opening
speech that at last he got his wool off. “I
suppose you will deny even the drought next ? ” he
bellowed. Promptly came the. reply—“lf you
talk much about it we may.”
About the last possible touch of comedy
in N.S.W. politics was the appeal of the See
Government to Carruthers to postpone his motion
of censure for a week so that Minister Crick might
go to the Cup. Some day a general election will
be postponed till a Minister gets back from the
dog-fight.
Tom Rose, M.L.A., addressing a N.S.W.
country audience, spoke a full hour in glowing
eulogy of his prospective boss, Carruthers, and
on sitting down stated he was willing to answer
questions. A hairy wayback rose slowly and
asked: “Could you tell me, sir, why it is you
have such a deadly down on Misther Caroothers ? ”
N.S.W. ASSEMBLY: A SUGGESTION.
The side whose representative is licked has its Bill .thrown out.
G. H. Reid, whom S.M. Herald stages as
Australia’s greatest master of platform repartee :
A Voice : Soft soap a penny a pound
Mr. Reid : Yes, that gentleman’s policy would make it
twopence per pound, and perhaps he would not be able to
afford an annual bath at that price.
Very like George Perry’s Domain wit.
The Morgan Ministry in Q. is already
beginning to find the inconvenience of coming
into office on an anti-taxation issue. The finances
are in a woeful state, but the Government is
pledged to introduce no further taxation this
Parliament. As a way out it intends to continue
the unjust and oppressive poll-tax for a year. It is
a bad way out.
M.L. Premier Seddon forecasts legislation
to abolish the Legislative Council in that State.
Yet Mr. Seddon’s Council is a comparatively
harmless collection of fossils, and rarely ventures
on anything but a timid grumble at his progressive
legislation. If Seddon had to deal with the
Victorian Council he would probably roast it and
feed it to the Maoris.
“Nemo ” writes from Swan Hill (Vic.) :
The Freetrade Circus, with G. H. Reid, the lightning
change artist, as chief performer, struck Swan Hill on
Monday night last. Admission being free there was a
good attendance. About 8.15 George waddled on to the
platform, followed by his chairman, the local Sawbones.
This was the signal for shriek No. 1; the picture was too
funny—dignity and impudence personified. The recep-
tion was a very poor one ; with the exception of a few
special creations who consider themselves high society,
there was no reception at all, and Reid froze consider-
ably. He was not taken seriously, and it hurt him. He
battled away for two hours, raved about the dear old
mother land, wobbled this way, wriggled that, and
finally sank into his chair, a wet and flabby heap. If he
doesn’t meet with a better reception elsewhere than he
got in this little Mallee township, he will have to bury
that Dry Dog again.
Sydney Telegraph overdid its lie when
it tried to persuade the public that at George
Reid’s meeting in Melbourne only three hands
were held up for the Protective policy of the
Deakin Government and all the rest of the
audience cheered frantically for Reid and his
importing policy. Only a silly ass of a paper
runs things to that length.
The chief work done by the S.A. Parlia-
ment in the session just closed was the passing of
a consolidating Lands Bill which has been
promised annually for 10 or 12 years. It codifies
countless conflicting Acts and amendments which
have long been the despair of everybody except
the lawyers. S.A. has now a very decent land
law and a great deal of terribly bad land.
Federal Labor leader J. C. Watson con-
veys a stinging rebuke to N.S.W. State Labor
party when he points out that, whilst refusing to
vote for the nationalising of 1000 square miles of
N.S.W. territory by the Australian Government,
that party made no protest against handing oyer
100 times as great an area in the Western Division
to private co.’s—mostly absentees—for 40 years.
The S.M. Herald country correspondent
doing his little best to earn a shilling or two over
the Glen Innes election :
North Guyra, Thursday.—Great satisfaction is ex-
pressed here at Mr. Thomas’s victory.
On polling day the Guyra folk, to the number of
50, went to vote, and the majority of them voted
for Thomas’s opponent, Hay.
Hurley, M.L.A., wants N.S.W. to sus-
pend the Public Works Committee for 12 months,
or take steps that no more proposals be placed
before it for the present. This is in view of the
scarcity of loan money, and is another evidence of
the fine old crusted belief that the country can’t
be expected to do anything except out of loans,
and that when loans are scarce progress must dry
up.
A clause in Seddon’s new Maoriland
Licensing Bill provides that all liquor seized in
no-license districts is to be handed to hospital
boards for the use of patients. This raises four
questions : —(1) What kind of patient will be able
to stand the home-made liquor which is sure to
come into vogue? (2) Will the patients be able to
get through all the liquor? (3) Will there be a
ruinous increase of patients ? (4) Will the hospital
doctor be sober occasionally with all that grog
pouring in ?
“ Burglar Bill ” : Never heard of a criminal
on the drop returning thanks to John Haynes or
anyone else who has striven to abolish capital
punishment. He generally thanks the governor
of the gaol, because he sent him in a
big breakfast on the morning
of the execution, and ex-
presses his high opinion of
the warder and the hang-
man. Then the chaplain
decides that he passed away
in a beautifully affable frame
of mind.
The necessity for the Bill lately rejected
in Victoria, providing that every married man
making a will shall be compelled to leave at
least one-third of his property to his widow, was
shown the other day by the testament of an
ex-policeman at Ballarat. He bequeathed £3400
to charities, and only 12s. a week to Mrs.
Policeman, and £5O to -his daughter. Deceased
was a kind of crank who used to write at great
length to the newspapers.
“F.C.”: If appearances don’t deceive
more than usual, one can forecast the death of the
Deakin Ministry to the day. Barton’s successor
promises to re-introduce the Arbitration Bill with
the State servants left outside its provisions as
before—the same abject grovel to Iceberg Irvine
repeated. And as the new House will be even
more democratic than the last, Deakin will there-
by sign his political death-warrant, and Kingston,
Higgins and Co. will arrive.
So long as magistrates continue to fine
shipowners (through the captains) £5 every time
they let a Chow or Jap sailor or fireman escape,
these will continue to make a farce of the Re-
striction Act by slipping over the ship’s side “ un-
beknownst. ” The captain almost always has from
£lO to £2O of Ah Fat’s or Yoshi warra Hakidado’s
wages in hand, and as that person knows better
than to sue for them, the skipper can pay the fiver
and then make a profit on the deal.
From Sydney Telegraph’s account of G.
H. Reid’s tour of the Victorian provinces:
A charge of two shillings had been made for admission
to the dress circle and the stalls.
Mr. Reid said he did not believe in this system of
charging for admission to political meetings.
Reid’s mind is so incurably crooked that he must
Yes-No even on the question of charging for his
circus.. He “does not believe in charging for
admission,” but he charges for admission.
PHEW!
It is predicted that Victoria will return
more Laborites to the next Australian Parlia-
ment than any other State. Victoria, since the
advent of Tom Mann, has become honeycombed
with Labor leagues, and even the Argus admits
that Labor is “ finely organised.” Also the ramp-
ant foolishness and brute violence of the Irvine
Government has done even more for the demo-
cratic cause than Mann has done. When the num-
bers go up that “paid agitator,” R. S. Walpole,
will almost certainly be an astonished individual.
As usual, the “ poor fanner ” cry, which
was used to cut down the land-tax in S. Australia,
turns out a hollow fraud. On the present assess-
ment the farthing would bring in £28,000, of
which £ll,OOO would be paid by rich landlords,
owninggsstates valued at £5OOO and upwards, and
another £6OOO by owners of land inside corpora-
tion boundaries. All that the “poor farmer”
would pay would be the small balance. So the
farmer was used as a stalking horse to save the
rich landlord, and the State’s finances once more
were sent to chaos.
A half-hearted attempt was made this year
to cut down the N.S.W. Speaker’s salary ; but it
fizzled out. All the same, the N.S.W. Speaker is
lucky compared with his brethren. At present
he gets £1370 a year, as against £llOO drawn by
the Speaker and President of the Australian
Parliament. The Queenslander draws £lOOO, but
the amount will probably come down (it has been
attacked pretty well every year); Jenkin Coles,
of S. Australia, receives £600; the Tasmanian a
mere £350 ; the Victorian £lOOO ; and the paternal
Lee Steere, of Westralia, £6OO.
Truly the way of the economist is hard.
The Australian Government, as a mode of saving
money, proposes to make the country postmasters
assist in the general election. Whereupon arises
the. wail of those who have been used to collect
their two or three guineas a day for receiving a
few dozen votes. There are 1800 polling booths
in New South Wales alone, and though everybody
is agreed that the Australian Government should
be economical, it is remarkable how unanimously
“Enquirer” and “ Pro Bono Publico ” are rising
up to demonstrate that this is the wrong economy.
“ W.W.” writes : *
The Bulletin, being a true disciple of Protection, may
be able to inform me how, it is that so much imported
fruit is consumed in the Commonwealth. Worked on a
sheep-run many years ago that was owned by a Scotch
marmalade-canner. The station store was at all times
well-stocked with marmalade made in Scotland. Now,
I am reliably informed that neither lemons, oranges, nor
sugar can thrive in the vicinity of Glasgow. 1 have
camped with jumbucks and cattle beside orchards in four
of the Australian States, and have seen f ■ uit lying rotting
under the orchard trees, and at the s:; me time comrades
spreading London-made jam on their damper.
Well, it’s partly lack of proper Protection, and
partly lack of proper sense. If the Government
gave adequate Protection, to keep the foreign
article out, the sense to use the local stuff would
soon come.
Among the pitiably small matters with
which N.S.W. Assembly amused itself last week
were these :
A question whether the Australian Government had
sacked a Post Office clerk.
Questi n whether the Premier was aware that a certain
individual had selected plate or dishes, or something, for
use in Government House.
Further consideration of a proposal that a committee
of nine members, including the Premier, should sit to
consider whether an unsuccessful candidate who stood
for Parliament 14 years ago, and got only 92 votes, should
have his lost deposit refunded.
That a committee of nine members, including the
Premier, should sit to consider whether a policeman had
made a mistake in arresting a man named Anderson.
That there should be laid on the table all documents
connected with the extension of a back street in an
obscure bush hamlet.
That a committee of nine members should sit to inquire
why Jones’s mine at Adelong isn’t working.
That a committee of nine members, including the
Treasurer, should sit to consider the reason for the dis-
missal of a railway-repairer.
That a committee of ten members, including the
Premier, should sit to consider whether a messenger was
unjustly sacked.
That a committee of ten members, including the Minis-
ter for Works, should sit to consider a laborer
at Fitzroy Dock ought to have been sacked but wasn’t.
That a committee of nine members, including the
Premier, should sit to consider whether a certain public
official who had written a book on vegetables had therein
borrowed another man’s ideas on vegetables, without
acknowledgment.
N.S.W. used to have a Parliament. Now it has
got a sort of mental marine store and banana
barrow.
. The ineffable impudence of Yes-No Reid !
Part of his Melbourne speech was taken up with
an attack on the Federal Government for its
“delay and hesitancy” in sending a Common-
wealth Contingent to S’Africa. But the one
bright spot in Reid’s career —the one occasion on
which he showed a real sagacity and a faculty
of seeing further ahead than the miserable
little advantage of the moment —was when
he more than delayed and hesitated, positively
refused, as N.S.W. Slate Premier, to send troops
to harry the Boers. The war had just broken out,
and half a-dozen screaming Premiers —Kingston,
sad to say, among them —wished to despatch con-
tingents and warships and kerosene and other
munitions of war. They urged Reid, as Premier
of the most populous State, to offer a contingent,
but urged in vain. He minuted on one of their
frenzied telegrams—
Put by for the present. I have come to the opinion
that the sympathy of the Australian colonies with the
legitimate desires of the British inhabitants of the
Transvaal has already been made sufficiently manifest. A
difference between the British people and the people of
the Transvaal scarcely calls for displays of patriotism at
this end of the world—the strength of the position being
all on the side of Great Britain.
Very shortly afterwards Reid went back on that
manly and sagacious statement, and he feebly ex-
plained that he was all the while nourishing a
great thirst for Boer gore, but refused to offer a
contingent “ for fear of irritating the Boers! ”
Row he has the unspeakable gall to pose as a
frantic Jingo hurt to the heart by the fact that
Barton didn't rush with enough haste to embrue
his hands in Boer blood.

M.L. Premier Seddon has put his second"
ballot fad in the Electoral Act Amendment Bill.
Like all electoral systems that carry elaborate
frills and flounces, the second ballot really isn’t
worth while. The present system may not be
scientific perfection ; but neither is the other—it
simply brings together the two men who, in the-
first round, had the biggest personal following. If:
there were a few more Protectionists than Free-
traders in a district, but if there were six Protec-
tionist candidates and only two Freetraders, the-
second ballot would most likely be between the-
two Freetraders, even though the Protectionist?
voters in the aggregate outnumbered the Free-
traders. Seddon’s plan, therefore, makes a longer
sum and leaves the answer just about where it
was.
A THREATENED MILLENNIUM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ If I get into power."— G. 11. Reid.
If I get into power
Some fine things you will see—
In less than half an hour
The times will brighter be.
The sun that seems to pine,
For lack of fire and wit,
Will on my eyeglass shirte,
And take new light from it.
The great news he will tell
To smiling hill and plain,
And say “ The world goes well—
George Reid’s on top again.”
The black will turn to white,
To sweet will turn the sbur,
And every day be bright—
When I get into power.
Good things will all be free,
Champagne as cheap as rum,
And rum as cheap as tea
In My Millennium.
Then cast your cares aside,
Put in your coat a flower,
Let smaller matters slide
And Get Me into Power.
C.R.

SYDNEY.
The members of N.S.W. Public Service
'lender Board did not lose much time in circum-
venting Parliament, which lately decided that no
fees were to be paid to the lafge-salaried officers
for Tender Board work done during office hours.
The very next meeting was called for 4.30 p.m.,
which was outside regular office hours, so that the
fee could be collected. The regular hour used to
be 2.30, and properly so, for the office staff is not
then required to work overtime while the Board
is sitting. The fee is £1 per sitting, and a return
of time worked after hours in return for the £1
will be awaited with interest.

The warlike little Jap. is declining rapidly
in physique. Last year only 44 per cent, of the
conscripts called could be passed by the doctors.
This doesn’t look healthy in view of the expected
set-to with Russia. No nation should go to war
on crutches, or while it is suffering from rickets.

and Fiji; and Agencies and Correspondents throughout

The Bank allows interest on fixed deposits ; collects for
its customers dividends of public companies, and interest
on debentures; and invests money on their behalf in
public securities in the Colonies or Great Britain ; under-
takes the agency of other banks ; issues drafts, circular
notes and letters of credit, and conducts all customary
banking business.

Many tyre troubles arise through ignorance of
the proper degree of inflation to the pneumatic tyre.
It should be pumped to such a degree of hardness that,
when the machine has got its full load, on the portion
that touches the road, it should show little or no
flattening. If the tyre shows an appreciable flattening
it should be further inflated. A tyre that is partially
inflated rolls in the rim, and when greasy roads are
struck there is a grave risk of side-slip. Moreover, a
tyre that is continually ridden in a soft condition will
soon wear out, for, by rolling, wear of the fabric is set up,
which sooner or later causes the cover to burst. “ Pump
hard ” therefore is a good motto for the cyclist. In such
tyres as the Dunlop this air pressure will enable the
wheels to glide over obstacles without sh'.cks or undue
vibration.*

according to its need.
When the brain begins

the nerves become
sensitive, it is a sign
that there is a loss'of
the nutrition contained
in the food eaten, and
this loss is, in general,
due to disease of the
stomach, and its allied
organs. Dr. Morse’s

the digestion and
assimilation of food.
It is not the quantity

amount of nourish-
ment which is retained
that benefits the system. If from any cause the
nourishment in the food is not extracted, the
whole body suffers. Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
