SPORTING NOTIONS.
Lord Cardigan s success in Melbourne Cup
means that Mo. will have to pay out freely, albeit it was
such a fine betting race and so many were backed for
large sums that the Dooks, as a whole, cannot possibly lose.
Certainly none other of the Sydney nags carried so much
money, but there were one or two Victorians who would
have brought disaster to the ring if thev had won and a
couple of New South Wales reps.—Ossian and Oblivion
(both owned and heavily backed by Minister for Lands
Crick) must surely have (had either got home) caused the
disappearance of at least two or three of the
raucous-voiced. Owner and hreeder of Lord
Cardigan is Mr. Mayo, of Maitland. The na<*
in appearance, takes after his dam, Lady Trenton.
He is light framed. As a two-year-old, he ran second in
a Hawkesbury Park Stakes, second in A.J.O. Novice
Handicap (one mile) to Highflyer, and second to Famous
in Warwick Farm Flying Stakes (one mile). Twice
this season w.f.a. races fell to him. First he
beat Cyanide and Sir Leonard, and then Wake-
ful, Postillion, Sir Leonard and The Victory, with-
out an effort. Famous only beat hinr by a head in
Wycombe Stakes (w.f.a., one.mile three fur.). Eandwick
Plate saw Lord .Cardigan receive 2st. llh> from Wakeful
yet in Melbourne Chip the champion mard was asked to
give him 3st. 6Jbs., and how it, was sized up by those who
came at the Derby winner (F.J.A.) that a horse of his
age was going to give such a proved top-notcher as Lord
Cardigan 161bs. surpasses all understanding. Might be
mentioned that Lord Cardigan is a grandson of his owner’s
old favorite, Black Swan.
, * * * *
F.R.C. Derby winner, FLA., is a beauty,
and, for a pony standing not more than 14.2, a remark-
able performer. His win excels all previous end to end
Derby triumphs. For a light-fleshed nag his middle
section is exceptionally well rounded, and thighs and
gaskins are urtique. F.J.A., who belongs to Rupert
Clarke, owns a splendid record ; Gwyn Nursery, Moonee
Valley Stakes,-St. Albans Handicap (4fur., 8.9), V.A.T.O.
Juvenile Stakes (sfur., 8.7), Oakleigh Purse (ojfur., 8.11)
A.J.O. Easter Stakes (7fur., 8.7)), South Australian
Derby, Toorak Handicap (7.13), and V.R.C. Derby. By
Wallace from La Tosca, by Robinson Crusoe, from
Nightmare, by Panic, F.J.A. is full brother to crack
2-year-old Class, and was bred by the late W. R. Wilson,
and sold one of the cheapest yearlings of his time. Time
(2.365) for the race equals record put up by Abundance
last year. Three hqlf-mile sections were covered in 53J,
51-$, and 514, and final 6fur. in lmin. 18$sec. —all inferior
to Lord Cardigan’s figures for same distances same day.
*** ' *
F.J.A.’s Derby was, without a doubt, the most
brilliantly won race in the history of V.R.C. blue-ribbon
contests. Newhaven-has been talked of as a phenomenal
Derby victor, because the red chestnut colt ran right
away, all the way, from seven commoners in moderately
fast time. F.J.A. made the running from end to end,
afid the race was 3see. faster than Newhaven's. Abun-
dance is the only previous winner to equal F.J.A.’s time
(2.365.), but whereas the big horse had the advantage of a
pacemaker in Great Scot, and could only catch him at the
distance post and win by-a length,'the little son of
Wallace was never approached bv any of his pursuers
save Sweet NeJI. Having shaken'off the filly, he came
clean away again and won by 2£ lengths. A great per-
formance. On returning to the “birdcage” k’-J-A.
nibbled the grass unconcernedly and looked fit to run
another Derby, whilst Sweet Nell, the runner-up. was
“all out.”
* * * •
Of course, the win-was a disappointment to the
crowd that backed Emir or Sweet Nell, yet the boo-hoos
of the punters and their roars of “ flub him out 1 ” (mean-
ing Rupert Clarke) only went to show their disregard of
all sporting considerations beyond the fact that they
had dropped- their coin. "It was the colt’s third
outing this season. He won the S.A. Derby; then
he took the Toorak Handicap at Caulfield, thereby earn-
ing a 71b. penalty for the Cup; and now he had put
up a record classic performance. “All lovers of a
good IjorSe,” to use a cant newspaper phrase, should
have cheered this remarkable pony. in hooting
him the hooters proved themselves to be frauds as
regards their professed fondness for sport. It’s a
successful bet that they really love, without regard
for consistency, or inconsistency, in the “form”
of the nag that has landed their wagers for them. Had
F.J.A. been palpably pulled to admit of Rupert’s popular
candidate winning the mob would have winked its other
eye and cheered lustily, but because the two game bits of
horseflesh represented the one owner on their respective
merits, and the result didn’t suit the public pocket, there
was a doleful reiteration of “ Rub him out ” from Hill
and Flat. As for Emir’s bearing on the case that is a
different story. Emir, owned by Rupert Clarke’s brother,
ought to have been the boss three-year-old of Scobie’s
Stable, judging from the suggestions of excellence (as a
galloping machine) that the big brown deceiver has shown
in public and private. He can out-sprint the lot of them
at times, and he is built to stay—but he doesn’t stay. On
paper Emir had done quite enough work to make him fit
to win the Derby, but he hadn’t grown very muscular on
it. He is said to have grown vicious, like Carnage of
yore, and if rumor, tells the truth, Emir is no longer
worth talking about as a great horse, though the stable’s
faith in-the animal may continue to find cash expression
after the outside world has sized him up as a bad job.
The Derby race didn’t appear to distress Emir. He ran
as though he had a constitutional aversion to hard work,
and it would be interesting to know exactly what his
trainer had to say for and against the colt’s chance what
time Ikey Mo was pegging him back in the market. The
bookmakers apparently were betting on information re-
ceived.
/ * * * *
Also, one would like to know whether Lewis—
the “ first jockey ”of the stable—was given the option to
ride F.J.A. in preference to either Sweet Nell or Emir.
The whole question as to there being an excuse, or no
excuse, for the crowd’s demonstration against the Derby
winner, seejns to turn upon a paragraph published in
Melbourne Age on the morning of the. race. The Age
was officially advised that Lewis, “as leading jockey for
Scobie’s stable, has not yet made up his mind as to
whether he will ride Emir or Sweet Nell.” But it was
already decided that “F.J.A. would be ridden bv
Richardson, who won the Caulfield Cup on Sweet
Nfll.” Now, if Richardson is No. 2 jockey for
Soobie’s stable, how came it that he was appointed
to ride what from the wording of the Age para-
graph, was fairly assumed to be the stable’s No. 3
candidate? People in the “birdcage” who saw the
three Ballarat candidates saddled up and sent off to the
fray will also ask why it was that F.J.A., the outsider of
the trio, had the distinction of being waited upon to the last
by trainer and owner, who walked beside him, breathing
final instruqtions to Richardson after Sweet Nell had left
the birdcage unattended. The thousands of angry de-
monstrators on Hill and Flat hadn’t seen this mark of
stable distinction paid to F.J.A., or they might have boo-
hooed the louder when the numbers went up. Even as
it was, they raised the biggest noise of disapproval ever
heard at Flemington.
* * * *
Rupert Clarke cut a pitiable figure. He was
quite unnerved. Jockey Richardson also failed to rise to
the uproar of the occasion, for when the newspaper inter-
viewers came along to the dressing-room he laughingly
blurted out an unnecessary excuse for his victory: “ I
just rode the race as: I was told to ride. I was told to
make it a nice pace. No one else had the least interest in
it, only me, and I won the race to suit myself." Practi-
cally he was saying : “ Don’t blame, anybody else. Blame
me.” But what had boy Richardson to be blamed for ?
And why did Rupert terremble and turn pale, like a poli-
tician on the hustings getting a first experience of his
constituency’s rotten eggs ?
* * * »
Wakeful put up a record in winning Melbourne
Stakes for the third year in succession. Lord Cardigan
and Rhapsody carried a stone more than their Cup
weights, while Wakeful humped 161 b. less. The S’Aus-
tralian candidate, Sojourner, staggered his followers by
finishing at the tail end of the field, behind St. Ambrose,
the English-bred horse having dropped back after making
one little rush which brought him into brief notice.
* * * *
An enormous Derby crowd came to rejoice and
remained to grieve at Flemington last Saturday. It was
a disastrous occasion for punters in general, and Vic-
torians in particular. An odds-on favorite got beaten for
the Maiden Plate ; the two rival idols from Scobie’s stable
succumbed to the 6 to 1 Bee Bee in the Maribyrnong, and
a 15 to 1 chance took the Hotham Handicap from the
others that carried good money. Then came the Derby
howl of anguish. Coburg Stakes went to the despised
Graft, and another outsider won the Encourage Stakes.
Wakeful, at 2 to 1 on, was the only victorious favorite of
the day, but none of the small punters dreamed of going
for a “ recovery ”on the mare. About 80 per cent, of the
crowd backed a loser every time, and went home in a
moody, broody state of mind. Even Ikey Mo was thought-
ful. A few more such days might mean a mortal sickness
for the public goose that lays the golden eggs, and Ike
doesn’t want the goose to be a dead bird.
* * * »
A Melbourne stockbroker tells a lovely tale of
a misunderstanding between himself and the boy who
rode a particular gee-gee for the first time. He (the
owner) had every reason to believe in the jockey’s
honesty, but in this instance he felt morally certain that
the house was stopped at a critical point of the race. So
he decided to inquire carefully into the matter, leading
off with a simple question. “ What do you think of the
colt?” he asked. The unsatisfactory rider grinned
meaningly and replied “ It’s a good ’un : I could’ve won
easy." “ Then why the devil didn’t you ? ” thundered the
owner. The amazed mannikin cleared his character by
tersely explaining the situation. “ When I was going to
the post, you told me you didn’t mean to back the ’orse
but would,,just let him run for the stake. Well, I thort
that was yfer perlite way of telling me to pull ’is blanky
’ead off.” ®

2sec. is the speediest recorded, bar Duke of Grafton’s
I.l} last year. She was bred by her owner at Motto
Farm, near Newcastle (N.S.W.), and is by Haut Brion
(imp.) from that beauty Ohand Bee Bee (imp.), by Royal
Hampton out of Miss Baba, by Springfield from Gunga
Joe, by Orlando. Bee Bee is half-sister to Chantress,
winner of last Newmarket Handicap and Futurity Stakes!
Stake secured is among biggest of the meeting. Bee Bee is
the 15th filly to score in the same event, and latest was the
14th occasion of a N.S.W. rep. succeeding. Also interest-
ing that only when the event was first run (32 years ago)
did a smaller field than last Saturday’s line up.
Ballarat sport, Mr. Wm. Bailey, owner of hot
favorite Hauturier—who went so badly wrong in a gallop
on Sunday as to necessitate immediate scratching—and

gathered more than a fair share of V.R.O. Derbies. Cocos
and Hautvillers were his, and he had an interest in each
of two other winners—Suwarrow and Camoola.

Remarkable how the halt and the lame of the
Cup nags of Saturday and Sunday and Monday morning
came into the betting Monday night. Wakeful was
given out a very doubtful starter, and yet a heap of coin
went on her subsequently, and she ran second. Flag-
ship, alleged to be more fit for hospital than racing,
carried heavy investments—lumps of which came after
his “ break-down," ditto Ossian, Sojourner, and The
Idler. Marvellous what a great restorative lengthened
odds prove for the stiff 'uns of the turf.
* * * •
“M.” writes: Re par. by “E. and 0.E.”
(8., 8/10/’O3) on W.A. racing. “E. and 0.E.” evidently
knows very little of racing in this State. Does he know
of any other place in Australia where an owner can start
a horse in a £lOOO Cup for a tenner ? If he only took
the trouble to look up the prize-money given away during
the past five years on the goldfields, he would discover
that the owners are being well catered for. As to the
leading clubs trying to make their courses compare with
the best in other States, Kalgoorlie and Boulder have
succeeded in doing so. A large amount of money has
been spent on each course, and they are used by the
people as parks. There is no other State so free from
welchers. There has only been one case at the Boulder,
one at Ooolgardie, and two at Kalgoorlie since racing
started on the fields. There has not been one case of
welching on metropolitan courses during the past
five years. Bookmakers are not allowed to bet on
any course in W.A. unless they are licensed by
the W.A. Turf Club. The majority of bookmakers
privileged to bet have shops of their own, and are mem-
bers of recognised betting clubs. Racing is not overdone
on the fields, and there is no more racing here until
March, with the exception of a charity meeting at the
Boulder, next month, when £250 will be given in stakes,
and the proceeds divided among local charities; £4OO
was realised at a similar meeting last year. There are no
proprietary clubs on the goldfields, and the management
of racing is in good hands. Nomination and acceptance
fees are the lowest in the Commonwealth, and book-
makers’ fees the highest. The writer knows several
bookmakers who have been paying as much as £5OO a
year for license fees. Dates for racing are arranged in
conference each year between the goldfields’ clubs and
the W.A. Turf Club. The proprietary clubs are not
recognised at the annual conference, and have to take a
back seat in the control of racing.

“J.A.C.”: A well-known Sydney publican
relates a remarkable racing dream. The night before a
big race he saw, in the visions of his head upon his bed,
a certain horse winning—horse, jockey, eolors were all
clear and past dispute. He told a friend, and two deeply
impressed men hurried out early to keep ahead of the
rush and get the odds from a shop Hebrew—a Hebrew
who seemed to have a strange vibration in the left eye.
Then they went to the course—and learned that their
horse had died that same morning. Hence the vibration
in the Hebrew’s eye.
* * * *
Telegraphed Monday that drawer (a Maclean
N.S.W., resident) of then first favorite (Abundance) in
Melbourne Cup sweep sold his chance for £5OO, which
was a most ill-advised proceeding, for Monday saw the
horse at fives, with sixes eagerly snapped up wherever
available. Drawer of Abundance could have laid off
£3OOO to £5OO and had the substantial balance coming to
himself if the horse won, but if he ran second or third
the £5OO would still be the drawer’s atop of place-money.
Sale was a handsomely profitable thing for the buyer,
who could straightway have laid-off to secure his £5OO
and then stood on velvet with the prospect of winning
£3OOO (half the first prize), or getting second or third
place-money (substantial sums in each instance), or, at
worst, pocketing Abundance's share as a starter. Almost
as good a spec, at the time was a Melbourne Mo’s
purchase of a Hauturier ticket for £7OO, but the scratch-
ing, on Monday, of the nag put a different complexion
on the transaction, though in the meantime the “ book”
had laid off so as to secure something in excess of his
investment.

“Marat”: What surprises one most in the
proceedings of the N.S.W. Select Committee on gambling
is the talkative egotistical manner of a few small fielders
hauled up to be questioned. One would expect bookies
to be a close-mouthed crowd who button up on every-
thing, but it seems otherwise. To judge by the evidence
of one small fielder, pony racing throughout the State is
under his sole control. Ikey is chock-full of vanity when
he gets a good opportunity, with a reporter intelligent
enough to translate his nasal gibberish into plain
Australianese—to understand, for instance, when he
mentions a “ horth ” that he means a bloomin’ moke.
* * * *
“E. and O. E.”: “L.W.” is quite right. For
“line” writing nowadays it is necessary to read the sport-
ing columns of the daily press. No racing reporter worthy
the name owns acquaintance with the weather ; for him
“ the meteorological conditions " are perfect—or other-
wise. The turf is “the green sward”; the first race
always “ starts the ball rolling ” ; betting in the ring
becomes a “ never-ending battle between the metallicians
and the punters ” ; the starters are “ competitors ” ; there
is a “ solemn hush ” as the horses line up to the barrier
and “ a stentorian cry of ‘They’re off!’ from thousands
of throats.” Of course as they “ flash past the post no
one but the judge can tell what has won,” and invariably
“ the gentleman in the box ” decides in favor of some-
thing or other. _ For years have I studied the racing
reports, and enjoyed them, but two unfamiliar gems
recently exalted me. “ The withdrawals are sufficiently
sparse to portend good fields ” strikes me as original.
And if “ the automatic dispenser of odds” has been used
before, I have not noticed it. The young man had
already said totalisator once in the sentence ; to repeat
the word would be a violation of all literary precedent.
** • *
“ C.P.” : The walking disease threatens to
undermine the health of the community. Already whole
business establishments are under its influence. It
usually starts this way. Smith, the accountant, appar-
ently in the best of health the night befcire, will put in an
appearance at the office one morning intoxicated with a
desire to walk to Brighton, or Botany, or Timbuctoo, or
anywhere. Not that he has any desire to go there, but
the disease is on'him, and he must walk. It is a mere
detail that Smith has probably not walked more than 400
yards a day in one stretch for 10 years or more. The
complaint is as contagious as measles in a public school,
and by lunch time everyone from the junior messenger to
the general manager is feeling his leg muscles and telling
how he used to be able to do his eight miles an hour
without turning a hair. Of course, they get up a match,
and the railway revenue suffers for a week while the
competitors get into form. It always rains on the day,
but that makes no difference. The consumptive ledger-
keeper and the accountant—who weighs 15st. 131 b.—start
off limit and go at a tremendous pace, the telegraph posts
slipping by at quite three miles an hour. As they near the
Tied House the pace is tremendous. A few minutes
later brewery shares go up with a jump, and there’s a
peculiar gurgling sound in the bar parlor. Then the
accountant starts back for his clothes. The invoice clerk
win? and gets so cocky afterwards that the member of
the Employers’ Federation, with a protuberance under
his vest, who runs the show from the office with the
glass door, decides that work is interfering with his sport,
so gives him his walking ticket. The consumptive book-
keeper doesn’t turn up for a month through an attack of
bronchitis or pneumonia, and the whole establishment
looks like a casualty ward. But it’s not in the office
only the epidemic rages. Postmen who travel something
short of 200 miles a day are just as susceptible, and after
delivering Saturday’s mail walk 12 miles with their
tongues out, and call it pleasure. The blacksmith comes
home after eight hours with the sledge-hammer, and
after tea starts out to get what he calls a little exercise so
as to be ready for Saturday. A man won’t argue a point
nowadays; he’ll walk you ten miles to decide whether
Protection or Freetrade is best, and the man who arrives
at the pub last has to change his views there and then.

Warner’s second ’Ome team opens its tour in
Adelaide, Saturday this week. Twelve, from whom 11
to meet visitors will be chosen on Friday, includes
Evans, Rees and Hay, who were not known in inter-State
matches last season. First-named figured on S.A. side
several times, but last year wasn’t included in 11 that
played Victoria or N.S.W., though he shaped against the
Englishmen. Rees is a colt who has done well all round
in club cricket. Hay did big things when opposed to
Warner’s first team, bagging 9 wickets for 8 runs each.
Expected that Hay will be troublesome and help make
up, to some extent, for retirement from big cricket of
Trumble.
• • • »
Marsh, the aborigine, threatens to again be a
thorn in the side of N.S.W. Cricket Association. He
simply routed the Leichhardt-Balmain team in the first
match he played this season. Further, no new blood
worth mentioning has appeared at the bowling crease,
and last year’s lot, which was a poor lot then, seems
poorer this year. The job of finding an excuse for not
playing Marsh is going to be a large contract.
* * * *
“ S.T.S.” • Re Colonial Secretary Lyttelton and
his wicketkeeping. The match, in 1884 (B. 22/10/’O3),
when he had to bowl, and did bowl surprisingly well,
was notable for two more points. Not only did the
whole English side take a turn with the ball while
Murdoch and co. had them collared, but W. G Grace
fielded in every position in the field. He bad done every-
thing except keep wicket, and he took the gloves when
Lyttelton relinquished them. Meanwhile some unknown
clever person was qualifying for the post of champion
Mean Man. He drove up in a cab to Lyttelton’s house
while the latter was saving his country (the match was
drawn), pitched a tale about A.L. being detained at the
Oval, and obtained his overcoat and dressing-bag—which
were never heardof again.

The 14 English cricketers now in Australia
played among them last English season 486 completed
innings for 15,728 runs with an average of 32 36. Cen-
turies to their credit total 183,' thus : Havward 41
Tyldesley 31, the skipper and Hirst 21 each, Foster 16,
Knight 15, Braurid 15, Lilley 10, Bosanquet 7, Arnold 6,
Keif 2, Rhodes 1. First-class cricket saw the side’s 9
bowlers get 862 wickets for 15,155 runs, averaging 17'58,
and all excepting one (Hayward) did better last than any
preceding season.

The withdrawal of Trumble and Darling, which
seems pretty definite, leaves open the billet of captainim-
the locals in the coming series of test matches. An added
lustre to the position is that if the season’s captain proves
a success he will be cert: i.i to have the refusal of leadin"-
the twelfth Australian Eleven to England. The choice
lies between M. A. Noble and Clem Hill, who each
captains his State team, as Laver, the Victorian skipper
is not even sure of a place in the team for the tests’
The idea of a match between the visiting English
Eleven and a team of N.S.W. “colts” is an admirable
one—always provided that players who have taken part
in representative and inter-State cricket are left out.
Similar matches have taken place between “colts” and
visiting English teams, but in many cases the “colts”
have included has-beens of bygone days.

Young South Sea Island native, “ Alicb ’
Wickham, who showed wonderful sprint swimming:
powers last season by covering 50yds in world’s record
time (25 3-5sec.), has already this season evidenced
marked advancement oh that form. Few weeks back saw
him shoot through the water at a surprising rate, but last
Saturday, at Rushcutter’s Bay (Sydney) baths, he knocked
3-5sec. off his own world’s best figures and threw the
hundred behind 3-5sec. better than Fred. Lane’s 60l-5sec.
feat. Swimming people generallv are wondering what
Wickham is going to do next, and foretell a warm time
for top-noteher Dick Cavill up to lOOvds.
* * » ‘ *
Championship catch-as-catch-can ’rastle, ’tween
Hindus Buttan Singh and Gunga Brahm at Sydney
National S.O., was the most exciting and interesting seen
for years in Sydney. Mostly, Gunga was on the defen-
sive, but not to nearly the same extent as at Wirth’s
Circus recently. His condition was better, and he shaped
more Confidently. Close calls were plentiful, principally
against Gunga, though he now and again played the part
of top dog. Half-an-hour after “ go ” Buttan secured a
half-Nelson with the left and a leg hold with the right,
and steadily worked Gunga over on to both shoulders
(legs uppermost), amid intense excitement. Seven
minutes’ spell (they wouldn’t have any more, though
conditions provided for 16) and Gunga rushed his man.
For 6min. they forced and bumped with great fury, when
Gunga, immediately after escaping a full-Nelson, got
t’other’s right arm between his elbow and ribs, and,
securing a powerful leg hold, forced Buttan to one
shoulder and, pressing all weight available, worked him
on to the other point. House cheered uproariously. Six
minutes’ spell, and both operated viciously. First Brahm
and then Singh rushed. The tussle bristled with
clever' moves, till Buttan brought the hammerlock to
bear, and, aided by a firm leg hold, had Gunga settled,
despite a desperate resistance, and, levering him over,
scored the winning fall and the right to continue cham-
pion. Singh offered to any man on the conti-
nent and throw him three times before being thrown
himself once, but there was no response.

A feature of the late series of Rifle matches
was the eclipse of the picked Kolapore shots who went
to Bisley last year. They were most of them engaged
but were put down by the stay-at-home pot-hunters.
Truly the “ King’s," the chief event, was won by Soden of
Queensland who was one of the team. But though he
went to Bisley he was not finally selected as one of the
team which managed to win the cup.

Wellington (M.L.) had its first, fight ’tween
professional pugs for years, when local ex-amateur Tim
Tracey recently faced Yankee gymnast, ball-puncher, &c.,
“ Kid ” Brown-Parker. (A pug' with a hyphenated name !
What next?) Tracey was best from first gong sound
•until fourth round saw B-P. spreadeagled and not a bit
anxious to resume. Tracey got the coin and heaps of
kudos. Enthusiastic friend’s are tipping him a “cert”
against'Australia’s best lightweights, but smashing.the
’Murkan, who had previously been well beaten in Sydney,
Adelaide, and elsewhere, isn’t much to go on, albeit
Tracey is in the lOst. class, while his first pro. victim is a
middleweight.
* * * *
Ebony bruiser Starlight, aged 50, and who has
retired “ for good ” thrice as often as Patti, shaped in
Melbourne, last week, through sheer necessity. He
bested local champion Jim Higgins from end to end of
six rounds, though the white started a 2 to 1 on favorite.
Higgins cried enough, alleging that his back was hurt.
Previously, when the same two clashed, Starlight
“chucked it” through a broken wrist. Australian
bruiserdomis all cripples, apparently; including Doherty,
whose hands went back on him in the Scanlan fight, and
champion M‘Ooll, who still nurses a lame shoulder,
sustained in his roughing tussle, three months ago, with
Biter Scanlan.

A Melbourne correspondent:—
The Dry Dog’s bark at Melbourne Town Hall on Friday
last was but a mild yap compared to former efforts. The
Man with the Monocle seemed dispirited after his cool
reception in the Northern Districts,-where, on an average,
the station-master and the local Jaypee were the only
people to “welcome" him. The “Freetrade” organisa-
tion, of which a person named Renwick is secretary, had
“distributed” tickets (nominally at 2s. and Is. apiece)
lavishly among such as were likely to cheer for the im-
porting “Cause,” and had whipped up every “Free-
trader ” in Victoria except the Abrahamses —whose
Sabbath was on. Hickford, a little clean-shaven
man, sat in the chair—a huge affair—and
reclined back in its cavernous depths, looking for all the
world like a sparrow in a kerosene tin. To the right of
George sat foolish old Simon Fraser, who said “Har!
liar ! ” every time Reid alluded to the price of wheat), and
“illimitable tracts of water,” or “the extremely in-
telligent Victorian workers”—just as if Fraser knew
anything about intelligence ! On George’s left sat Robert
Reid, of the painfully well-known importing firm
of Robert Reid and Co. The front of the “house” had
been most carefully packed, so that when the eye-glass
and shirt-front of George ascended the horizon at least a
regulation “ burst of applause ” might be provided. Even
if it hadn’t been, the fortunate presence of the Ap„gus
would have remedied the deficiency next day. What
George talked about doesn’t matter. His most notable
utterance was the half-veiled threat that if he “ didn’t
get a show ” (what a showman it is !) Deakin wouldn’t get
one in Sydney.

The Jenkins Iscariot Government has
closed up S.A. Parliament after a very short
session. (S.A. had a 20-days’ session in 1875, and
a two-days’ session in 1881, and a four-days’
session in 1887, but for some reason an ill-informed
Sydney daily paper described the one just eflded
as the shortest on record.) It was a disastrous
time. The Jenkins Government first proposed to
keep the finances square by renewing the extra
in the £ land-tax imposed last session, but the
Assembly threw out the proposal. _ Then it pro-
posed a re-assessment of land to see if it could get
some extra revenue that way, but the Council
threw that out and replaced, the land tax.
The Assembly threw that out again. Then,
having been licked three times, the Jenkins
Iscariot Ministry closed the session in disgust.

An episode at George Reid’s Melbourne
meeting (Sydney Telegraph’s report):—
It was a hypocritical pretence to seek to exclude
colored races by submitting their own countrymen to an
education test. This was an incident of the weakness of
the Government in giving way to the dictation of the
Labor party.
A Voice : Did you vote for it ?
Mr Reid: No.
The Voice : You did.
Mr. Reid : If I did I was wicked. (Laughter.)
It takes a tolerably brassy front to laugh when
bowled out so badly as that.
The fact that the See Government has
been licked at Glen Innes (N.S.W.) bye-election
doesn’t amount to much in the way of a surprise ;
the See Government is licked everywhere these
days. The most interesting feature of the case was
that at Glen Innes both parties were against
reduction of members. Yet both See and Car-
ruthers have declared in favor of it in the
Assembly. And neither wants it, and each
accuses the other of not wanting it, and each
repudiates the insinuation with anger, and neither
does anything.
Marriage and Health.
Married men live longer than single men. So,
at least, a study of the United States census
returns would seem to indicate. But though
marriage may be conducive to longevity, it
certainly does not secure immunity from indiges-
tion, biliousness, and the host of weakening and
distressing ailments which have their origin in a
disordered stomach and a torpid liver. Yet even
that may be secured by the timely and regular
use of Mother Seigel’s Syrup, a natural and
digestant made of fruits, roots and herbs. _ 1 or
several years I was a martyr to indigestion and
flatulence,” says Mr. Blackie, of Post Office
Chambers, Auckland, N.Z. “My cure was
effected about four years ago by Mother Seigel s
Syrup. I experienced relief before the first bottle
was finished. Eight bottles completely restored
me to health.”*
Rumored that Alexander Hay, defeated at
Glen Innes, is not to be dropped unhonpred into
the ranks of the defeated. “ He will in every
respect ornament the N.S. W. Legislative Coun-
cil,” is the expression going the rounds. See’s
first appointment to the Legislative Council was
Solicitor Fitzgerald, when that monument of
eloquent silence was similarly refused a seat., The
Council is a place where the man who can’t be
trusted to help at running the country’s business
for three years is sent so that he may help to run
the country’s business for life.
Preacher “ Elijah ” Dowie, of the wings,
has created a sensation,*according to cable report,
by claiming to be the illegitimate son of' a duke,
which seems rather a rough thing to say about his
mother. What with his cold reception in New
York, where there were no converts, and the
takings didn’t pay for gas, and 3000 of his
followers sneezing simultaneously, and the
accusation that he was about to leave with the
cash, or that his wife had already left with the
cash, and his own influenza, Dovyie’s stock seems
rather low just now.

The other day a constable arrested in a
Eaddock, near Ipswich (Q.)> a poor woman and
er three children. They were living in a camp
measuring 6ft. by 4ft., and constructed of old bags
and kerosene tins. There was no bedding save
dried grass. They had all tramped there from
Brisbane, and were living principally on meat
begged from a slaughter yard. The names of the
were Ida Sophia, Lilia Evelyn, and
Maud Ellen!

“ Melbourne’’ : Was waiting for a suburban
train, the other night, when about half of it came
up to the platform. The guard had to climb out
and walk along a drain some 50 yards before he
could climb on to the platform. He was pretty
wild then. Signalled the driver to draw lip level
with the platform. Driver evidently took it for the
starting sign, proceeded to get full steam on, and
was only recalled by vigorous whistling. When
he backed in again the guard asked him, in quite a
savage voice, what kind of pea-nut barrow he used
to drive before he blacklegged. The driver care-
fully recorded the remark, and that guard will
probably be hauled up to explain.

When Town Clerk Clayton, of Melbourne,
returned from his wanderings round the globe the
principal thing he brought back with him was an
idea, picked up in some cold climate, how to
prevent the dust nuisance. The method consisted
in applying a thin coating of tar to the roadways.
So long as the weather was cool the system
worked splendidly, but the first really hot day the
tar melted, and every citizen who crossed the road
gathered on his boots a sticky mass about the size
of a hen, and when he tried to walk on the pave-
nient.bShad to tear each foot loose as if he was
struggling to drag payment of a just debt out of a
Kyabram farmer. Then the public spoke dis-
paragingly about Clayton, just as if he was one of
Bent’s engines. So, to dry up the tar, a thick
layer of sand was laid down, which immediately
proceeded to fly about and deposit itself in the
lunch. Not that a man objects to sand for lunch,
but he prefers to have it on a separate plate.
Consequently Melbourne was worse than before
Clayton had his great idea. Finally the council
fell back the good old system of watering, and
the ladies held up their skirts as usual.

A peculiar argument in defence in a
Sydney court, where an accused was charged with
not accounting for all the sums of money entered
on his charity list:—
In some cases where people subscribed 2s. 6d. it might
be entered as 7s. 6d. or 10s., as an encouragement to
others to contribute liberally. In the collecting lists of
various churches it was a recognised thing that many
persons were put down for £5, £10, or £20, who never
paid up a single shilling.
In Christchurch (M.L.) the town refuse,
including the remains of the stray dog, is burned
in a destructor, and the heat from the destructor
generates electricity for the supply of the town.
This keeps Christchurch very clean and quiet, for
if the proper officials don’t bring in enough refuse
and stray dog to keep the machinery going there
is trouble. Their anxiety to clean up amounts
almost to a disease.

A queer-grained Maorilander recently
p -titioned for restitution of conjugal rights, and
i.reatlied a pathetic story into the ear of the
bench. He stated that his vvife was a bit unruly
sometimes, but he didn’t mind ; she had gone to
town to renew the lease of the farm, had renewed
the lease' in her own name, kicked him out,
refused to hand over his clothes, threatened to
1 rain him with an axe, and done other sundries.
So he had to take another farm on his own
account. He incidentally mentioned that the
lady was a great religious enthusiast, and had
sampled various brands, including Anglican,
Presbyterian and that of the Dippers. At present
she was a red-hot Dipper. The court couldn’t con-
ceive of a man in his position looking for further
trouble, but granted his petition on general
principles.

HAVE YOU NOTICED IT?
That a crooked arm or leg on a child at
once excites your pity; that it excites all" the
more pity if you reflect that there is no neces-
sity for thi3 deformity ? It proceeds in most
cases from Rickets. Rickets is a disease in
which the bones are deficient in their earthy
constituents, and consequently have lost their
natural hardness; they beoome soft, like
gristle, and somewhat brittle, so that they
are not only easily bent, but easily broken.
Rickets is generally associated with a ten-
dency to scrofula —either hereditary or en-
gendered by poor living and unhealthy in-
fluences, such as deficient ventilation and
light, damp, impure air, and bad food.

under your eyes where Scott’s Emulsion was
given to the child, how quickly Rickets is
relieved, and then cured, by a course of
Scott’s Emulsion ! Scott’s Emulsion checks,
relieves, and finally removes Rickets, sup-

stituents, thus restoring their natural hard-

taint.

disease. If, therefore, you notice any weakness

DEATH!
The'cold clasp of Death will inevitably enfold each and all of us at last, but Science has given many
of us the means of postponing: the dread embrace until our tale of years is spent, and it would be the
height of folly to refrain from availing ourselves of the aid winch knowledge affords. Death is
hastened by ignorance. Nature has endowed us with the sense of pain, solely in order that, by seek-
ing relief, we may avoid playing into the hands of death. When we feel pain we are out of health.
Pain long endured is a strain upon the system which nature cannot withstand. Many of us, however,
go on suffering certain kinds of pain, just as if Science could not come to the help of her sister Nature.
There is, for instance, no necessity for anyone to suffer from
Rheumatism
Gout
Neuralgia
Backache
Sciatica
Indigestion
Anaemia
Blood Disorders
Biliousness
Jaundice
Gravel
Stone
Bladder Troubles
General Debility
Sick Headache
The pains caused by this long list of disorders are but Nature’s means of letting us know that the
kidneys or liver are for some reason unable to perform their work efficiently, for all of these dis-
orders are produced by the retention in the system of urinary and biliary poisons, which would be
thrown off naturally if the kidneys and liver were doing their duty. Few people realise how important
in the scheme of life is the healthy action of the kidneys and the liver, therefore the following
description of the functions of those vital organs will be of the deepest interest to many.
The Kidneys filter and extract from the blood about three pints of urine every day. In this
quantity of urine are dissolved about an ounce of urea, ten to twelve grains in weight, of uric acid,
together with other animal and mineral matter varying from a third of an ounce to nearly an ounce.
When the kidneys are in health, all this solid matter is in solution and is invisible. Directly the
kidneys, through either weakness or disease, become unfit to do their duty properly, a proportion of
the solid matter remains in the blood, becomes actively poisonous, and causes us to suffer from uric
disorders such as Rheumatism, Gout, Neuralgia, Lumbago, Backache, Sciatica, Gravel,
Stone, Bladder Troubles and Bright’s Disease. A simple test to make as to the condition of
the kidneys is to place some urine, passed the first thing in the morning, in a covered glass,_ and let it
stand until next morning. If it is then cloudy, or there is a brick-dust like sediment, or if particles
float about in it, or it is of an unnatural color, the kidneys are not healthy, and no time must be lost in
adopting remedial measures, or Bright’s Disease, Diabetes, or some less serious but more painful,
illness will result.
The Liver. —In the liver various substances are actually made from the blood. Two or three-
pounds of bile are thus made from the blood every day. The liver takes sugar from the blood,
conyerts it into another form, and stores it up so as to be able to again supply it to the blood!
gradually, as the latter requires enrichment. The liver changes uric acid, which is insoluble, into-
urea, which is completely soluble, and the liver also deals with blood corpuscles which have lived their
life and are useful no longer. When the liver is inactive or diseased we suffer from Indigestion,.
Biliousness, Ansemia, Sick Headache and Blood Disorders.
The health of the liver and of the kidneys is so closely connected that it is almost impossible for
the kidneys to be affected and the liver to remain healthy, or vice versa.
It is nearly thirty years since scientific research, directed specially to diseases of the Kidneys and
Liver, was rewarded by the discovery of the medicine now known throughout the world as
Warner’s Safe Cure.
It was realised, at the outset of the investigation, that it was necessary to find a curative agent which
would act equally upon the kidneys and upon the liver, these organs being so immediately associated
in the work of dealing with the body’s waste material, and after many disappointments, the medicine
which possessed the required action in the fullest degree was at length discovered. Warner’s Safe
Cure cures all diseases of the kidneys and liver, and, by restoring their activity, these vital
organs are enabled to rid the body, through the natural channels, of the urinary and biliary poisons,
the presence of which, in the system, are the cause of Rheumatism, Gout, Neuralgia, Lumbago, Back-
ache, Sciatica, Blood Disorders, Anaemia, Indigestion, Biliousness, Jaundice, Sick Headache, Gravel,
Stone, Bladder Troubles, and General Debility. Warner’s Safe Cure cures all these disorders simply
by removing the cause of the disorder. This is the reason why cures effected by Warner’s Safe Cure
are permanent cures. [1&
The Representative.

Who’s only half-a-man;

And fawn on every foe.
They do not care for prin-ci-ples —
They do not soar so high ;
Their man must raid the Treasuree,
Or make a desperate ti*y ;
They’ll sing his praises far and wide,
In cottage and in dome,
If only safe he’ll bring them back
The public plunder home.
They keep his pocket well in view,
Their fingers in his purse ;
And he must smile and smile and smile,
And never dare to curse.
To Sect and Club and Hospital,
To College and to School,
They make him his donation send —
The Legislative Fool!
They keep his brain upon the strain,
His nose upon the stone,
The gorgeous title —M.L. A.—
Is all he calls his own ;
They suck him like an orange dry,
Then throw him lightly down,
And choose upon next polling day
His rival —Draper Brown.
He starts, perhaps, with open brow,
With bosom kind and warm,
Perhaps because he’s somewhere heard
The country craves “ Reform ! ”
He finds the country only cares
For him whose cunning toil
Can carve for those who sent him in
The lion’s share of spoil.
And when he’s done with “ Public Life ”
And laid his honors down,
lie gaily cracks the village stones
To gain an honest crown ;
He cracks the stones and cracks a joke
And drains a jolly can,
For now he’s put the Statesman off
He dare put on the Man !

PHILANDER FLAM.
The Development of the Woman Voter.
The M.L. National Council of Women has cut
itself clear of the influence of the narrow-minded,
bloodless Gimlet Brigade, and lately adopted this
broadly-liberal and humane set of resolutions :
(1) That the time has come to remove all legal disquali-
fications from illegitimate children. (2) That the State
should make generous provision for the maintenance,
supervision and education of such children, the parents
being compelled to contribute according to their means.
(3) That, when paternity is proved, such children should
possess a legal status equal to other children, being regis-
tered in the father’s name. (4) That a female officer be
appointed to discover parentage before birth. (5) That
Government be urged to establish homes for such children
and for destitutes.
With a few conspicuous exceptions, the women
who first take up politics are hard, stern female
frequenters of prayer-meetings, who haven’t any-
thing to offer a “ fallen ” sister but a tract or a
turn at the washtub at greatly reduced wages.
M.L. and S. Australian experience proves, though,
that this sort doesn’t have the field to herself
for long. The whole crowd of womankind
soon Btarts in; and not having much per-
sonal knowledge of politics, the crowd votes for
one of two kinds of men—the particularly
well-known man, even if he doesn’t pose as a
saint (Seddon, for instance, in M.L., or Kingston
in S.A.), or for a “good” man—a man of whom
the Rev. Obadiah Jones has spoken kindly. In
S. Australia, where Obadiah lives in great
numbers, the women have reached this stage.
Moreover, there, at Obadiah’s instigation, they
mostly vote for the “good” class of politician;
and the present awful collection of baby-kissing
mediocrities is the result. Of the four S. A. Minis-
ters Jenkins is patronised by the Methodies, and
attends their tea-meetings; Foster is an ex-
Methody parson; Gordon is a Presbyterian
parson's son; Butler lives on the favor of the
Anglican section. That represents the second
stage in the political apprentice-work of women.
And it is pleasant to think, anyhow, that the
M.L. resolutions, quoted above, are characteristic
of the third stage of development, when women will
collectively exercise in the community much the
same influence that woman individually exercises
in the household.

“Chasselas”: Other States are not slow in
offering inducements to Victoria’s manhood,
which the calm, masterly policy of the Irvine-
Shiels combine is driving out of the country.
Westralian Government is making extraordinary
offers to farmers per medium of illustrations and
advertisements in Victorian weekly papers.
Amongst inducements are 160 acres of free land,
free survey, free railway trips whilst inspecting
land, and free vehicles and guides at nearest rail-
way stations. The ’Groper Government also
advertises that it has recently bought and cut up
several large estates, and will have them available
for closer settlement in suitable blocks shortly.
Already some of the Mallee farmers cultivating
land on the tenant system in S.W. Victorian
plains have taken up land in Westralia,
and are moving out, while more will follow
every month. They have no faith in Irvine’s
Compulsory Resumption of Land, and, even
if they had, recent mouthings of the Upper House
convince them that the Bill will be thrown out in
much the same fashion as was the Woman’s
Franchise Bill. Unless the Australian Govern-
ment will take a hand and impose a very heavy
graduated land-tax and absentee-tax, to break up
the big estates, there is nothing to hope for. Has
Premier Deakin any views on the subject ?

A fine old Sydney Tory, recently orating before
the Institute of Bankers, got so far on his wild
career of misrepresentation as to allege that the
socialistic Government of N. S.W. prohibits irriga-
tion, that work being reserved for the State.
There seems to be very little indeed in the way of
stupendous lying that a Tory won’t say when
once lie gets started. The N.S.W. Government
certainly looks rather coldly on persons who ask
to be allowed to seize upon a river, annex the
water, sell it at a high price to settlers, and pro-
vent anyone who doesn t pay their price using the
water. That isn’t irrigation so much as specula-
tion. But for everyone who wants to irrigate his
own land the Water Rights Act, passed by the
socialistic Parliament of N.S.W. in 1896, gives
the settler liberal privileges in the way of
damming rivers, creeks, &c., provided he
doesn’t want to take all the available water
and leave none for other settlers below
him. And it wasn’t till N.S.W. had a
socialistic Legislature that private irrigation was
rendered lawful in that State. In the old days,
when there was no Labor party, and the party
that the Institute of Bankers admires ran the
that tne institute or Hankers admires ran cue
show, _a man might dam a creek to provide him-
self with water for irrigation, but anybody had a
right to destroy his dam and ruin his irrigation
scheiiie. Ho could get absolutely no rights at all.
The insane policy of the Torv party made irriga-
tion nranl icallv imnnssible in NS W unless a
tion practically impossible in in.O.w., uni s
man defended his irrigation works with a gun.
But so well have things progressed under the new
law that from one Land District alone over 200
applications have been received for the right to
dam creeks and rivers for irrigation purposes, and
~n,. ” r 1 r
they were all granted, except where a man pro-
posed to annex practically the whole water sup-
ply. It was under the Institute of Bankers
friends that irrigation was illegal, and if it isn’t
ashamed of itself it ought to be.

Patriots, through curse of circumstances, and poli-
ticians, by compulsion of the grocer, may stand idly by
and see Australia set aside as a card of no account in the
cut-throat game now being played in the Balkans. The
Czar of Russia, and the Kaisers of Austria and Germany,
and Edward the Seventh of England, and Albert of
Monaco, and Henry the Forty-ninth of Reuss, and the
Presidents of the Republics of Andorra and San Marino,
and other high European potentates may sit upon their
thrones, satisfied with saying “ Shoo i” and “ Shame ! ”
while Pavlofpaziz is hacking the head off Bismillahvitch,
and Hassan Karaosmanoff is shedding the blood of
StefanopolousSulif-manowsky. Probably these potentates
know something about the character of the persons named.
But Joe Cook, of the Federal House of Representatives, is
not that sort of heartless hairpin. He rose in his place, some
little time ago, and asked, in a voice husky with emotion,
“whether the Prime Minister would consider the advisa-
bility of cabling to the Imperial authorities the protest
of Australia against the continuance of atrocities in the
Balkans, and also of urging joint action with other
European Powers towards bringing the matter to a speedy
end.” The Prime Minister replied vaguely to the effect
that there was probably a good deal to be said on both
sides of the question, and that he felt some hesitation
about instructing the Imperial Government in the manner
suggested. Mr. Deakin has evidently lost a good deal of
the bright enthusiasm of his younger and less responsible
days, and after that vague answer he let things slides
They are still sliding, and our chance of smiting Abdul
the Damned in Stamboul, and throwing the Divan with a
great splash into the Sublime Porte, is being lost. I think
we should send a contingent to Macedonia.

Etheridge (N.Q.) is largely a field of blasted
hopes Its mines are little use to it without a railway,
and it can't get one. A Croydon to Georgetown railway
proposal has been twice before Parliament; both times it
looked like getting through, but finally was lost by a
small majority—once by only one vote. A while back,
when Chillagoe looked brighter than it does now, the
Lindsay Express Co. came on the scene ; it got a finger in
about two dozen of the most promising Etheridge shows,
and also took up a strip of country from Georgetown to
Chillagoe for the purpose, so it said, of laying a line to
Almaden. Nothing, however, has been done towards
manning the mines or building the line, or getting
authority to do it. Things were going to hum within six
months ; but.after waiting for about two years Etheridge
had added this to the long list of the good things that
haven’t come off.

“Nulla”: Re the discovery of gold slugs at the
new Dee (Rockhamjston, Q.) ru-h, there is the usual
local controversy as to the weight of the largest nuggets
found in the district. Back in J uly, 1869, a record was
established by young Cadden picking up a nugget at
Mount Wheeler weighing 2680 z. lldwt., Next day, and
only a few inches away, a lad named Luckman found
another scaling 1790 z, 14dwt. In September, 1870,
yss&nnon and Burke found a nugget of pure gold at Rose-
wHta'eighmg 970 z. 12dwt.
the most inland town of N.S.W.,
is quite a after a shower of rain. The surface
country is auriferous, and the washing away of the loose
earth leaves the gold exposed. All hands and the cook
turn out, looking for “colors” and nuggets. Day’s
pickings vary according to luck, from nothing to upwards
of 15s. The great drawback to earning a quiet living in
this easy-going fashion, is the infrequency of showers.
* * * *
The Westralian Government, some months
ago, decided, on the advice of metallurgist Klug, not to
build furnaces at the Phillips River copper-field, as the
local miners asked, but to set up a sampling depfit, and
make advances against ore. Provis, late bf S. Australia,
has been in charge of the new idea since it was set going,
and about 1600 tons of ore have already been stacked.
The idea was to make liberal advances, so that the miners
could go on developing their shows; then, if develop-
ments were satisfactory, furnaces could be built, while,
if they were not satisfactory, a vessel could be chartered
to carry off the lot to eastern smelters. So far, no recom-
mendation has been made to the department one way or
the other.

Talk about the ups-and-downs of mining!
Collmann and Tacchi, once among the most brilliant of
Bendigo’s .producers, won last half-year, on its own
account, £4 worth of gold. But these Bendigo investors
do stick to their old shows. This mine has practically
returned nothing since 1899, yet all the time the share-
holders have kept briskly probing in all directions down
to nearly 2000 ft.
* * * »
The drought in the Eastern States put just over
a million and a quarter into S. Australia’s calico bag.
(S. Australia usually carries its money in a small calico
bag with a tape run round the top of it.) This result is
arrived at by comparing the price actually realised for
the exported farm produce and what would have been
realised if it had got the average of the prices which pre-
vailed between 1895 and 1902. For its hay crop alone it
received £1,165,814, whereas on the other basis it
would have had only £585,400 —a 100 per cent, rise and a
few strawis • over. But, all the same, when, in order to
keep the finances straight, Treasurer Butler proposed
that the land-tax should be retained as it was last year,
the cry of the “poor farmer” was raised all over the
House, a farthing was knocked off the tax, and the
Treasurer was deliberately left with 1 an estimated deficit
of about £25,000.

An unexpected curse at Private Enterprise
and approval of the Socialistic policy of State Interfer-
ence from S.M. Herai.d’s “On the Land” article
.(2/11/’Q3).:--“The only export trade in perishable pro-
ducts that has been satisfactorily developed by this State
is that of the despised Australian rabbit. This is the only
line which has been sent solely under State supervision,
and is also the sole instance in which quality and get-up
have improved all the time, and the lead established by
another State, in the same product, effectually wiped
out. However, our commercial people seem to have
decided that such successes as these are unwarranted
interferences with those engaged in the trade—who volun-
tarily submit their rabbits for inspection and treatment,
and swear by the State system—and it is therefore only
reasonable that producers should look to private enter-
prise to show its superiority in enlarging the export of
produce-business.” ‘

Letter received from S’Africa by a leading Ade-
laide business man :—“ I promised to let you know the
position in Transvaal. Well, things are very unsatis-
factory. The Government, which, of course, is Crown
colony", is more corrupt than ever was the fiber Govern-
ment. I won’t go so far as to say Milner takes bribes,
but it is certain that a big proportion of his underlings
do, and Milner is (though brilliant) a very weak-charac-
tered man. He is entirely under the influence of the
German Jews by whom he is surrounded. These Jews
seem to think that Britain fought this war entirely for and
on their account. They have and are engineering the
depression in Jo’burg. They want Chinese labor, not
because Kajjirs- cannot be got, but to oust the white
employees, They are afraid these latter will combine to
oppose reduction of wages. The name of Australian is
execrated by the ‘magnates,’ because Australians are
the leaders of the opposition to Chinese. The position
in July/, was this re Kaffirs; before the war 96,000:
Kaffirs were employed, 33 per cent, of these were perma-
nently unfit for work through drink; efficient labor =
64,000 Kaffirs. In July 76,000 Kaffirs were at work, and if
these were concentrated on producing mines the former
output of "485,0000 z. per month could be got. But this
does not suit these greecy Jews ; they want the ‘com-
pound system’ as practised in Kimberley, so as to
get all the profits at present enjoyed by the
merchants of the town, regardless of the fact that
everyone else in the country will be ruined. This labor
bogey is a myth. It is certain that another 30,000 to
40,000 Kaffirs can be got at once. The talk of wanting
360,000 more ‘boys’ is nonsense. This number will
not be required for 10 years. The Deep Mines will
not be payable. The grade of ore undoubtedly falls
at depth. Forty million tons crushed on Rand since
start to (late. Average profit 10s. 7d. per ton. If grade
of ore (and it does) falls off ldwt., or 4s. 2d., and costs at
these great depths rise ldwt., or 4s. 2d., the profit will
only be 2s. 3d. per ton. The outlay to equip one of these
Deep mines On a 200-stamp basis is £BOO,OOO to £1,000,000,
and time- 4* to \7 years. Two hundred stamps crush
360,000 tons per annum. What possible hope of paying
dividends is there on shares at several pounds (market
valuation) premium ?”

The olever Sydney youngster, Fanny Powers, who went
to England under-th'e protecting wing of Harry Rickards*
has made a successful first appearance in Londbn
Oxford Music flail. As the Oxford is one of the three or
four leading variety theatres of Fogtown, and as she
replaced one of the “stars of Vaudeville," Miss Vesta
Tilley, her parents and her teacher, Harry.Leston, have
reason to feel gratified.

new Municipal Theatre. The ventilation is bad, and the
pit has been designed in such a way that the pittite can
see hardly more than the lowest third of the stage, so
that when an actor stands up he looks like Charles I.
after his head was cut off. v j

“ Only Me ’’: The poem, “ Our Brother," by Het Thule
(B. 22/10/’O3), reminds me of the chorus to a song written
by George Ade, in his musical comedy, “ The Sultan of
Sulu "
We want to assimilate, if we can,
Our brother who is brown ;
We love our dusky fellow-man,
And hate to hunt him down.
So, when we perforate his frame,
We want him to be good ;
We shoot at him to make him tame,
If he but understood.
This seems to me to fit our case exactly.
“T.D.” : To anyone with a knowledge of the people
who appeared in the recent “ society vaudeville ’’ show
at Melbourne Princess’s the daily paper notices were ex-
cruciatingly funny. One ingenuous critic plastered
his half-column with references to “ Society's ornaments,"
“ ladies whose dancing has hitherto been confined to the
ball-room," and his own overwhelming surprise at the
hoof dexterity of young ladies of the “ vice-regal visiting
list.” Purves and Boffard were really about the only
people in the show who had even a nodding acquaint-
ance with the average V.R. aide-de-camp.
George Vining took the London Princess’s with a
capital of only £5O, and immediately struck oil with
Boucicault’s “ Streets of London.” A bigger success
followed, with “ Arrah-na-Pogue.” The splendid mechani-
cal business of the last scene was suggested by there
being under the stage an apparatus made at a cost of
£6OO for one of the previous management’s failures.
After “Arrah-na-Pogue” came another big hit with
“ Never Too Late to Mend." Vining made a fortune,
and lost it all in his next production, “Acis and
Galatea.” He died poor as a rat.
Granville Bantock, who came out to Australia as
orchestra conductor of “ The Gaiety Girl ’’ co., has lately
been trying his strength at serious work in London. He
produced “ an orchestral interlude from the unperformed
oratorio of ‘ Christus,’ ” but Bantock’s attempt to give a
musical representation of “ the region of desolation into
which Christ retired to prepare himself for the ministry
on earth ”is said to be unsatisfactory. He attempted too
much. A Bantockian tone picture of Jonah in the un-
furnished apartments of the Whale might have been
more successful.
State Labor leader M'Gowen has given his
idea about the Federal capital: It ought to have
an area of 1000 sq. miles, but the Federation
ought to buy, 900 sq. miles of it. He “ would
oppose in every possible way the proposal that
N.S.W. should give up such a large area of valu-
able territory as 1000 sq. miles whilst no contri-
bution was made by the other partners.” But the
other States did make a contribution —they under-
took not to make any claim on the Federal capital.
And even if this wasn’t contribution enough, it is
four years too late now to talk about it. N.S.W.
trusted George Reid to make a bargain for it; it
ratified that bargain; and there’s the end of it.
If it doesn’t think now that it was a good bargain,
all it can do is not to trust George Reid to make
any more. N.S. Wales can’t afford to be a fraud
because its ambassador was a fool.
“Scotty the Wrinkler” (Phil Moubray)
died in Narandera Hospital, last Monday, after
an operation for cystitis. Moubray was one
of the most curious personalities of the Aus-
tralian back-country. Coming of a well-known
Scotch family, he was for some years a British
artillery officer, and saw service in India.
Then, somehow, he drifted out to Aus-
and lived as a “ whaler ” —camping oh
the river bends of the back country, existing
on what he could fish and hunt—eked out
with a little money earned by literary work.
Moubray was a man who had been well educated,
and took no pains to conceal the fact. His great
store of back country experiences made his para-
graph contributions to The Bulletin of great
interest. For all his dogmatic opimonativeness,
“ Scotty” was a kindly soul, and there are many
who will sorrow at his end.

It can be claimed fpr Dr. Ross that he enters
more thoroughly into the current spirit of
N.S.W. Parliament than any other member.
For gorgeous folly, what could surpass these ques-
tions tabled the other day ? : >
(1) Will the Minister for Instruction obtain from the
Government Astronomer a report giving the following
information : (a) whether the season is likely to be wet
or dry, favorable or-, unfavorable, for the-, approaching
harvest, judging by late and present meteorological per-
turbations ; (b) when viewed in relation to the unfavor-
able weather season and disastrous harvest that have
recently occurred in many agricultural districts in. the
Northern Hemisphere ?
(2) Can he furnish any physical or known or recognised
scientific meteorological data or principle upon which
weatherioreCasts are framed or foretold, and upon what
recognised scientific basis or principle each conclusion
and forecast of the approaching weather is arrived at ?
(3) Will he obtain information as to how climatic
changes and erratic perturbations of the seasons are
brought about, for the information of the general public,
and by way of demonstrating the advancement made in
the realms of alleged scientific weather forecasts,
ibeteorology, and modern scientific research in the
twentieth'.pentury ?
Dit. Ross tffill yet, in the artless pursuit of know-
ledge. table some such questions as these:—
How many tail feathers has Elijah Dowie in his left
wing? And why? What principles of pterylography
govern the matter? Will the Premier see that the
practice is stopped, and also find out why matches don’t
grow on icebergs ?
In view of the extreme improbability of malarial fever
ever afflicting New South Wales will he give instructions
for the inoculation of all mosquitoes against malaria ?
And, after all, the House may as well gambol with
Db. Ross as transact any of the other matters it
oalls business.

A newspaper can hardly put a policeman
to a worse use than that of making him a subject
for puff paragraphs. Melbourne Herald lately
perpetrated this shockingly funny advertisement
of one Detective-Sergeant JD. G. O’Donnell:
He has succeeded in writing in a clear and legible
hand, on a piece of paper half the size of an ordinary
postcard, no less than 1050 words. He has also written
on a piece of paper the size of a threepenny piece the
Lord’s Prayer, with the words “Gloria in Excelsis Deo”
at the foot and “ Lord’s Prayer ”at the head The ser-
geant is very clever :at imitating signatures, and could
easily act the. part of a “Jim the Penman” cleverly.
Fortunately his talents are listed on the side of law and
order.
The people are not at all interested in these cali-
graphic feats. Even if O’Donnell is capableof stand-
ing on one leg for a longer time than any other de-
tective, the accomplishment adds nothing to his
official Value. What is needed just now is a de-
tective who can capture ten dangerous criminals
in half an hour and put the case against them into
a nutshell.

postal note or P. 0.0. to Bulletin office.

The feelings of a N.S.W. South Coast
tow*' received a rude shock when people
read in a local rag under the heading “Cable-
grams :—“The Duke of York states that
the sceptre is the happy slave of the glass jug.”
People even began to think of Edward VII., with
his crown on, fetching the beer home to the Tower
of London for dinner. But it proved to be “The
Duke of York Stakes—Sceptre first, Happy Slave
second, and Glass Jug third. Thus Edward VII.
left the court without a stain on his character.
Boyd, M.L.A., of Victoria, just returned
from a visit to S.A., says he found two of Vio-
tom s lost railway carriages in use on the lines
there. The vanished rolling-stock of Victoria has
been a screaming joke for some years; last stock-
taking showed that dozens of carriages and trucks
and various engines had dropped through a crack
somewhere and disappeared. So now Shiels will,
presumably, stand on the border line and wail
bitterly to Jenkins, “ Gimme back me trains ! ”
“Barney ” tells a possible truth :
A man, breathing heavily of beer, and chewing a ham
sandwich, flopped on the seat next to me on a suburban
train, the other night. He hadn’t got through with the
sandwich before he dropped into a heavy sleep, a piece of
crust hanging, pipe fashion, from his mouth. The train
arrived at the next station with a bit of a jerk, which
woke him up. He blinked owlishly at the lamp in the
carriage, carefully felt in all his pockets for matches, and
tned.three times to light the crust. Then he discovered
Wa ! f c rust:, and the way he sneaked it into his
pocket and looked around the carriage to see if he’d been

play “The Teeth up the Chimney."

“ D.”: Re our national propensity for prop-
ping ourselves up with anything that comes handy.
Went after a job once. There were about 68
applicants, and, pending the arrival of the boss,
we propped ourselves up with a newly white-
washed wall. When the boss was examining our
credentials he asked each one to turn round while
he hunted for traces of whitewash on him. The
man who got the job didn’t lean against the wall.
He had evidently been leaning against a bag of
bonedust before he arrived, and he smelt so badly
that the fellows who were leaning against the
wall had made him go away and lean against a
lamp-post all by himself. That was what got him
the billet. The boss was evidently blind in his
nose.
One of the reforms for which old Hori
Grey fought for years seems likely to be adopted
very soon in Maoriland. The great democrat
never could understand why one-man-one-vote
wasn’t as good and proper a thing for country
local governing bodies as for Parliament and for
city municipalities. Over 20 years ago he tried to
get a change made; but there were then only a
few with him. In 1886 he called for division after
division while the Consolidating Counties Bill was
before the House, but the majority against him
was overwhelming. In 1899 the numbers were
about equal; and now, in the Government’s
Local Government Franchise Bill, the principle
has been adopted, and, if it passes, as it seems
likely to do, one-man-one-vote will be practically
the law in all M.L. public institutions where the
still broader one-adult-one-vote does not prevail.
They do things thoroughly in the Chinese
ports. Shanghai Times, of July 18, describes the
oapture of a band of thieves, whose offence was
“hat-snatching.” The police seem to have been
unable to detect these desperate ruffians for some
time, so when they were caught it was dealt out
to three of them thus: 500 blows each, followed
by a month in the cangue (kind of pillory, fitting
on the neck, and portable—the wearer carries his
pillory about with him); then imprisonment vary-
ing from six months to a year, and banishment to
end up. There is no encouragement to be a mem-
ber of the Push in China.
“F.C.” on the parson’s son :
Met a friend of mine, a country baker, lately, in the
last stages of explosion. Hadn’t seen him for three
months, and then he was lamenting the loss of his “ boy ”
who used to do his most profitable round. The youth
had departed without the usual formalities, incidentally
taking the monthly payments with him. I enquired as
to the missing one. “Never heard any more of him.”
“Go round yourself now, I suppose, or do you trust the
new man?” “Don’t ask me! Don’t mention it 1 Look
here, you know I can’t do three rounds and bake
myself—l must have someone. Well, I got old
’s son to take that young ruffian’s place. It
was a bit of a come - down for him a parson’s
son—but I didn’t reckon to have to watch him. He
ante’d up all right for two pays, but last month, blow me
(five minutes here for insulting language)—blow me, if he
didn’t clear out with the takings too, and he took the
.name boards off the cart, and sold the whole turn-out,
horse and all, for £25. I got that back, but it cost me a
tenner ; as for him (ten minutes here for
further and stronger language). Say, do you happen to
know a hard-faced reprobate, of disreputable parentage,
who will come back with, say, half the cash and the
turn-out ? ”


A fine cave has been discovered on the
Margaret River (W.A.), about a mile from the
Lake cave. A skeleton of a man thinly encrusted
with lime and in a good state of preservation was
found in it, as well as a quantity of casual bones.
It is thought that the skeleton is what is left of
one of the crew of a Dutch vessel lost on the coast
in the dark ages. Captain Fremantle was search-
ing for survivors of this vessel when he discovered
the mouth of the Swan River, where Fremantle
now stands. A good many lost Dutchmen left
their bones about the western and northern coast.
A Ceylon tea-planter named Jordan, who
is now in Australia trying to “encourage more
friendly relations ” between his small Bull-owned
island and this continent, lets some light into the
preferential-trade project. In 1900 alone Ceylon
received 208,000 Tamils from India. Says the tea-
planter :
As laborers they are splendid. They turn out at six in
the morning, men, women, and children, and work till
four. . . . The wages are about 6d. a day, and the women
and children get less. Out of this they pay for food and
wardrobe which, however, is usually something less than
a handkerchief. They live very cheaply. Sixpence a day
is not much, but on' the mainland they probably would
not get more than from Id. to lid.
Our fellow-subjects, to whom are we cordially
invited to open our doors wide 1

Captain Arthur Paul, long known in Vic-
toria as one of Lansell’s mine-managers and
advisers, died at Melbourne lately. Paul was in
charge of the 220 mine at Bendigo when the
deep-sinker took it over, and so got into the
latter’s service. Probably the biggest mistake he
ever made was when he and the millionaire went
over the Broken Hill mines together in their earliest
days, and decided that the prospects were not
good enough to warrant the poor little prices then
current. Lansell was full of the idea that there
ought to be other lodes just as good in the district;
he didn’t know one field in Victoria which had
one good line of reef and no more. They, there-
fore, took up “ promising country ” around Mount
Robe, and dropped what money they put into it.
Years after Paul managed the North Broken Hill
mine, and at the height of the lead boom earned
profits from it; but when that boom collapsed he
went out of the mine-managing business.

PEOPLE cannot understand why Diamonds do not come down in price.
Unless some new Diamond fields are discovered they will never come
down, and I think that proportionately they will go as much higher
in the next ten years as they have in the last ten years. The reason
is this: Ten years ago it took less than one-third of the effort to produce
Diamonds as it does to-day. In other words, the strata of blue ground in the
Kimberley Fields that were then worked were so prolific that Diamonds
were found practically on the top of the earth. After the very prolific strata
were worked out and it was necessary to go down a little deeper into the
earth Diamonds were not quite so plentiful, and to-day it takes at least three
times the effort to produce the same amount of stones it did ten years ago.
Pearls are also going up in value at a rapid rate.

INVESTMENT.

only with the view of selling our Diamond goods, but actual facts which you
