The Lonely Tomb.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The blue Spring sky was overhead,
The south wind whispered to the trees.
I sought a story for a song ;
My heart was full of melodies.
And so I wandered slowly on,
Till, on a hill-top bleak, I found
An aged man, of aspect stern,
Who sat upon a lonely mound.
“My oath,” he gruffly said, “ you’re right! ”
You ain’t the first that’s called him brave.
He lost his life to save ’is mate’s ;
An’ this is where we dug ’is grave.”
Then seized I on that ancient man,
And said : “ Upon yon simple stone
We’ll carve a golden epitaph ! ”
He said : “ You leave the thing alone !
“It ain’t your tomb t’ fuss around.
Read what he’s got—just R.I.P. ;
He don’t want more, I know, because
He told me ’fore he died, yer see.
“Says he : * I’ve been a quiet cove,
And never cared for fuss or show ;
Just put a simple tombstone up—
T’ let ’em see I’m there, y’ know.
“ ‘ I only want ter R.I.P.’,
He sighed, and gives an orful corf—
‘And, if yer don’t mind, Bill, old boy,
Just keep th’ goats and poets off.’ ”
I glared awhile at that old man
Ere down the hill I madly flung ;
Forgotten were those melodies -
I left that noble song unsung.
VIC.
KODAK.
The Camel’s Back.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We tried to turn over a new leaf, but found it too
heavy. It fell back, and did some moral damage
perhaps. The boss —who had the contract to
supply the Silverstream saw-mill with logs—
sacked us after three days’ trial. He would
have sacked us sooner, only he hadn’t the moral
courage. He was a little boss, too, with a sandy
complexion. Little men inclined to be ginger
generally have moral courage. He said he
wouldn’t want us any more. That didn’t hurt us
much—Jack and I were used to getting the sack,
and to people who didn’t want us. He said we
weren’t bushmen. We got over that—we weren’t
bushmen, and consequently we didn’t have the
bushman’s pride or ambition. Did you ever fell
trees in rough country, and cut them into logs
with a cross-cut saw, and “pack” them out of a
gully over stumps and rocks, when you had never
done any graft before ? That’s what we did. We
worked hard for the first two days, and got three
logs. Then we took things easy, and waited for
the sack, even as a Chinaman waits for death.
It was a sort of sinful relief to find ourselves on
the track again. We were professional wanderers.
We had no money—not even enough to pay our
fares in by the train, 18 miles. We had a dirty
Eiece of paper to the owner of the mill requesting
lm to pay me 7s. 6d. and Jack 12s. The want of
money didn’t worry us so much —we were used to
it. But we hadn’t any tobacco. That’s what
hurt us.
We had tramped 13 miles since dinner-time
without a drink —exoept water; there’s plenty of
that in Maoriland. I wanted two long beers, and
so did Jack. But we wanted a smoke worse. I
think we met more men on that road who didn’t
smoke, or had just cut up their last pipeful, or
who wanted to borrow a pipeful themselves, than
we had met on any track during the whole course
of our vagabond career.
We had still five miles to go. It is seven miles
from Petone to Wellington. There seemed to be
about a thousand points. Every now and then
Jack would say, “ The pub. must be round the
next bluff.” But it wasn’t, nor the next, nor the
next, nor the one after that. Jack’s language had
been getting gradually less fit for publication
from the time we left Petone. He oursed the
sawmill and the world, and the day he learned to
smoke, and he prophesied against each succeeding
bluff in language which wasn't even fit to be
written down on a piece of paper and handed up
to the magistrate. But now he was too tired to
curse. We gave up hoping and speculating at
last, and just tramped on doggedly. It was hours
after dark. We saw the lights of Wellington
across the curve of the bay all the time, but they
never seemed to get any nearer. They just
seemed to stay off, and keep as far away as the
stars. But we looked to see if we had our orders
to the mill-owner safe, and hoped on.
We were dirty and ragged, and hadn't had a
shave or a bath for a week. We were unspeak-
ably tired and thirsty. There were eight of us in
reality ; there was the man I was, and the ghost
of the man I might have been, and the spook of
the (youth I had been, and the spectre of the
wreck I yet might be. Ditto with Jack, I think,
for he walked on with his head bowed and thought
—just thought. You are seldom too tired to
think.
Round the foot of a big, dark bluff there swept
a merry party of ladies and gentlemen on horse-
back, cantering out from Wellington along the
hard, white, star-lit road. They were part of a
variety company, I believe. One of the girls
turned in her saddle as her horse half-shied, and
exclaimed:
“ Oh, two old swaggies—he-he-he !”
And the others said : “Ha-ha! Ho-ho ! ” and
“ He-he ! ”
It was the last straw. I fully expected Jack
to straighten himself up in his scornful wrath and
turn round and prophesy against them, but he
didn’t. His face bowed a little lower, just a little
lower, and he folded his arms tight and high up
on his chest and plodded on. He didn’t say any-
thing. I felt that the iron had entered into his
soul.
We slept out that night, and next morning the
owner of the sawmill refused to cash our orders.
We never got the money.
I gave the new leaf another trial and succeeded.
I’in reformed. I back horses on commission now.
That's respectable enough—for Australia !
HENRY LAWSON.
A Summer Night in Bourke.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The sky is bright and clear; its color only
matched by the little bluebell which peeps from
amongst the saltbush on the plain. The seemingly
boundless expanse is covered with a stunted
growth of mulga and lignum. Across it the heat
haze shivers. The atmosphere is still and stifling;
there is no breath of air. By-and-by a dull yellow
cloud gathers in the west. Slowly it creeps
towards us, then the choking dust-storm is upon
us like a whirlwind of devils. The sun sets—a
sickly yellow orb whose fierce glare is tempered by
this curtain of dust.
Just at sundown with a sounding thump I throw
my swag against the wall of the “Oxford,” and,
having relieved myself of the billy, I walk into
the bar and call for a drink. “ Have one yourself,
Billy?” I casually remark to the landlord, for
that is the way of Bourke. The air is full
of dust—it fills every cranny and settles upon the
furniture. In consequence the place is illumi-
nated. The smoking kerosene lamps flicker in the
midst of a yellow halo, and add their pestilence to
the general oppressiveness. The temperature at
six had been 110°.
We sit at the table 20 minutes after the bullock-
bell. All our coats are off and our sleeves rolled
up. It is too hot to eat, but we are very thirsty.
We pick over our food and curse the Chinaman.
Why the devil does he make the tucker so
infernally greasy ? One by one we walk out under
the verandah.
At the invitation of the publioan we play four-
handed “crib.” “Fifteen two, fifteen four ;
mine’s a pint; why the devil didn’t you chuck
out the ace”—this, together with the inevitable
post-mortem on every deal, is heard with variations
until midnight. We then lounge around gasping
for breath and yarning until the beds begin to
cool down a bit—about 2 a.m. At 3 I am out
again staggering along the passage to the water-
bag, for after many drinks my tongue is dry and
my mouth parched.
“Bring us back a drink,” ray room mate says in
a tired voice. The poor devil is too weary to get
out of bed. There is a big drought on, for another
fellow has the water-bag to his mouth, and it is
nearly empty.
When I go back to the room the other fellow
stretches out his hand in the darkness and gropes
for the glass. We had pulled down the blinds,
for in a darkened room we had a better chance of
sleeping. He clutches and drinks. “ Thanks ! ”
Witli a sigh he limply turns again with his face to
the wall.
This performance is repeated half-hourly until
about 4.30. Then the sun rises, glaring over the
horizon. I am more listless now than when I
retired.
I gaze across the yard. Half-a-dozen miserable
wretches lie there, fully dressed, vainly endeavor-
ing to snatch a few minutes’ sleep. The
atmosphere of a room would stifle them. They
continually shift their blankets from place to
place, knowing full well that there is not one spot
less trying than,another. As if there could be a
cool corner in Hell ! 1
With the first peep of dawn the heat-waves
again begin to quiver, and all hands make for the
bar. A parched wretch has been knocking at the 1
publican’s door for the last half-hour. I had
noticed him going to bed with a bottle of beer in
each hand. “For God’s sake, Mr. Publichouse,”
he implores, “give us a drink.” The publican, in
stentorian tones, has meanwhile been endeavoring
to curse him off the premises. “If you don’t
come out soon we’ll go down to Paddy’s,” threatens
the thirsty one. This fetches him, and the owner
of the stentorian voice emerges in bare feet,
growling horribly. He carries the key of the bar
in one hand, and holds up his trousers with the
other.
The boarders had by this time lined up in their
pyjamas—bank clerks and sundries, with white
faces, each with a tremendous thirst and sleepless
eyes. The workers drink pints, and the johnnies
whisky and soda. We repeat the dose several
times before breakfast.
They wander aimlessly into the dining-room
somewhere about nine. Most of them enter the
room with a wild glare and quickly discover the
olive bottle. They grab it, and with unsteady
hands dive into it with a fork. The man who had
retired to rest accompanied by the two bottles of
beer now lurches in, with a fishy look in his eye.
Without any undue or undignified hurry he seizes
his porridge spoon, and with shaky fingers pours
out a dose of Worcestershire sauce. With the air
of a man who makes a virtue of necessity, he
drinks it and takes his place at the table. Con-
versation fails, for all hands feel pretty seedy.
I think of ice-cold water dripping into crystal
pools ; of fern-clad gullies; green fields and snow-
capped mountains. But when I look up, I see
through the shimmering air naught but the
dazzling roofs of the houses and the dusty glaring
roads.
W.J.A.
“ Ne’er-do-Weel ” about a large and
generally disliked fish :
“Mailer” (8., 17/9/’O3) says hungry sharks draw no
rigid “ color line.” Well, in the matter of broken bottles
or iron they may not, hut in regard to flesh they do
I have seen blacks and browns diving for coins in shark-
infested waters all over the world, and I never saw either
humans or sharks put themselves out the least little bit
in the world. On a voyage to the West Indies, Colon, and
the S. American ports, some years ago, we lay for a
couple of days at Port-au-Prince, Hayti. The whole time
we were there a huge shark lay motionless under the
stern of the vessel, and treated all our attempts to catch
him with contempt. The harbor was literally swarming
with sharks, but the black boys were diving for coins as
though such a fish had never been heard of in Haytian
waters. The crack diver was a fine, well-set-up buck
about 30 years of age, with a dazzling grin. He'
offered to dive from his boat, which was on the port side,
under the ship, for a florin if it was dropped into the sea,
from (he starboard side. The coin was dropped and a
signal given to him at the same time and he dived under
the ship and secured the money. Then, to our horror,
he proceeded to swim leisurely back to his boat round the
stern of the vessel. We shouted to him but he only
grinned a more dazzling grin than usual and continued
on his way. When a few yards from where the shark lay
he dived right under it, came to the surface again,
grinned another dazzle and swam to his boat. Though
the nigger appeared to go close under the shark the latter
never betrayed the slightest knowledge of his proximity 1 ,
did not even swish its tail—cut the colored gent dead, in
fact. Kingston Harbor (Jamaica) tco, is alive with
sharks, especially when the turtle ships come in, but the
. blacks never seem to stop bathing on account of them.
But I saw a yellow fellow slip into The Race round Port
( Royal Point. He was whirled 100yds. away in a flash and
I the sharks had him before we could get to him. He was
fa very white “ colored pusson,” and the white man’s fate
' befeli him.

The Last Drink.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Earth was like a wizened pear,
Her breasts were cold and dry ;
The dying sun, with bloodshot glare,
Rose trembling in the sky.
The sky was hung with scarlet clouds ;
It was the last dread dawn—
The Resurrected, in their shrouds,
Walked on and on and on.
It was a sight to shudder at—
The day was Judgment Day—
The white road to Jehoshaphat
Stretched leagues and leagues away.
Silent as tombs were church and mart,
The pale sea moaned afar—
But Doolan of the Dauntless Heart
Stood fast behind his bar.
He smiled a smile of gruesome mirth,
That red-haired Irishman ;
He was upon the blighted Earth
The Last Lone Publican.
No traveller with him could stay
To dine at ease, or sup ;
His pub. in grim facetious way
He called “ The Stirrup Cup.'*
But I knew Doolan and we dined—
We would in Hades thrive ;
Of all the myriads of mankind
We were the last alive.
And half with glee, and half with awe,
I sat with pipe and glass
In Doolan’s “Stirrup Cup” and saw
The weird procession pass.
A Publisher I knew went by
With whom I used to war,
And passed with terror in his eye
A pious Editor.
“ Come in,” cried Doolan, “ come and wet;
Come in and drink,” cried he.
“ It is the last drink you will get
For all Eternity.”
But dolefully they passed in packs,
With pants, and groans, and sighs,
And obelisks upon their backs
And tons of tombstone lies.
Then Doolan put his shutters up,
His sign took from the door.
And so we left “ The Stirrup Cup,”
And never saw it more.
N.S.W.
V.J.D.
The King and the Pope.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We came down to Leghorn at dusk, having seen
the King of England acknowledge the welcome of
the English colony in the Maremma, and some
American friends at Leghorn told us of the sudden
and unnoticed visit of the Queen of Portugal, and
of her haste to escape from such a frigid climate.
“She wasn’t a queen at all,” piped the smallest
American —eight years old. “ She might have
worn her week day crown.”
“Quite a dowdy,” said the lady opposite me.
“Nice, pleasant, stout face enough; but fearful
clothes, worn anyhow, and a gem hat.”
So much for personal appearances—it was the
other appearances that drove the lady away.
Queen Amelia aocompanied King Edward from
Portugal to Naples, and there had all the honors
due to a visiting sovereign, and it was thought
that she would accompany the King of England
to Rome. But the royal house of Portugal is
devoutly Catholic, and the visit of Queen Amelia
to Rome would, of course, necessitate the recogni-
tion of the King of Italy, whom the Pope, still
trying to keep up the farce of temporal power,
insists oh styling the “King of Sardinia.” And
such a stultification of the Pope might mean an
extension of the excommunication said to be kept
in force against the reigning house of Italy, to
Queen Amelia and the Portuguese King. So at the
last; moment Queen Amelia parted from the King
of England and sailed to Leghorn in her yacht.
But the telegraph was quicker.
There were three vessels of the Italian navy-
two torpedo boats and a destroyer—and as soon
as it was known that the Queen of Portugal had
been so afraid of the Pope as not to venture the
appearance of slighting him, those two torpedo
boats and the destroyer were rushed out of harbor
and sent to cruise purposelessly in the Mediter-
ranean. The port officials and the Mayor and
other shore authorities were ordered out of town
also j and when Queen Amelia arrived there was
no welcome given and no notice taken, although
the Queen of Portugal is related to the King of
Italy through the marriage of her sister to the
Duke of Aosta. Queen Amelia’s sister met her
at Leghorn, and the Portuguese yacht stayed
a few hours and then put to sea again. The
authorities are doubly sorry for the necessity of
the step, because while the Portuguese yacht
was in harbor a French warship—the first for a
century—entered the port, and there was nobody
to bid it welcome.
The Pope’s attempt to keep up the farcical idea
that he is Lord Of Rome is all the more ridiculous
when the attitude of the people to the church is
considered. The Bishop of Leghorn a year ago
spoke publicly of “ The Pope-King,” and until a
few months ago had to be guarded by carabinieri;
and I myself saw, in the Cathedral square of
Leghorn, in the Easter of 1902, a procession of
cardinals, dignitaries of the church, and priests,
guarded by soldiers with their carbines at the
ready. And in this polite land it is bad form to
bow .to a priest and shameful to have one for a
friend. In Calabria and Sicily the worst insult is
“ soldier of the Pope.”
To us in Australia who know the Australian
priest this may appear cruel and unjust ; but the
priests in Australia are mostly well-educated
gentlemen, and the priest in Italy is too often the
most ignorant and dirty son of the most ignorant
peasant.
Having seen how Queen Amelia’s insult to the
Italian people was requited, it came as a surprise
to the English colony in Tuscany and to most
Italians that the King of England had visited the
Pope. It has been explained by the newspapers
—and there are apparently very few Italian news-
papers unfriendly to Britain—that the visit to the
Pope was necessary owing to the fact that King
Edward is shortly to visit Ireland, and all the
journals speak appreciatively of the tact shown
by the King in making his visit as much the visit
of a private citizen as possible. The Pope as
tactfully seconded Edward Vll.’s endeavors to
steer a middle course—and the efforts of Cardinal
Rampolla to insist on the interview being as
between reigning sovereigns were doomed to
failure.
That the Italians should bitterly oppose any pos-
sibility of a return to theocratic rule is easily
understandable to any man with a fair knowledge
of Italian life. Italy is only 40 years old and has
made wonderful progress. Wealth has increased
—but to the few only ; to the many, the facilities
for education have increased, and consequently
the standard of education has been raised. But
the heavy taxation falls on the poor in a monstrous
incidence, and, although wealth has increased,
wages generally remain at starvation point. A
wealthy man keeping horses for amusement has
no horse-tax to pay; the charcoal burner or the
carrier pays £3 a year for a horse and £2 for a
donkey. Ten millions annually go to the army ;
some more to the navy, and a great deal to the
church. There must come a radical change in the
incidence of taxation and the modes of expendi-
ture ; and that change will have to be so radical
as to amount to a revolution.
Of all outgoings the people are most jealous of
that paid to church establishments. United Italy
from 1866 to 1901 seized the property of 64,445
religious bodies, whose yearly income amounted to
£2,344,404. Of these 45,337, with a yearly income
of .£1,964,000, were suppressed ; 6000 of them were
monasteries, and these the Government converted
into hospitals, barracks, museums and schools.
Other church property was seized and confiscated,
and an endowment for the income of priests was
made by the State. But this the people do not
now regard as a fair exchange. The church
property, they say, was originally paid for by the
people, and when it was seized, the people merely
took back their own ; and, on the other hand, they
regard taxation for the upkeep of a single priest
as a gross injustic.
The hope of a return to Papal temporal power
is the wildest of dreams. The Italian says of
temporal power : “ It denounced railways as irre-
ligious, and couldn’t even keep the streets of Rome
free from offal.” It was Bomba of Naples—the
beloved of the pontiff of 1866—who objected to rail-
ways because, he said, “tunnels were demoralis-
ing.” The Papal claim rests on the fact that 1400
years ago Phocas (afterwards Emperor of Con-
stantinople, but then a groom in the Imperial
service) killed the Emperor, the Empress, and
their five children, and made himself Emperor.
Pope Gregory the Great congratulated him, and,
in return, Phocas decreed that the Pope should
be “Universal Pope and Universal King.” Many
present - day dignitaries hold their dignities
on similarly flimsy foundations, and for 1400
years the Popes were Universal Kings—except
when they were under the thumbs of either poten-
tates at Constantinople, or feudal lords, or Western
kings, which was often. It is further alleged that
forfivejiundred years the Pope has been in the
hands of the Jesuits, which is probably untrue.
The more tolerant Italians say of the Pope,
“Poor creature —he has to do as he is bid.” But
that does not make his rule the more light or the
mor6 right—although it is the position of almost
every ruler on earth to-day. The widest stretch
of imagination cannot make King Edward a real
Emperor.
The laws are stringent against any attempt to
reinstate temporal power, providing heavy fines
and gaol punishments against priests “who
abuse their office to the damage of the nation and
its free institutions; who censure the institutions
and laws of the State, or who damage legitimate
private interests, or who disturb the peace of
families.”
And the surest proof that Papal power is gone
for ever from Italy is the monument of United
Italy in the Street of the Twentieth September
at Rome, by the old gateway of the Porta Pia—the
monument to “Victory and Rome Redeemed.”
The inscription in the granite is a living fact to
the Italian :
When, to universal right— Roman-like, asserted—the
Fates added the free conscience of Humanity, by this
breach Italy re-entered Rome.
No Christian resignation or religion in that. It
is the sternest citizen talk. Roman-like—Uni-
versal Right—The Fates —The Free Conscience
of Humanity.
Italy is emphatically not religious. Said Crispi
—“ To be a sincere Catholic and a friend of Italy
is a contradiction.” And Premier Zanardelli
recently said, in addressing his constituents at
Brescia, “ The Church appears better than it once
was; but has this improvement come about
because the Church is growing really better ?
Nothing of the kind. It is because the strong
arm of the law checks it. ”
Contrast this continuous childish attempt to re-
cover that power which was an anachronism
whenever it was not inoperative, and which has
gone for ever, with the magnificent treatment the
newly-emancipated nation gave the Church forty
years ago. When Young Revolution—agnostic
of everything but of its courage and its pike-
beat in the wall by the Porta Pia, and had the
Churoh at its mercy, it could have slain and
burned and wiped the Papacy from the earth.
Instead, it made princely concessions to the fallen,
and acted more magnanimously than any church-
man has ever done. And when King Humbert
lay murdered, the magnanimity was requited by
the Pope stating that he only tolerated the
memorial services to the dead. It is a pity that
the heirs rof Victor Emanuel 1., who braved ex-
communication to secure a United Italy, did not
also brave the church by a burial without service.
Thinking all these things, I said, with some
disgust: “ Why, the holding of political power by
any church in an age that produces a Marconi is
an insult to intelligence.”
“The English are a great people,” said my
American friend, with apparent irrelevance.
“ They’re all right,” I replied.
_ “ Well, they allow a House of Lords, and bishops
sit there and make laws. How’s that for temporal
power?”
I said that it was not quite the same thing.
“Not quite, but it’s only a question of degree.
And the people that allow churchmen to talk live
politics or make laws for live men are soft-headed.
A churchman never touches life really. He thinks
he knows something about death—but, on any
other subject but Heaven and Hell, his opinion is
valueless.”
So then we went to the sea wall and watched the
torpedo-boats come back to port. For Queen
Amelia and the Portuguese yacht had gone away.
LEGHORN, ITALY.

A BALLADE OF CAPTAIN STARLIGHT.
(Inscribed to Alfred Dampier.)
[For The Bulletin. ]
The plume, the helm, the flash of armour bright,
The call to arms, the clarion note of war,
The battle-steed full harnessed for the fight,
The warning beacon blazing from afar,
The warrior’s trust in his exultant star,
The beating heart that holds no thought of fear,
The joy of strife which nothing base can mar—
These were the glories of the days arrear.
The joust, the tourney, and the play of sword.
The flaunting pennon and the sheen of mail.
The knightly splendor of the banquet board,
The minstrel's harp, the bardic chanted tale,
The stoup of wine, the foaming beaded ale,
The hooded falcon and the flying deer,
The chase through covert, over hill and dale—
These were the glories of the days arrear.
We hare grown sordid, and our days are spent
In huokst’ring trifles in a grimy mart;
Farewell the moated keep, the warrior’s tent.
The glad great courage of the martial heart !
The world grows smaller, and the gods depart
And leave the earth a desert, dull and drear.
Farewell! Farewell 1 Our tears unbidden start
And fall in sorrow for the days arrear.
Some few among us make a desperate stand,
And fight as Titans fought in mythic times ;
Some take the highway in a new-found land ;
Some hurl at Circumstance their futile rhymes,
And curse a world of dollars and of dimes,
And rail at Fortune with divine despair—
The while they sing of golden far-off climes
That gleamed with glories in the days arrear.
Hadst thou but lived in those resplendent days,
When knightly creeds were short as knightly brand,
When nought was dearer than thy ladye’s praise,
And nought was fairer than her lily hand ; '
And nought but battle grim was half so grand ;
And nought so heartening as knightly cheer,
Thou mightst have reigned the ruler of the land—
A lord puissant of the days arrear.
L’Envov.
Captain, at least thou hadst a record rare
As one who faced his fate and felt no fear,
As one who knighthood’s mail didst ever wear
In the large manner of the days arrear.
F. J. Broomfield.
A female observer of human points is responsible for the
statement that Brightie Smith possesses the most elegant
feet to be found at the end of any Australian actress.
She certainly has a general appearance of being very
nicely finished-off.
“ P.X.": Apropot recent Bulletin remarks concerning
the staging of Popes. Froude translated and published a
16fch century French satire on Julius 11., which was
staged in Paris and made a sensation. One scene was at
the gate of Heaven. Julius had the key, but—“ What
the devil is this? The gate’s not opened ! Something is
wrong with the lock.” But Peter, who also appeared,
wouldn’t help ; and a warm argument followed, during
which the doorkeeper became very insulting, and the
curtain fell with the Pope still on the outside.
“ Pilsener," from Wellington (M.L.): The Maoriland
Brass Band tour is as dead a matter as pond water, but
since you have published one man’s screed on the sub-
ject you might as well let mine desolate the public also.
The chief reason why the band was a financial and social
failure was its lack of harmony and musical cohesiveness.
The bandsmen quarrelled on shipboard and broke up on
shore. Two of the big men carted their wives round with
them, at the expense of the common fund, and the bands-
men who had not thought to do likewise made things
sultry. All musicians are prone to anger, and some more
so than others. The cataclysm came in a stormy meeting
of the band and its several heads. At this meeting one
head was hurried from the room firmly gripped by the
slack of his trousseau, what time the second head argued
volubly with the third one as to the moneys expended.
Before this time half the original members had drifted
off, and the sorrowful remnants soon afterwards came
back to the land of the moa’s bones in three consign-
ments. They have since been interviewing pressmen in
successful endeavor to get their dirty garments made
blacker with printer’s ink.
“ Hiphen ” : “ Monsieur Beaucaire,” the American
sling-off at English old-world snobbery, gives food for
reflection. The hero—a Frenchman of the most virulent
type—wipes the stage with about six Englishmen ; and
the intelligent, discerning, and supposedly British-loving
audience applauds to the echo. Does this show that the
Australian cares not the face value of a politician’s oath
for the ill-treatment of the English by rabid foreigners ?
(If so, perish hopes of preferential trade.) Or does it
show the broad-mindedness of the British Lion’s whelp in
taking such tail-twists good-humoredly ? It may be only
a sign of Anglo-Saxon canniness, in always being “ in ”
with a winner ; or, again, the fact that the Frenchman is
royal may have something to do with it. Yet fancy such
a play being produced elsewhere ! If an Englishman on
the French stage, or a Jap. in Russia, were represented
as “ biffing” severely the men of those respective nations,
and winning the beauteous girl for his own, what would
the audience do ? Why, simply bite large bits off the
theatre and manager, cold-meat the leading man and
author (if handy), sneak the cash-box and leading lady,
and behave like a Melbourne football crowd. It is greatly
to this cold-blooded indifference that the English race
has got on in the world. Englishmen hav e never been
annoyed or bad-tempered when called liars or horse-
thieves by bilious foreigners. Not a bit ! They went on
carving another slice of territory off the globe and erect-
ing a few more churches and breweries. Many a bit of
territory has Britain got while foreigners were dancing
excitedly like cats on hot plates, and explaining how
wrong it was of that country to take the previous bit.
“B.”: Old John MTntyre played the Bailie for the
last night of the amateur “ Rob Roy,” in Melbourne.
He didn’t play it because he could play it, so presumably
he played it as a verra guid electioneering advertisement.
Old John recalls a performance that happened 40 years
lang syne at Bendigo (photos, show the juvenile
lead in duckskin pants and John in side-whiskers), and
exclaims : “Ma conscience 1 That was a performance.”
He always held the centre of the stage, with fierce lime-
juice beating on him, and his powerful Scotch voice has
been heard saying “ Ma conscience” many a time when a
political crisis was looming, and it was advisable to fix
public attention.
